Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Condominium owners<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">They own separate houses built facing one courtyard; they want to build a wall so that each house opens to its own courtyard.</i> who want to make a separation in the courtyard build the wall in the middle. At a place where commonly one builds with unhewn stones, or hewn stones, or half-bricks<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is the definition of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 3a" href="/Bava_Batra.3a">Babli (3a</a>). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Habakkuk 2:11" href="/Habakkuk.2.11"><i>Hab.</i> 2:11</a> the word means “wood splinter”.</i>, or bricks, everything follows local custom<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If one of the parties wants to build following local custom, he can force the other party to agree with him.</i>. For unhewn stones, each partner gives three handbreadths. For hewn stones, each gives two and one half handbreadths. For half-bricks, each gives two handbreadths. For bricks, each gives one and a half handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A wall made of half-bricks is wider than one made of whole bricks since the mortar between the pieces takes up an additional half handbreadth.</i>. Therefore, if the wall collapsed<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If a wall collapsed and it is no longer known who built it, one has to assume that it was built following these rules.</i> the place and the stones belong to both of them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Condominium owners who want to make a separation,” etc. As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.1.5.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i>: “One does not subdivide a courtyard unless each party has four cubits.” They said, there if the two parties do not agree<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This is spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.1.5.1">Mishnah 7</a>: one party may force the dissolution of a condominium if each party will be left with a legal minimum of space.</i>. But here if both parties agree<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">It says explicitly that “they wish to make a separation.” In that case they are not bound by minimal conditions (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 2b" href="/Bava_Batra.2b">Babli 2b</a>).</i>. And you may even say, here if the two parties do not agree<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the next sentence, “they wish” means “either of them may wish”.</i>. If this one wishes, one forces the other; if the other one wishes, one forces this one. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one forces for courtyards; one does not force for roofs<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that the parties wish to build the wall in order to insure privacy, so that one party cannot see what the other is doing in his courtyard. The courtyards were always in the back of the houses, not visible from the street. One assumes that both buildings are one-storey houses and that the wall will be built high enough that from one flat roof the neighbor’s courtyard cannot be seen. There remains the question what is meant by “splitting because of the roof.”</i>. Rebbi Nasa wanted to say, in case of a courtyard which is higher than the roof<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Two houses built on a hillside; the courtyard of the upper one is higher than the roof of the lower. According to R. Nasa, the owner of the lower house cannot go to court to force the owner of the upper house to build a wall which will guarantee the privacy of the lower house.</i>. But in case of a roof which is higher than the courtyard, one forces<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">As noted before, the wall must be built high enough that from one flat roof the neighbor’s courtyard cannot be seen.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan wanted to say, in case of a courtyard which is higher than the roof one forces<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the inhabitants cannot but see what is going on in the lower house. This violates the privacy rights of the inhabitants of the latter; the former can be forced to construct a fence which will bar them from looking down.</i>. But in case of a roof which is higher than the courtyard, one does not force<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the use of the roof is infrequent, the damage done to the other party by an occasional glance is minor and does not warrant the construction of a fence. The Babli disagrees in the name of Samuel, 2b.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Similarly for a vegetable garden<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">An irrigated area of intensive agriculture.</i>; a place which customarily is fenced in, he must fence in<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If one of the parties wishes to construct a fence the other party is forced to contribute land and money to its construction.</i>. But in an agricultural area<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">בקעה is a region of extensive agriculture.</i>, a place which customarily is not fenced in, one does not obligate him. But if he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">One of the farmers.</i> wishes, he builds inside his land and makes a façade<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">A smooth face; the wall or fence must be covered with smooth stone plates. This characterizes the fence or wall as private property of a single owner.</i> to the outside; therefore if the wall collapses both land and stones are his.<br>But if they<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Two property owners in the agricultural area.</i> did it with mutual agreement they build the wall in the middle and make façades on both sides; therefore if the wall collapses<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If a wall collapsed and it is no longer known who built it, one has to assume that it was built following these rules.</i> both land and stones belong to both of them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Similarly for a vegetable garden,” etc. It was stated: For a vegetable garden, whether it be a place where it is customary to build a fence, or a place where it is not customary to build a fence, one forces<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">As pointed out in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 4a" href="/Bava_Batra.4a">Babli, 4a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> is ambiguous in the case of a place where it is not customary to fence in vegetable gardens. From the first sentence, one would infer that one would not force one farmer to participate in the costs of a fence which his neighbor wants to put up, but from the second sentence it seems that only in agricultural areas he does not have to participate. The Babli restricts the power of the court to order participation in the building of a fence to places which do not have a declared policy against erecting fences. It is possible to read this into the Yerushalmi text.</i>. But in an agricultural area, at a place where it is customary to build a fence one forces; where it is not customary to build a fence one does not force.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody’s [property] surrounded another’s on three sides. If he fenced in the first, and the second, and the third sides, one does not hold [the other] liable. Rebbi Yose said, if the latter then fenced in the fourth side, one rolls everything over to him<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since he now uses the three fences put up by the other owner for his own purposes, the latter can ask for half the cost of erecting the other three fences.</i>.<br>If the wall of a courtyard collapsed, one obligates him<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">If one of the parties wants to rebuild, the other is forced to share the costs.</i> to rebuild up to four cubits. There is a presumption that he gave, unless he brings proof that he did not give<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If later the party which rebuilt the wall claims that it was not paid by the other party, he is the claimant and the burden of proof is on him.</i>. Higher than four cubits one does not obligate him, but if he built an adjacent wall, even though he did not put on roofing, one rolls everything over to him<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If only one party is in charge of rebuilding, he cannot claim reimbursement for the costs of raising the wall higher than four cubits, even if the wall which had collapsed was higher. But if the party which is not in charge then builds a wall on his own property in the full hight of the separation wall, at a right angle to it, and now has three walls, the two newly built ones and his house, which he may use to create a new roofed space, he uses the full hight of the rebuilt wall and has to pay his share for the full hight.</i>. There is a presumption that he did not give, unless he brings proof that he gave<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">If he did not pay his full share, putting the roofing on the other person’s wall would be illegal. Therefore, the rules of torts apply rather than those of civil claims. Since it is common usage that the second party pays only for the costs of building up to four cubits, the presumption is that he did not pay for his share of the part of the wall which exceeds the customary height.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody’s [property] surrounded another’s,” etc. <b>Halakhah 5:</b> “If the wall of a courtyard collapsed,” etc. Rav Ḥuna said, only at the rate he is building now<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Commentary to Mishnah 4. The farmer who only built one fence has to pay to the one who built three at most half the cost of his fences if they were built like the fourth (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 4b" href="/Bava_Batra.4b">Babli 4b</a>). E reads בְּשַׁעַר “at the rate” instead of L’s בְּשָׁעָה “at the time”; the reading of E was translated.</i>. For if he built it arched<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Commentary to Mishnah 5. The wall was built in the way of Roman architecture, sturdy Roman arches with a thin filling below and a flat top. This saves material but costs more in labor.</i>, he had to build arched. But here if it was built arched and he built it with bricks, he can collect the cost of arches, and if it should collapse, he has to rebuild it<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The party which rebuilt the wall can require to be paid for the quality of the previous wall, but if it was executed with inferior material or workmanship he has to rebuild it at his own expense.</i>.
“Rebbi Yose said, if the latter then fenced in the fourth side, one rolls everything over to him.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">While this is a quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>, Rav Huna’s remark refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.1.3.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>” Rav Ḥuna said, one rolls over the length of the wall to him<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the new wall built by the other party who did not construct the original wall higher than four cubits starts in the middle of the wall of partition, he has to pay for the elevation of the wall of partition in its entire length. This also is Rav Huna’s opinion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 6a" href="/Bava_Batra.6a">Babli 6a</a>, opposed by the authoritative Rav Naḥman who holds that he only has to pay for that part of the wall which he uses for his own purposes.</i>.
So far lengthwise. What about its width?<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah refers to the case that the new wall be built at a right angle to the dividing wall; the builder then intends to put on logs which form the roof parallel to the dividing wall, between his house and his new wall. He uses the dividing wall only as back wall for his new room; the dividing wall does not carry any weight. Nevertheless, it is enough to make him responsible for half the cost of the entire dividing wall. The problem now is about a wall built parallel to the dividing wall, where the house will form the back wall of the new room to be constructed. If the owner of this piece of land pays half of the cost of the dividing wall, may he put his logs on it to form a roof over his new room?</i> Rebbi Nasa said, the wall of the courtyard is only made to save for him<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The purpose of the wall is to create privacy, rather than to carry any weight.</i>. They<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The members of R. Nasa’s school.</i> intended to say that if he wanted to make a roof, he cannot make a roof<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If the wall was not built for weight-carrying from the start, no party can put any weight on it. E has an additional argument:<br>וְחָזַר וְתַנָּה. סָמַךְ לוֹ כוֹתֶל אַחֵר אַף עַל פִּי שֶׁלֹּא נוֹתַן עָלָיו אֶת הַתִּקְרָה מְגַלְגְּלִין עָלָיו אֶת<br>הַכֹּל. הָא אִם רָצָה לִקְרוֹת אֵינוֹ מַקְרֶה.<br>He (R. Nasa) repeated and stated: “but if he built an adjacent wall, even though he did not put on roofing, one rolls everything over to him.” Therefore, even if he wanted to make a roof, he cannot make a roof.<br>The inference which his students wanted to draw from his statement already follows from the text of the Mishnah. The payment required is just for the wall as is, not for any additional use.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it by his beams<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">מָרֵישׁ is a weight-carrying beam. The person building a wall parallel to the dividing wall might put up vertical beams at the two ends of the dividing wall and use them together with his newly constructed wall to make a wooden frame on which to place the roof beams. Then he gets a room of which the dividing wall simply is a wall but carries no weight. The payment of half the cost of the dividing wall is enough to make this a legitimate construction.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One forces him to build a porter’s lodge and a door for the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Any owner of one of the houses opening to the courtyard can take the other owners to court and have them contribute their fair share to the building and maintenance of a secure entrance to the courtyard. According to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:17" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.17">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 11:17</a>, the condominium can force an absentee landlord to contribute to this but to nothing else.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, not all courtyards are appropriate for a porter’s lodge. One forces<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The town administration can force contributions from all property owners to projects concerning their common safety.</i> him to build a wall for the town with double doors and bolt. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, not all towns<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Villages usually are unwalled.</i> are appropriate for a wall. How long does he have to stay in a town to be like an inhabitant of the town<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">To be a local taxpayer.</i>? Twelve months; if he bought an apartment he immediately is an inhabitant of the town.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One forces him to build,” etc. Rebbi La said: So it is; the way of a provident person is to build himself a wall, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 10:15" href="/Proverbs.10.15"><i>Prov.</i> 10:15</a>.</i>: “The wealth of the rich is the fortification of his might, etc.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Similar, but materially different, texts are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:6:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.6.8"><i>Peah</i> 8:7 (Notes 105–106</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 4:9" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).4.9">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 4:9</a>.</i>: “If he stayed there for thirty days he is like the townspeople for the charity chest, for clothing six months, for assessments<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For imperial taxes imposed on the locality as a lump sum.</i> and penalties<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ζημία; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.18"><i>Peah</i> 1:1, Note 84</a>. These are extraordinary taxes imposed by the imperial government.</i> twelve months.” After all these, was twelve months said only for assessments and penalties? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said for Passover wheat, both to take and to give<sup class="footnote-marker">44*</sup><i class="footnote">12 months are required both to be subject to the local tax to supply the needs of the local poor for Passover (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:1:1-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.1.1-14">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i> 10:1</a>) or to receive welfare support in this matter.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not divide the courtyard unless there be four cubits for each one<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In a common courtyard, the four cubits in front of the door of each house along its entire width are for the private use of its owner. Therefore, the 4 cubits mentioned here are measured starting at a distance of 4 cubits from the house along the entire width of the house, as stated by R. Joḥanan in the Halakhah and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 11a" href="/Bava_Batra.11a">Babli, 11a</a>. According to the Babli, the four cubits are personal property only in front of the doors.</i>, nor the field unless there be nine <i>qab</i> for each one<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Qab</i> is short for <i>bet qab,</i> the area to be sown by one <i>qab,</i> 1/6 <i>se’ah,</i> of seed grain. The <i>bet se’ah</i> is defined as 2500 square cubits. Therefore, the <i>bet qab</i> is 416 2/3 square cubits. The rules will prevent the division of estates from resulting in plots too small to be worked on. For the majority, the minimal size of an inherited field is 3750 square cubits.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says, nine half <i>qabbim</i> for each one<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">1875 square cubits.</i>. Not the vegetable garden unless there be half a <i>qab</i> for each one<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">208 1/3 square cubits.</i>; Rebbi Aqiba says, the area of a quarter [<i>qab</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">104 1/6 square cubits.</i>. Not the dining hall, nor the storage room, nor the dovecote, nor the stole, nor the bathhouse, nor the olive press unless there is one for each of them. This is the principle: Anything which is divided and retains its name one divides, otherwise one does not divide. When is that? If the two of them do not agree. However, if both of them want it, they may divide even if it is less<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The rules are guidelines for the courts for the resolution of disputes; they do not restrict the ability of the parties to contract following their wishes.</i>. But Holy Scripture<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">A scroll of biblical texts.</i> they should not divide even if both of them want it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not divide the courtyard,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the four cubits which they mentioned are outside the four cubits of the doors. Bar Kappara stated thus: One does not subdivide a courtyard unless there be eight cubits for each party<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In a common courtyard, the four cubits in front of the door of each house along its entire width are for the private use of its owner. Therefore, the 4 cubits mentioned here are measured starting at a distance of 4 cubits from the house along the entire width of the house, as stated by R. Joḥanan in the Halakhah and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 11a" href="/Bava_Batra.11a">Babli, 11a</a>. According to the Babli, the four cubits are personal property only in front of the doors.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the four cubits which they mentioned are not acquired by him<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">They remain condominium property.</i> but they are for him to park his animal temporarily and unload his package<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The co-owners of the courtyard may freely pass through these four cubits but not temporarily use the space otherwise; the owner or renter of the house may use it to load and unload animals and carriages but not as permanent storage area.</i>. Rebbi Jonathan asked: did they only say for him to park his animal temporarily and unload his package temporarily<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Is it possible that these rights not be connected with property rights?</i>? It was found stated: also the circular enclosure of a cistern has four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:15" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.15">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 11:15</a>. The area around the common cistern has to be kept clear so that the dwellers in the courtyard have free access and are able to put their pails there. Both the cistern and the circular area surrounding it clearly are condominium property; there is no basis for R. Jonathan’s question.</i>. They said in the House of Rebbi Yannai: the chicken coop also has four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 11b" href="/Bava_Batra.11b">Babli, 11b</a>, R. Yannai disagrees.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">56*</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.1.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 5:1, Notes 16–20</a>. The courtyard is there for entering and leaving the houses. Any other use is possible only if it does not disturb other inhabitants of the courtyard.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Banaiah. Everything the co-owners can prevent one another from doing in the courtyard except laundering because of the honor of the daughters of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 57b" href="/Bava_Batra.57b">Babli 57b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Mattaniah said, this is at a place where women do the laundering, but not at a place where men do the laundering. And what was said, except laundering because of the honor of the daughters of Israel, only that in his own four cubits he may prevent it. And what was said, everything the co-owners can prevent one another from doing in the courtyard, in the entire courtyard except in the other person’s own four cubits where he cannot prevent him. But if the place was at an incline, even in the other person’s own four cubits he can prevent him, since he may tell him: you are pouring out in your domain but it flows down into mine.
<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel but materially different treatment of the same subject is in <i>‘Erubin</i> 7:11, 24d lines 12–16.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon. If anybody does not want to participate in an <i>‘eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">A major bone of contention between Sadducees and Pharisees in Second Temple times was the interpretation of <i>Ex.</i> 19:26: “Nobody shall leave his place on the Sabbath day.” It is agreed that this implies that one may carry things at will at “one’s place”, i. e., in one’s house, and that one may not carry in the public domain. As a matter of principle, it is agreed that a condominium space, such as a courtyard, does not qualify as “one’s place.” In pharisaic interpretation, this is only a rabbinic interpretation; it is not biblical. As a consequence, there is a rabbinic possibility to turn the courtyard into a common space by collecting contributions to symbolically prepare food for a common meal of all dwellers around the courtyard and depositing it in one of the houses. This is called <i>“ ‘eruv</i> (mixing) of courtyards”. The whole idea of <i>‘eruv</i> is rejected by Sadducees who consider reliance on it as a breach of the biblical Sabbath prohibition. It is similarly possible to turn a dead-end alley into a common courtyard of all dwellers there by affixing either a vertical lath or an horizontal beam at the entrance to the alley and then treating the alley and all courtyards which open into it as one large courtyard where then carrying is permitted. But if even one dweller in a courtyard or a dead end alley rejects the entire idea of <i>‘eruv,</i> no <i>‘eruv</i> can be made. The presence of Gentiles is irrelevant for purposes of making an <i>‘eruv.</i></i> one enters his house and makes the <i>‘eruv</i> against his will. But was it not stated: if he does not want to make an <i>‘eruv,</i> if he does not refuse one enters his house and makes the <i>‘eruv</i> against his will? Because he does not refuse. But not if he does refuse<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">One Sadducee living in a courtyard makes the <i>‘eruv</i> impossible for the entire dead end alley. No Sadducees are known from after the time of the war of Bar Kokhba except Samaritans.</i>. But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:18" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.18">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 11:18</a>; Babli <i>‘Eruvin</i> 80a.</i>: “The dwellers at a dead end alley may force one another to make a lath or a beam for the dead end alley.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">How can one force a rabbinic institution on Sadducees or Samaritans?</i>” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, not that he refused<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">He is neither Sadducee nor Samaritan.</i> but out of ill-will he does not want to participate in an <i>‘eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This is a particular case of the principle that “one forces people not to behave like the people of Sodom” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 12b" href="/Bava_Batra.12b">Babli 12b</a>), <i>viz.,</i> to refuse anything which does not hurt himself but will benefit others.</i>.
Rav Huna said, a courtyard is divided by the number of doors<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 11a" href="/Bava_Batra.11a">Babli 11a</a>. According to Rashi, Rav Huna speaks about distribution of an inheritance in case the father willed a house in his compound to each child but did not specify anything about the surrounding areas. These then are distributed in proportion to the doors of the houses each child inherited.<br>According to Maimonides (<i>Šekhenim</i> 2:1) condominium ownership in the courtyard is determined by the number of doors opening into it from each house.</i>. Rav Ḥisda said, the <i>baraita</i> refers to manure. It was stated as follows: “For the cooking stove one handbreadth, for manure according to doors, for the covered walkway according to people.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This text is thoroughly corrupt. The mention of the cooking stove may refer to the minimal distance of a cooking stove from a wall (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.2.1">Chapter 2, Note 22</a>); but probably it does not correspond to anything.<br>The remainder of the text is a corruption of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:12" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.12">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i>11:12</a>. The manure mentioned here is not animal excrement but the garbage thrown into the courtyard from the houses, which is broken by people stepping on it and in time transformed into material which can be used as fertilizer. From time to time this is swept together and then divided up among the different owners. Since one assumes that every door is used to throw garbage into the courtyard, it is reasonable to distribute the fertilizer proportional to the number of doors.<br>The “covered walkway”, ἐξέδρα, should be read as “quartering”, ξενία (אכסניא), soldiers to be given quarters by the population. They are to be given quarters proportional to the number of dwellers in each house.</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:21" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.21">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 11:21</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 108a" href="/Bava_Metzia.108a">Babli <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 108a</a>. Both Babylonian sources speak of “5 vegetable gardens.” If a water counduit has to be repaired, every user has to pay for the repairs up to the place where he syphons off the water.</i>“Five courtyards which were using a water conduit and it broke. All of them repair together with the uppermost. The uppermost repairs at its place; the others with the second one. The second one repairs at its place; the lowest one repairs with all of them and at its place.” <sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:20" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.20">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 11:20</a>. Since a multi-storey house whose ground floor crumbles must be torn down, it is clear that one must read with the Tosephta בִּיב “pipe, sewage pipe” instead of בַּיִת “house, ground floor apartment.” Every apartment which uses the sewer is required to contribute to its repair from the point of its connection to the sewage system.</i>“Five apartments<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">E דייאטות; Greek δίαιτα; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.6.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:6, Note 200</a>. One should read with E מִשְׁתַּמְּשׁוֹת <b>בְּבִיב</b> “they use the same <i>sewer</i>”.</i> are built above a house which broke. All of them repair with the lowest. The lowest repairs at its place; the others with the second one. The second one repairs at its place; the uppermost repairs with all of them and at its place. <sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This part shows clearly that the entire <i>baraita</i> refers to a crumbling sewer system, not a crumbling house.</i> If it was a storm sewer they<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The rate payors have to pay for its upkeep.</i> can prevent it being used for laundry. For laundry they cannot prevent it being used as storm sewer.”
“But Holy Scripture<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">A scroll of biblical texts.</i> they should not divide even if both of them want it.” Rebbi Hoshaia said, for example, Psalms and Chronicles. But Psalms and Psalms one may divide<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 13b" href="/Bava_Batra.13b">Babli, 13b</a>, Samuel explains that if the Bible is written in separate scrolls, the heirs may divide the scrolls; the collection does not have to remain intact. If Psalms are written in five different scrolls, they may be given to five different recipients.</i>. Rebbi Uqba said, even Psalms and Psalms one may not divide. Since one may not divide, each of them comes and reads in the other’s [book]<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In order to emphasize and remind everybody of his co-ownership. But if the heirs could split the scrolls, they would lock them away in some treasure chest and never look at them again (explanation of R. Eliahu Fulda).</i>.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person may not dig a cistern close to his neighbor’s cistern, nor a ditch, nor a cave, nor a water canal, nor a washer’s pond except if he distanced himself from his neighbor’s wall three handbreadths and lined with lime<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Any construction with water has to be at a distance of three handbreadths and must be waterproofed.</i>. One removes olive peat, and manure, and salt, and lime, and rocks<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">All except rocks are corrosive.</i> from his neighbor’s wall three handbreadths or lines with lime<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The wall must be insulated against the corrosive action of the dangerous materials.</i>. One distances seeds, and ploughs<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">One does not plough closer than three handbreadths from a wall.</i>, and urine three handbreadths from the wall. One distances a grindstone<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A grain mill worked by hand, consisting of a moving grindstone and a larger stationary stone base.</i> three from the base, which means four from the moving stone, and the oven<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The oven is a removable earthenware frustum of a cone sitting on a larger masonry base on which the fire burns.</i> three from the base<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κοιλία “belly”; a stone or brick base which is concave in the middle for the fire; the frustum of the cone of the oven is placed on the rim which is higher than the place of the fire.</i>, which means four from its rim.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person may not dig a cistern,” etc. So is the Mishnah: “[A person may not dig a cistern close to <i>the wall of</i> his neighbor’s cistern. Nor a ditch, nor a cave, nor a water canal, nor a washer’s pond except if he distanced himself from his neighbor’s wall three handbreadths] and lined with lime<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The text in brackets (in the Hebrew in a different type face) is from E, missing in L. According to L, one insists that in this sentence one reads וְסָד in contrast to the next, which reads אוֹ סָד. According to E, one specifies that the minimal distance is only required between the walls of the cisterns, not the surroundings (which inside a person’s domain are four cubits, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 1:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.1.5.2">Chapter 1 Note 55</a>). Then it is explained that וְסָד here and אוֹ סָד in the next sentence mean exactly the same, “or one lines.”</i>. If he lined with lime, any distance. If he did not line with lime, three handbreadths. From the following: “One removes olive peat, and manure, and salt, and lime, and rocks from his neighbor’s wall three handbreadths or lines with lime.” Here you say, because rocks generate heat<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since rocks are mentioned together with olive peat and manure.</i>. But there you say that they do not generate heat<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 4:1, olive peat, manure, salt, and lime are mentioned as generating heat and therefore forbidden as material in which food can be stored for use on the Sabbath. The omission of rocks implies that they do not generate heat.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, in no case do rocks generate heat<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 19a" href="/Bava_Batra.19a">Babli disagrees, 19a</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Bava Batra 19a:13:1" href="/Rashi_on_Bava_Batra.19a.13.1">Rashi, <i>s. v</i>. הנך משום</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Batra 19a:13:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Batra.19a.13.1">Tosaphot <i>s. v.</i> משום דמשתכי</a>.</i>. But because they loosen the earth and damage the ground around the wall<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">All rules of this Mishnah are derived from the principle that no activity on one person’s grounds should endanger another’s buildings.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 19b" href="/Bava_Batra.19b">Babli 19b</a>.</i>: “If it was solid rock, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">To urinate at a wall.</i>.” As we say, Rebbi Joḥanan left the synagogue and let water behind the door. But we do not know whether it was because it was solid rock or because he suffered pain and could no bear it<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, the statement is accepted as practice.</i>.
This means, about grindstones there<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>, but for our grindstones three from the pine cone<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στρόβιλος. The grindstone was shaped like a pine cone with a handle on top by which it was moved.</i> which is four from the enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The wooden enclosure around the immovable stone basis of the grindstone.</i>. “And the oven three from the base which are four from its rim.” Rebbi Yudan bar Pazi said, from the outer rim inwards; but the rabbis say, from the inner rim outwards<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The distance of four handbreadths from the oven for R. Yudan bar Pazy is measured from the outside of the wall of the oven, for the rabbis from the inside.</i>. If if was built like a dovecote<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If the oven is not shaped like a cone but like a cube.</i>, what [are the rules]? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta 1:3</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>. The cone-shaped oven, with a fire at the bottom and a hole on top, is made to create a draft and, therefore, is much hotter than the stove which is an earthenware cube with a circular hole on top for the pot, where the fire is lit on the clay bottom.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, unless there be under it insulating material three handbreadths and for a stove one handbreadth.” Is a stove not build like a dovecote, and you say both above and below a handbreadth? Also here, both above and below a handbreadth<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If the stove is used inside the house, it needs fireproofing material on the floor, the ceiling, and the nearby wall.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person should not put an oven in the house<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The ground floor which either has a dirt or a stone floor. Therefore, no fireproofing is needed below the oven. But the ceiling, which also is the floor of the second storey, is made of logs covered with plaster; it is flammable.</i> unless there be above it a space of four cubits; or if he puts it on the upper floor unless there be under it insulating material three handbreadths and for a stove one handbreadth<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If the stove is used inside the house, it needs fireproofing material on the floor, the ceiling, and the nearby wall.</i> But if he caused damage, he must pay what he damaged<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he observed all rules spelled out in the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Simeon said, the reason they indicated these measures is that he not be liable to pay if he caused any damage,.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person should not put,” etc. It was stated: Nor a smithy. Rav Qarna stated: If he was like a metal worker<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical metal worker; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 4:22" href="/Genesis.4.22"><i>Gen.</i>4:22</a>; he only spends a small part of his time as a smith; most of his time he manufactures metal vessels.</i>, or it was built originally as a smithy<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A separate building. It is possible to read: If it was built as a smithy from the start, i. e., if the smithy existed before the construction of the warehouse; this is the interpretation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 20b" href="/Bava_Batra.20b">Babli, 20b</a>.</i>, it is permitted. How is it in front of his dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Out in the open.</i>? Rebbi Aḥa said, certainly not in front of his dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This already was settled in Halakhah 1:7.</i>. Rebbi Jonathan said, a person causing damage has no claim of undisturbed posession<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 23a" href="/Bava_Batra.23a">Babli 23a</a>. Even if the other inhabitants of the courtyard do not object, a new owner can object if an activity is classified in law as damaging.</i>. Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Rosh (Chapter 2, §18) reads “R. Joshua ben Levi.” This reading has to be accepted.</i> said, the rabbis object to permanent smoke. As the following: A woman was burning sand<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Maybe to make glass. It is possible to read: She was burning the bird <i>Ḥol</i> (Phoenix, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 19:18" href="/Job.19.18"><i>Job</i> 19:18</a>). For חולין Rosh reads קלא or קולין “roasts”.</i> below [the apartment of] Rebbi Ilfay<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Rosh reads: R. Eleazar.</i>. He wanted to object to her. The case came before Rebbi Nasa who said, they said it only about continuous smoke.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person may not open a bakery or a dye shop<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Both create excessive heat which might damage the goods stored there. The owner of a preexisting storage facility can go to court and request an order prohibiting the operation of a bakery or dye shop.</i> under the storage room of another person, nor an animal barn<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The smell would ruin most of the goods stored there.</i>. In truth<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">A technical term indicating pre-rabbinic practice which somehow deviates from the principle just stated.</i> they permitted for wine<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Which is held to age better in the heat.</i>, but not an animal barn. About a store in the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Somebody bought a property in the courtyard to open a store where there was none before.</i> one<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Any of the co-owners of the courtyard.</i> may object and tell him, I cannot sleep because of the noise of those coming and the noise of those leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The customers.</i>. One who manufactures vessels has to leave and sells them in the market<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">But he can manufacture in a place which otherwise is a bedroom community as long as he does not create traffic of people who do not belong there.</i>; nobody may object and tell him, I cannot sleep because of the noise of the hammer, nor because of the noise of a grindstone, nor because of the noise of children<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody may object to a school in his courtyard.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person may not open,” etc. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa moved a preparer of fried food from one stall<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Latin stibadium “bench”, Arabic مسطبة.</i> to another stall. One preparer of fried food was dwelling below [the apartment of] Rebbi Eudaimon the brother of Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Aḥa passed by and did not object<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph really should be taken together with the preceding one. It seems that the store was there before R. Eudaimon moved in. The latter therefore needed a confirmation from the head of the local court that “a person causing damage has no squatter’s rights” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.2.2">Note 38</a>), which R. Aḥa refused. Since the fourth generation authority refused to enforce the pronouncement of the first generation R. Jonathan, its legal validity is in doubt.</i>. He said, the rabbis are passing by and do not object. Rebbi Aḥa got angry with him. Rebbi Eudaimon the brother of Rebbi Yose became seriously ill. Rebbi Yose came to visit him. He said, I shall go and ask him<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">He promised to pacify R. Aḥa.</i>. He went and said, if a court had mercy on somebody would the court prepare shrouds for him?
A person sold his entire house<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">A private dwelling with the entire courtyard.</i>; he reserved for himself one stone bench<sup class="footnote-marker">45a</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic مَسُطَبَة “bench, anvil”.</i> where he went and sat. The case came before Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose. They said, it is not in your power to go and sit there and observe who comes and goes in his house<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Your property right does not override the buyer’s right to privacy.</i>. A person sold half his house; he reserved a bake shop for himself. The case came before Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose. They said, you are coming to him; he is not coming to you, as it is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 20b" href="/Bava_Batra.20b">Babli 20b</a>. The buyer of an apartment above a bake shop cannot complain.</i>: “If the barn or the store preceded the storage room, he cannot object.”
It was stated: In truth, they permitted for wine even though it diminishes it because it improves it<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 20b" href="/Bava_Batra.20b">Babli 20b</a>.</i>. Rav Hoshaia when he heard this statement put his wine in the attic of the bath house. His wine became foul-smelling. He said, the Mishnah led me astray. But the Mishnah did not lead him astray; the smell of the bath house made his wine foulsmelling<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli endorses the opinion that the Mishnah be valid only for Palestinian, not Babylonian wines.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Different texts are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 21b" href="/Bava_Batra.21b">Babli 21b</a>.</i>: About his neighbor he may change his mind. Once he accepted it he can no longer change his mind<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">There are situations where one of the co-owners of the courtyard has lost his veto power even if the troublesome store was new. The question to be discussed is, what means “accepted”? If a new owner does not immediately complain, this certainly does not make him lose his right.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, even after he accepted it, he can change his mind, for he can tell him, they come and go here, they are looking for you and do not find you, and they increase traffic here<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">For him the veto power over a new enterprise in the courtyard remains valid if he can prove actual impairment of his quality of life by the new store.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Mana, one said, what is “his neighbor”? He let him live in his house<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody can complain about his own tenant.</i>. Once he accepted it he can no longer change his mind. Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This should be: “the other”, אוחרי.</i> said, one said, what is “his neighbor”? He opened a store for him<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that an owner can annul a lease if the tenant causes him problems. But if he modified his building for the new tenant’s business, he cannot complain.</i>. Once he accepted it he can no longer change his mind. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel parallels Rebbi Meïr, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:11, Note 163. A woman was informed of bodily defects of her prospective husband before her marriage. The majority hold that once married, she cannot ask the court to force a divorce because of the husband’s defects. R. Meїr disagrees as explained in the text.</i>: “For all of these says Rebbi Meїr that, even if he contracted with her, she can say ‘I thought that I could stand it, now I cannot.’ ”
“Nobody may object and tell him, I cannot sleep because of the noise of the hammer, or because of the noise of a grindstone, or because of the noise of children.” Those who teach boys and those who feed one another, their fellows may object to what they are doing<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah notwithstanding there can be only one (one-room) school in a residential courtyard, and only one restaurant.</i>, for he can tell him, they come and go here, they are looking for you and do not find you, and they increase traffic here<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This text is copied from the previous paragraph; it neither fits in grammar (number) nor in argument.</i>. Those Sepphoreans can object to one another for nailing down their looms<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Since the periodic concussions of the air will weaken the walls.</i>. Rebbi Abime bar Tobi instructed to put one loom between two walls<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In a residential area there can be no more than one loom to a room.</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Ḥaqula distanced a saw-horse four cubits from the wall<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Again to prevent the wall from being damaged by the noise caused by sawing.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Abun said, those pillars which are weakened are weakened because of the force of shouts. The wall of Ḥoms fell before the noise of the Persians’ arrows<sup class="footnote-marker">62,</sup><i class="footnote">Reading גירי “arrows” for גורי “lion whelps”.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In Sapor I’s invasion of Syria.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody’s wall was close to another’s wall he may not build his wall close by unless he moves it four cubits away<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Even though his wall is mentioned twice in this sentence, there is only one wall to be built. Since the next sentence speaks of a wall, either of a house or a wall of separation, parallel to the other person’s existing wall, probably the wall mentioned here is to be built at a right angle to the existing wall. In the interpretation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 22b" href="/Bava_Batra.22b">Babli, 22b</a>, there should be space between the two walls so that people may walk between them and by their walking compact the earth holding both walls.</i>. If there are windows, on top, from below, and on equal height, four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If there are windows in the other house’s wall, the new wall must preserve the other person’s privacy. Therefore, the wall must be either 4 cubits higher or 4 cubits lower than the window. If it is of equal hight then it must be at least 4 cubits distant to allow daylight into the window.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody’s wall was,” etc. In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, the Mishnah speaks of those coming first to settle in a new town<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 22b" href="/Bava_Batra.22b">Babli 22b</a>, in the name of R. Yose b. Ḥanina. They imply that in a settled area, a wall built at a right angle may be built adjacent to the existing wall.</i>. Rebbi La said, The Mishnah speaks of a sealed wall<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This wall mentioned in the first sentence is solid, having no windows.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah speaks of a barricade<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic سدم “to close a door, to barricade”; not the wall of a house but the fence of an enclosure.</i> wall. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the Mishnah says so: “If there are windows, on top, from below, and on equal height, four cubits.” On top four cubits, lest he could stand and see; from below four cubits, lest he could stand and see; at equal hight lest he obscure the sides<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If there are windows in the other house’s wall, the new wall must preserve the other person’s privacy. Therefore, the wall must be either 4 cubits higher or 4 cubits lower than the window. If it is of equal hight then it must be at least 4 cubits distant to allow daylight into the window.</i>. This was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>, including the statement of R. Yose bar Abun.</i>: “One distances the wall from a window by the size of a window.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One distances the ladder<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The permanent ladder leading to the upper floor apartment.</i> from a dovecote by four cubits lest a marten jump<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Onto the dovecote and eat the pigeons. If a builder contracts to build an access to an upper floor and disregards this rule it is malpractice and he has to rebuild the access at his own expense.</i>, and the wall four cubits from the leader<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">By which the rainwater flows from the roof to the earth. Near the leader the earth is soft and does not support anything.</i> so it can support the ladder. One distances the dovecote from town by fifty cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is axiomatic that a pigeon chick which cannot yet fly does not move farther than 50 cubits away from the dovecote. Therefore, any chick within 50 cubits belongs to the owner of the dovecote; the rule prevents property disputes about pigeon chicks.</i>. A person may not build a dovecote on his property unless he have fifty cubits in each direction<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is axiomatic that a pigeon chick which cannot yet fly does not move farther than 50 cubits away from the dovecote. Therefore, any chick within 50 cubits belongs to the owner of the dovecote; the rule prevents property disputes about pigeon chicks.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, the area of four <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The dovecote has to be the center of a square of surface area 300’000 square cubits, of side length 547.72 cubits, in its owner’s possession.</i>, the distance one sends a pigeon. But if he bought it even with the area of a quarter [<i>qab</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A square of area 104 1/6 square cubits, of edge lengths slightly over 10 cubits; cf. Chapter 7, Note 49.</i>, it is in his power<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">An existing dovecote which does not conform to the rules does not have to be removed.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One distances the ladder,” etc., until “in his power.” Rebbi Eleazar said, that is, to the right hand side. But to the left he may put it up immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If the leader is built so that the water flows to the right hand side, one may build the ladder close to it on the other side since its stability will not be compromised.</i>.
Here, you say 50 cubits. But there, you say 30 <i>stadia.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, they feed within fifty cubits; they fly even four <i>mil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.7.6"><i>Bava qamma</i> 7:10, Notes 105–107</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A pigeon chick which is found within 50 cubits belongs to the owner of the dovecote; farther away than 50 cubits it belongs to its finder<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is axiomatic that a pigeon chick which cannot yet fly does not move farther than 50 cubits away from the dovecote. Therefore, any chick within 50 cubits belongs to the owner of the dovecote; the rule prevents property disputes about pigeon chicks.</i>. If it was found between two dovecotes, if it is closer to one of them, it belongs to it; in the middle, both [owners] shall split evenly.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A pigeon chick which is found within 50 cubits,” etc. That is, if no public road interrupts between them<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last case in the Mishnah, if the chick was found between two dovecotes less than 100 cubits from one another.</i>. But not if a public road interrupts between them<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">One may assume that the chick was lost by a passer-by who gave up hope to recover it; therefore, it belongs to the finder. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 23b-24a" href="/Bava_Batra.23b-24a">Babli 23b/24a</a>).</i>. This is learned from that, and that is learned from this<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">“That” is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.3.5">Halakhah <i>Bava meṣia‘</i>2:4, Notes 54–55</a>. Something found in an apartment shared by two people has to be advertised; in a hotel frequented by 3 or more people it belongs to the finder.</i>. This is learned from that: if there were two<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Dovecotes within 50 cubits of the chick.</i>, he has to make it public. That is learned from this: if there were three<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Dovecotes within 50 cubits of the chick.</i>, it would belong to its finder<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">“That” is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.3.5">Halakhah <i>Bava meṣia‘</i>2:4, Notes 54–55</a>. Something found in an apartment shared by two people has to be advertised; in a hotel frequented by 3 or more people it belongs to the finder.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One distances a tree from a town by 25 cubits; but carob tree or sycamore by 50 cubits; Abba Shaul says, every non-fruit tree by 50 cubits. If the town existed before the tree, one cuts it down and does not pay for it; if the tree existed before the town, one cuts it down<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The developer of the town has the right to cut the surrounding trees down provided that he pay for them.</i> and pays for it. If there is a doubt which one existed before the other, one cuts it down but does not pay<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since the burden of proof is on the claimant.</i>.<br>One distances a threshing floor 50 cubits from the town<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Because the chaff flying from a threshing floor is unhealthy.</i>. A person shall not make a threshing floor on his property unless he owns 50 cubits in each direction<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The directions are N,E,S,W. He must own a 100 by 100 square, not only a circle of 50 cubits radius.</i>. He distances it from another’s orchards or ploughed field so that he will not cause damage<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In case of a dispute, the burden of proof is on him that he did not impair another person’s property.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One distances a tree from a town,” etc.. אֲפִילוּ בְתוֹךְ שֶׁלּוֹ אָסוּר Because it creates shadow where it stands or because its falling leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This translation follows the reading of E שינויו, which S. Lieberman reads as שֶׁינְּוִיָּו, equivalent to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 48b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.48b">Babli נְבִייָתוֹ (<i>Avodah zarah</i> 48b</a>).<br>In the parallel, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 24b" href="/Bava_Batra.24b">Babli 24b</a>, the word is נויי which Maimonides (<i>Šekhenim</i>10:1), and Rashi by the absence of a commentary, understand as “beauty” but R. Gershom (correct text in <i>Arukh completum s. v.</i> ניא) explains the Babli’s “because of נויי” that all places in and around the town should be freely accessible (maybe for the fire brigade.) He seems to identify the Yerushalmi’s ניא with the Babli’s נויי. Torczyner and Ben Jehudah, in the latter’s <i>Thesaurus,</i> do not come to a conclusion as to meaning and etymology of the word.</i> are dangerous? What is the difference between them? If it stands on one’s own property. If you say, because it creates shadow where it stands, on his property it is permitted. But it you say, because its motion is dangerous, even on his property it is forbidden.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One distances carcasses, and graves, and tanneries 50 cubits from the town, and one builds a tannery only to the East of a town<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since most days of the year the wind blows from the West. The bad smell of the tannery will be downwind.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, one may build in any direction except to the West, and distances himself by 50 cubits.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One distances carcasses,” etc. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: To the place where one prepares the skins<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This explains why animal carcasses come under the same rules as tanneries: The process of making leather starts with skinning carcasses.</i>. In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, up to the place where one might smell what he makes<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to the problem of locating a tannery. Any location is fine where the smell does not reach the town.</i>. It was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta 1:8</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 25a" href="/Bava_Batra.25a">Babli 25a</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Aqiba says, he may build it in any direction and distances himself, except to the West because that [wind] is frequent.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since most days of the year the wind blows from the West. The bad smell of the tannery will be downwind.</i>” Rebbi Mana went to accompany a person afflicted with a skin disease. His father told him, do not walk to the East of him but rather to the West of him.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One distances a soaking pond<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A person who makes a pond to soak his flax has to put it far enough from existing vegetable fields to avoid being sued for any damage to the vegetables.</i> from vegetables, and leeks from onions<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In order to avoid crosspollination at flowering time.</i>, and mustard from bees<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid causing the honey to smell of mustard.</i>. Rebbi Yose permits mustard.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One distances a soaking pond,” etc. It was stated: One distances an onion bed from leeks, but Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon permits it. Rebbi Jacob ben Dosai said, from these teachings [it follows] that mutually they have to distance themselves one from the other<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah warns a person not to plant leeks next to an existing bed of onions to avoid a damage suit; the <i>baraita</i> does the same for a person wanting to plant onions next to an existing bed of leeks. One has to conclude that in all cases the latecomer has to be careful not to hurt existing cultures and the rules of the Mishnah work both ways.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Because the bees’ mouth becomes sharp and they destroy the honey<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The person putting a bee-hive near to a bed of mustard only hurts himself, rather than the grower of mustard.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One distances a tree 25 cubits from a cistern, but carob tree and sycamore 50 cubits, whether on top or level<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">In order to avoid damage to the walls of the cistern by the roots. The required distance applies if the tree is planted on a level with the cistern or above it on a hillside. In the Babylonian sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 25b" href="/Bava_Batra.25b">Babli 25b</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 1:12" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).1.12">Tosephta 1:12</a>, it is emphasized that this also applies if the tree is planted on a hillside below the level of the cistern. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.1.2.4"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 1:2 (Notes 28–34</a>), R. Yose ben Rebbi Abun characterizes the Babli’s opinion as R. Simeon’s and holds that R. Yose, who is the higher authority, allows a tree to be planted below the cistern. He will require that the cistern’s bottom not be below its owner’s property line on the hill. (It is not obvious that the opinions of the editors of the other parts of the Yerushalmi are accepted in <i>Neziqin</i>.)</i>. If the cistern existed before, he shall cut it down and pay for it<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">He (the tree’s owner) shall cut his tree down if he (the cistern’s owner) pays for it.</i>; if the tree existed before, one shall not cut it down. Rebbi Yose said, even if the cistern existed before, one shall not cut it down since the one digs on his property and the other plants on his property<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owner of the tree legally may plant a tree on his property, the provident owner of the neighboring plot will build his cistern at 25 cubits’ distance from his property line.</i>.<br>A person should not plant a tree next to another’s field unless he distanced himself from it by four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This is good advice rather than a legal requirement. A person planting a fruit tree on his property needs a circle with a radius of 4 cubits around it for servicing the tree; he has to avoid trespassing on other people’s property.</i>, whether vines or any other tree. If there was a wall between them, each one goes close to the wall<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since then there is no danger of trespassing. גָּדֵר, like Arabic جَدُر, is a rural stone wall made without mortar, not a fence as in modern Hebrew.</i>. If its roots extended to another’s field, he lowers the roots three handbreadths in order not to hinder the plough<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the field’s owner is free to remove any roots in his soil down to a depth of 3 handbreadths (about 30 cm) without asking or paying anybody.</i>. If one was digging a cistern, or a ditch, or a cavern, he cuts and excavates and the wood is his.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One distances a tree,” etc. <b>Halakhah 12:</b> “A person should not plant,” etc. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The rabbis’ reason is that civilization is based on cisterns<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The tree’s owner did not break any law in planting it, as R. Yose insists. Then why do the rabbis <i>force</i> the tree’s owner to cut it down if the cistern’s owner offers him money?</i>. Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Joshua answers the rabbis thus: just as you hold that civilization is based on cisterns, so I am holding that civilization is based on trees.
<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Text of E, missing in L, but presupposed by R. Yose’s reaction to it.</i>[Rebbi <i>Yasa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The correct attribution is in the quote at the end of the paragraph.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Roots are irrelevant for First Fruits.]<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">They are very light and take up a large volume.</i> Rebbi Yose said, he said it correctly. For if he did say that roots are important, nobody could ever bring First Fruits since the roots of one enter the other’s ground and vice-versa. Therefore, Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Roots are irrelevant for First Fruits.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a tree overhangs another’s field, he may cut it in the height of the plough’s ox-goad<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">He may cut all overhanging branches of a fruit tree to the height of the ox-goad used with his plough.</i>; carob tree and sycamore along the plumb line<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the field may cut these trees vertically at his property line.</i>. Over an irrigated field along the plumb line<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the field may cut these trees vertically at his property line.</i>. Abba Shaul says, every non-fruit-bearing tree along the plumb line<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the field may cut these trees vertically at his property line.</i>.<br>If a tree overhangs the public domain, one has to cut it so that a camel with a rider on it may pass; Rebbi Jehudah says, loaded with flax or bundles of vine cuttings<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">They are very light and take up a large volume.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, any tree [must be cut] along the plumb line because of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">105,</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the field may cut these trees vertically at his property line.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon essentially rejects the rabbis’ rule as following Gentile law (<i>Corpus iuris, Decretum de arboribus caedendis</i> requires that branches overhanging a public road must be cut up to a height of 15 Roman <i>pedes,</i> not quite 4.5 m.) He insists on a Jewish rationale. The impurity of the dead is transferred not only by touch but by being under one “tent” with a corpse. The crown of a tree forms such a “tent.” If the tree overhangs a road but grows inside a fence or wall which obstructs the view, a person passing under it could be inadvertentently become severely impure (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.3.4"><i>Nazir</i> 7:3, Notes 163–164</a>). Therefore, in the Land of Israel all overhangs have to be eliminated. He has no rules for other countries.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a tree overhangs another’s field,” etc.<br>“If a tree overhangs the public domain,,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">A Hebrew version of this story is in <i>Tanḥuma Šofeṭim</i> 3 [<i>Ginze Schechter</i> I (New York 1928), p. 130]. A similar story, about R. Yannai, is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 60" href="/Bava_Batra.60">Babli, 60a/b</a>.</i> Rebbi Jonathan was careful in judging; there was a Roman there who was his neighbor both in the field and the house. Rebbi Jonathan had a tree overhanging the Roman’s property. A similar case came before him<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">A case of a tree overhanging from one person’s property over another’s.</i>. He told them, go and return next morning<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">A most unsual procedure.</i>. This Roman said, because of me he did not decide the case. Tomorrow I will abandon my work<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of E: אוּמְנִין דִּידִי “my job” makes good sense; no conjectures are needed. The text of L must be classified as a scribal error.</i> and see how the judgment goes. If he judges all people and does not judge himself, he is not human. At nightfall, Rebbi Jonathan sent for his carpenter and told him, go and cut everything that overhangs the Roman’s. In the morning, the parties appeared before him. He told him, go and cut what overhangs into his property. The Roman said to him, what is with yours? He told him, go and see; what was done with mine do with yours. He went out and looked. He said, praised be the Jews’ God.
A woman honored Rebbi Jonathan with figs<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">As a bakshish, which as a judge he could not accept.</i>. He told her, please if you brought them covered, take them away covered; but if you brought them in the open, take them away in the open, so that people should not say that she brought him denars and he honored her with figs<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">But still accepts bribes.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥanina brought Rebbi Jonathan to his garden and served him figs. When he left, he saw one tree of white <i>benot ševa‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Delicacy figs; cf. <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2:8, Note 135.</i>. He asked him, why did you not let me eat from these? He told him, they are my son’s. Rebbi Ḥanina was afraid of robbing his son.
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Practice follows Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Everybody agrees that practice follows Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Babli does not discuss the matter, it follows that it accepts practice following the anonymous Tanna, which is appropriate outside the Land of Israel.</i>.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Confirmation of claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A person who claims legal acquisition of real estate but has no deed can defend himself against an accusation of being an illegal squatter and must be confirmed by the court in his rights if he can prove undisturbed possession for three consecutive years by witnesses.<br>There is a second meaning of חֲזָקָה “permanence of the status quo”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.9"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1, Note 30</a>.</i> of houses, cisterns, ditches, and caverns, and bath houses, and dove-cotes, oil presses, irrigated land, and slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Who in this respect follow the law of real estate; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3, Notes 347–355</a>.</i>, and everything which yields continuously is three years from day to day. The claim of undisturbed possession of an unirrigated field is three years, but not from day to day<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">But undisturbed collection of three consecutive harvests.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The claim of undisturbed possession of houses, cisterns, ditches, and caverns,” etc. From where confirmation of claims of undisturbed possession? <sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel argument is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 28" href="/Bava_Batra.28">Babli, 28a/b</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, we heard from those who went to Usha<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli identified as R. Ismael. In the aftermath of the Bar Kokhba war and its consequences, the Synhedrion was reconstituted at Usha under the leadership of R. Jehudah.</i> that they learned it from the notorious bull<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of the notorious bull only apply to an animal which gored three times. The limit of three years is popular usage; no biblical sources are claimed.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, who holds that a bull becomes notorious after three days? Not Rebbi Jehudah? As we stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.2.6.1">Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i> 2:6</a>. Only R. Jehudah requires that the agressive behavior of the bull be confirmed by testimony for three consecutive days; R. Meïr holds that three incidents are sufficient, not necessarily on consecutive days.
