But nobody holds that legal possession can be claimed on the basis of 3 incidents of interrupted squatting.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, they said three years only for the case that if he<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The original owner of the land.</i> is in Spain, he<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The squatter.</i> should be in possession for one year; they went and told him during one year, and he<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The original owner of the land.</i> returns within one year.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This means that intrinsically R. Jehudah only requires one year of undisturbed possession. If the institution of חֲזָקָה is based on R. Jehudah’s teaching, it is only a tenuous analogy, without any claim of Biblical origin.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">A claim of possession must be based on a claim of ownership. But transfer of ownership is only a precondition for the transfer of possession which needs a separate action. This is detailed in <i>Qiddušin,</i> Chapter 1, from which most of this paragraph is taken.</i> Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If someboldy is selling a house to another, when he delivered the key [the buyer] took possession<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Note 435</a>. The text here agrees with the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 51-52" href="/Bava_Kamma.51-52">Babli (<i>Bava qamma</i> 51a/52b</a>) against the Yerushalmi <i>Qiddušin.</i></i>. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If somebody is selling a house to another, when [the buyer] stored produce there, he took possession<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Note 435</a>.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, only produce usually stored. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: If somebody is selling a cistern to another, when he handed over its pails [the buyer] took possession<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Note 434</a>. There, the statement is by R. Simeon bar Abba in the name of R. Joshua ben Levi.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, this must read: R. Simeon ben Laqish in the name of R. Joshua ben Levi. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.5"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4 (Notes 432–433</a>), it is in the name of R. Yannai.</i>: If somebody sold a flock to another person, when [the seller] delivered the <i>maškokît,</i> [the buyer] took possession. What is <i>maškokît</i>? Some say, the shepherd’s staff. But some say, a <i>pandura</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.5"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4 Note 433</a>.</i>. And some say, the bell-wether. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Bar Qappara: If somebody is selling a burial chamber to another, when that one buried there one dead it is confirmation of claim for the entire chamber. When he buried three dead in three chambers, it is confirmation of claim for the entire burial cavern<sup class="footnote-marker">16a</sup><i class="footnote">For the rules of burial caverns see Halakhah 6:8.</i>.
“And slaves.” <sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is partially quote and partially reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.2.3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 4:2, Notes 107–111</a>.</i> Rebbi Simon the brother of Rebbi Jehudah bar Zavdi in the name of Rav: As long as a baby lies in the public domain, his father or his mother can testify about him. Once he has been collected from the public domain, one needs two witnesses and its father and mother are like two witnesses. Rebbi Abbahu said, can a person be suspected to say about one who is not his son that he is his son<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Why should not his father alone be empowered to recognize his child?</i>? Maybe it was said about the property of a convert<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">If he has no Jewish children, he has no heirs in Jewish law and all Jews are potentially entitled to inherit from him. If then he recognizes an otherwise unknown person as his son, he potentially damages other people’s claim; the recognition needs two witnesses, <i>viz.,</i> father and mother. But once he is married in Jewish law, the Torah gives the father the sole power to recognize his child; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.17"><i>Deut.</i> 21:17</a>.</i>? He told him, but all our teachers in the Diaspora testify that in this form we heard it from Rav Ada bar Abuh<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In E and <i>Or zarua‘</i> I §340 (end): Rabba bar Abuh. This seems to be the correct attribution.</i>. Rav Ḥisda said: That is, if the baby is not moving. But [the case of] a baby which is moving refers to what Rebbi Joḥanan said: property rights on jumping calves and lambs which move from place to place cannot be proven by possession<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, such a child is a foundling; his parents cannot act as witnesses since by their testimony they declare themselves his relatives and thus disqualify themselves. This conclusion is the opposite of the one reached in the longer text in <i>Qiddušin;</i> it parallels the conclusion reached by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 36a" href="/Bava_Batra.36a">Babli, 36a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.11"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5, Notes 502–513</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 52b-53a" href="/Bava_Batra.52b-53a">Babli 52b/53a</a>.</i> Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If two fields of a proselyte were involved<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">By becoming Jewish, the proselyte changed his ethnic status and severed his links with his biological family. If he failed to start a Jewish family, he died without heirs; the fields now are ownerless property. {It was characteristic of Egyption intercommunal law that inheritance across ethnic boundaries was impossible. So the daughter of a Roman <i>missicius</i> as Roman citizen could not inherit from her native Egyptian mother (cf. R. Taubenschlag, <i>loc. cit. Bava meṣia‘</i> 1, Note 84, p. 142.)<br>The fields can be acquired only by performing some agricultural work, e. g., weeding. But work on a field only acquires real estate adjacent to the place on which the work was done.</i>, with a boundary strip in between. If he cut off from one of them with the intent of taking possession also of the other but not the boundary strip in between, the one was acquired by him but not the other. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yasa: If he intended to acquire starting [from]<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, missing in L, confirmed by the text in <i>Qiddušin.</i></i> the boundary strip and below? Rav Ḥisda said, property of a proselyte which he took possession of in the North by cutting off with the intent of also acquiring the one to the South, but did not have the intent of acquiring the strip in the middle, he acquired the one to the North but not the one to the South<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is really the same as the one attributed earlier to R. Joḥanan. The genuine source is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a> where the two statements refer to different situations.</i>. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.4.1">Mishnah 5:9</a>. This refers to a standing crop on a field. After harvest, the produce is taken into possession by the buyer by moving some of the crop. The problem is, how can standing crop be acquired while the field remains the seller’s property? Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.11"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5 Note 513</a>.</i>: “If it was connected to the ground and he cut off anything, he acquired.” Samuel said, he acquired only what he cut off. Rebbi Eudaimon the Emigrant said, this was said concerning the property of a proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since no real estate was acquired, taking some stalks can have no influence on the remainder of the ownerless crop.</i>. Rav Ḥisda said, Rebbi Isaac: It was reported that this was said concerning the property of a proselyte, so it should present no difficulty for Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since if the crop was paid for and the buyer was told by the seller to harvest it, Samuel must agree that by cutting the first stalk he takes possession of the entire crop.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.2"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Notes 414–417</a>.</i> Rav Ḥisda said, “draw this animal close to you in order to take possession,” he took possession<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Implied by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i>1:4</a>.</i>. “To take possession of its offspring,” he did [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, missing in L, required bt the text in <i>Qiddušin.</i></i> take possession. “To take possession of it together with its offspring,” he did (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L, missing in E, to be deleted with the text in <i>Qiddušin.</i></i> take possession. Rebbi Nasa<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qiddušin:</i> Yose.</i> said, that is, if it was not pregnant. But if it was pregnant, they treated [the fetus] as one of its limbs, as we did state<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of an Amoraic text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 6:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.6.2.9"><i>Demay</i> 6:3, Notes 79–83</a>, and its slightly garbled parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.7"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Notes 439–441</a>.</i>: He who sells the fetus of his animal to another person did not do anything; the future children of his slave girl to another person, he did not do anything; the tithes of his field to another person, he did not do anything; the airspace of his dry land to another person, he did not do anything. But he may sell him an animal and reserve her fetus for himself, sell him a slave girl and reserve her children for himself, sell him a field and reserve the tithes for himself, sell him dry land and reserve the airspace for himself.
<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is a slight rewording of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.2.4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 4:2, Notes 112–117</a>.</i> Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Ḥisda: Three are trustworthy immediately: a midwife, a caravan, and the one who purifies her colleagues. A midwife as long as [the woman] sits on the birthing chair, from the verse: “The midwife took and wound, etc.” A caravan, as Rebbi Simon, the brother of Rebbi Jehudah bar Zavdi, said in the name of Rav: As long as [a baby]<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, missing in L, required by the parallel in <i>Qiddušin</i> and the context.</i> lies in the public domain, either its father or its mother can testify about him. Once he has been collected from the public domain, one needs two witnesses; its father and mother may become its two witnesses. The one who purifies her colleagues, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Niddah</i> 9:4.</i>: “Three women sleep on one bed. If blood was found under one of them, all three are impure. If one of them checked herself out and found herself impure, she is impure and the other two are pure.” Rebbi Abba said, only within 24 hours.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Ismael says, three months in the first and three months in the last with twelve months in the middle, for a total of eighteen months<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The three years of undisturbed possession required in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> are not three calendar years but three harvests with time for preparation before the harvest and processing afterwards.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, one month in the first and one month in the last with twelve months in the middle, for a total of fourteen months. Rebbi Ismael said, when has this been said? A field of grain. But for an orchard, if he harvested his yield<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The grape harvest, which in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut.</i> 22:9</a> is called “the vineyard’s yield”.</i>, lifted his olives, and collected his cut figs it counts for three years<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Depending on the trees in the orchard, three different undisturbed harvests establish a presumption of lawful possession.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Ismael says, the first three months,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 36b" href="/Bava_Batra.36b">Babli 36b</a>.</i> Samuel said, these are the words of Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Aqiba. But the Sages say, three years grain cutting, three years grape harvesting, three years olive harvesting. Rav said, these are the words of Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Aqiba. But the Sages say, three years from day to day. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Just as they disagree here, so they disgree about Elijah’s years<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Whether the three years’ draught mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 18:1" href="/I_Kings.18.1"><i>1K.</i> 18:1</a> were 14 months, 18 months, or three full years.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> There are three regions for claims of undisturbed possession: Judea, Transjordan<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">According to some interpretations, the region between the Judean foothills and the Mediterranian; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.9.2.1"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 9:2, Note 39</a>.</i>, and Galilee. If he was in Judea and somebody claimed undisturbed possession in Galilee, or in Galilee and somebody claimed undisturbed possession in Judea, it is not undisturbed possession unless he be in the same province<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If the original owner can prove that he was not in the province during the claimant’s occupation of the land, a claim of undisturbed possession will be disallowed and the claimant evicted unless he has documentary proof of his claim.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, they said three years only for the case that if he<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The original owner of the land.</i> is in Spain<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Or Apamea in Phrygia; that would be presupposed in R. Ismael’s and R. Aqiba’s interpretations.</i>, he<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The squatter.</i> should be in possession for one year; they went and told him during one year, and he<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The original owner of the land.</i> returns within one year.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This means that intrinsically R. Jehudah only requires one year of undisturbed possession. If the institution of חֲזָקָה is based on R. Jehudah’s teaching, it is only a tenuous analogy, without any claim of Biblical origin.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “There are three regions for claims of undisturbed possession,” etc. Rav said, this was taught in emergency situations<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">That a person being in a different province of the same country cannot claim undisturbed possession only applies in wartime or similar circumstances when there is no regular communication between the regions. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 38a" href="/Bava_Batra.38a">Babli, 38a</a>, this opinion is explained as following Rav’s opinion that the injured party may protest anywhere, not necessarily in the presence of the person in actual possession.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥanina said, the years of <i>‘orlah</i> are in dispute between them<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnah and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.2.2">Halakhah 2</a>. For Samuel, a new orchard which may not be harvested can never be acquired by a claim of undisturbed possession; for Rav it may be acquired after three full years. It also is possible that the statement refers to the dispute between R. Aqiba and R. Ismael. Since R. Aqiba only requires minimal preparation for a harvest, he will recognize years in which the trees cannot be harvested as included in the time required for a claim of undisturbed possession. R. Ismael, who requires extensive preparations, will not recognize those years.</i>.
Rav said, there can be no claim of undisturbed possession from a fugitive, nor one made from one country to another. Samuel said, there can be a claim of undisturbed possession from a fugitive, and a claim of undisturbed possession made from one country to another<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As the Babli points out, 38b, this statement by Rav contradicts the earlier one that the rule of the Mishnah only applies to emergency situations. Samuel’s position is clear; he holds that a protest can be made before any court which will sign a dated statement which may be used before the court at the place of the disputed real estate without time limit. For the position of Rav, two explanations are given there. One is that he holds that a protest must be made in person and that his statement about the Mishnah was to explain the thinking of the Tanna, not his own. Another is that he also holds that a protest can be submitted in any court but that such an appearance in a distant court can be expected only from a fugitive because of nonpayment of taxes or other debt but not from a person accused of a capital crime. Also, the choice of the word “country” instead of “province” refers to a country outside the Roman Empire, such as Persia or India, from where any communication might be difficult or impossible.</i>. Rav Naḥman bar Rav Isaac<sup class="footnote-marker">48*</sup><i class="footnote">The identity of this Amora is not clear. The fourth generation Amora Rav Naḥman bar Isaac never is quoted as Rav Naḥman bar <i>Rav</i> Isaac.</i> said, a verse supports what Samuel said: “The king gave her an adjutant,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 8:6" href="/II_Kings.8.6"><i>2K.</i> 8:6</a>. Since the Ṣhunamite woman needed the king’s intervention after an absence of 7 years in the Land of the Philistines it follows that she could get no relief in the regular courts since a claim of undisturbed possession could be made against a proprietor living in another country.</i>” etc.
Rebbi Eleazar said: Even two אבטיניות, e. g., שלומי and נבירו<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 55a" href="/Bekhorot.55a"><i>Bekhorot</i> Babli 55a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.3">Tosephta 7:3</a>) the spelling is אבטליות or אבטלאות; with a change of liquids, also אבטיניות. In E and <i>Megillah</i>1:1 l. 44: אבטוניות. In <i>Sefer haIṭṭur</i> 1–2, p. 88b (מודעא) אפסיטואות. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 38a" href="/Bava_Batra.38a"><i>Meïri Bava batra ad</i> 38a</a> (ed. A. Schreiber p. 213) אכסניות “hostelries”.<br><i>‘Arukh</i> explains “big cities”; no acceptable etymologies are known. (If the word be Greek, the Yerushalmi transliteration would be more reliable than the Babylonian. But Jastrow’s αὐτονομία is doubtful, as is Zuckermandel’s ἀποτέλειοι.)<br>The names of the towns appear as שלומי, השולמי or נבירו, נמירי, נמורי ;נמר (גבירו, נמוכי). S. Klein-י) (עבר הירדן היהודי 1925 ם determines the places as Bet Nimra in Transjordan opposite Alexandria in Cisjordan.</i> separated by the Jordan and a person may see another taking possession of the field, his property, there is no creation of a claim of undisturbed possession unless he was in the same locality, in the same province<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If the original owner can prove that he was not in the province during the claimant’s occupation of the land, a claim of undisturbed possession will be disallowed and the claimant evicted unless he has documentary proof of his claim.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This is a slightly more detailed exposition of a paragraph in <i>Yebamot</i>12:1, Note 29.</i> Rav said, the main confirmation of a claim of possession is storing the produce. Does Rav not agree about weeding and turning the soil? Rav agrees about weeding and turning the soil. What does it mean that Rav said, the main indication of a claim of possession is storing the produce? That if they saw him ploughing, and harvesting, and binding into sheaves, and threshing, and grading, but did not see him storing the produce, there is no claim of possession without storing the produce<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since all other work could be done by a hired hand. While taking the final produce without ever having worked the land might be an indication of theft, working the land without enjoying its yield disproves ownership.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.1.12"><i>Yebamot</i> 12:1, Notes 27–28</a>, about the act of freeing the childless widow from levirate marriage. It is quoted here as an appendix to the previous paragraph.</i> Rav said, the essence of <i>ḥalîṣah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since no real estate was acquired, taking some stalks can have no influence on the remainder of the ownerless crop.</i> is the untying of the shoelace. Did not Rebbi Abba say in the name of Rav Jehudah, Rebbi Zeriqan turns to it in the name of Rav: The words of the Sages: If she slips off but did not spit, or spat but did not take off, the <i>ḥaliṣah</i> is invalid unless she slips off and spits<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since if the crop was paid for and the buyer was told by the seller to harvest it, Samuel must agree that by cutting the first stalk he takes possession of the entire crop.</i>. Rav agrees “unless she slips off and spits”; it is implied by what Rav said, the essence of slipping off is untying the shoelaces.
Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina asked Rebbi Joḥanan: Must a protest be filed before a court? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: a protest does not have to be filed before a court<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A protest against unlawful occupation of land is a matter of testimony, rather than court action. It needs only two witnesses and does not have to be in writing; this is the position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 39b-40a" href="/Bava_Batra.39b-40a">Babli (39b/40a</a>).</i>. But some say, Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina asked Rebbi Joḥanan’s student: Must a protest be filed before a court? Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: a protest must be filed before a court<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">It needs three judges and a written confirmation by the clerk of court.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 39a" href="/Bava_Batra.39a">Babli, 39a</a>, R. Ḥiyya bar Abba is quoted as holding that a protest is a matter of witnessing, needing two witnesses but no court, and R. Abbahu that it is a matter of court record, needing three judges, both in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>. Samuel said, even if he protested against him in front of workers, it is a protest<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A protest against unlawful occupation of land is a matter of testimony, rather than court action. It needs only two witnesses and does not have to be in writing; this is the position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 39b-40a" href="/Bava_Batra.39b-40a">Babli (39b/40a</a>).</i>, but he has to protest in each one of the three years. Giddul ben Minyamin had a case; his judges were Ḥilqiah bar Tobi, Rav Huna, and Ḥiyya bar Rav. Ḥiyya bar Rav told them, so said my father: Once he protested the first three years, he is not required to protest further<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 39b" href="/Bava_Batra.39b">Babli, 39b</a>, disagrees and in addition holds that the protest has to be renewed at the end of each three-year period.</i>. It was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.4">Tosephta 2:4</a>.</i>: “If he<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The occupant.</i> ate it as his own for six years, he<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The former owner.</i> protested against him the first three years; then<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: “at the end”, implying that the occupant was silent during the first protest.</i> he<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The occupant.</i> said, you sold it to me, you gave it to me as a gift, there is no claim of undisturbed possession. Because of an initial claim<sup class="footnote-marker">60a</sup><i class="footnote">An assertion of ownership at the start would be an argument accepted by the court.</i> it would have been a claim of undisturbed possession since<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.4.1">Mishnah 3:4</a>.</i> any claim of undisturbed possession not accompanied by a claim of legal ownership is not a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: Any claim not asserted from the start is invalid.</i>.” Samuel said, if it is so, nothing would be left for the great people of the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.1.2"><i>Bava Meṣi‘a</i> 5:1, Note 9</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Any claim of undisturbed possession not accompanied by a claim of legal ownership is not a claim of undisturbed possession. How is this? He<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The former owner.</i> said to him, what are you doing on my property? Nobody ever said anything to me<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The occupant.</i> is not a claim of undisturbed possession. You sold it to me, you gave it to me as a gift, is a claim of undisturbed possession. A person entering because of inheritance does not need a claim<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The heir does not have to establish his ownership by investigating how the bequeather acquired title.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Any claim of undisturbed possession not accompanied by a claim of legal ownership,” etc. But it needs proof of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In order to be safe from actions against his title, the owner has to hold on to his deed until he has established a three years’ claim of undisturbed possession.</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 2:2</a>. If a person informs another that property which he holds did belong to his father, he must be believed without proof that he holds legal title since “the mouth which forbade is the mouth which permitted.”</i>, we have stated: “Rebbi Joshua agrees that if somebody says to anothet, ‘this field’, etc.” <sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">A shortened reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:2:3-10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.2.3-10.6"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:2, Notes 37–43</a>.</i> As the following. Reuben ate from a field claiming that it was his. Simeon brought witnesses that his father died in possession. One removes [the field] from Reuben and hands it over to Simeon. Reuben went and brought witnesses that [Simeon’s] father did not die in possession. Rebbi Naḥman ben Jacob said, I took it from Reuben, I am returning it to him. Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav died before Rav Naḥman b. Jacob became Chief Judge, one must read with the text of <i>Ketubot</i>: “The rabbis here” who disagree with the Babylonian decision and hold that a revision of a prior court decision has to be treated as a new case and must follow standard court procedure.</i> said, when you gave it to him, you gave it on the basis of a court decree. From there on the burden of proof is on the claimant.
Who informs<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This also refers to the last statement of the Mishnah, that the heir is not required to establish title.</i>? Rebbi Abba said, the witnesses to the death inform. Think of it, if the witnesses did not know! Rebbi Yose said, the field always is in the possession of its owner. From there on the burden of proof is on the claimant<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">An heir is always held <i>prima facie</i> as owner and possessor. The burden of proof in an attack on his title rests on the attacker.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Partners<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">If a property was bought by partners and one partner used the property for himself during three years, this still does not establish a claim of ownership.</i> and sharecroppers<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The sharecropper is supposed to work the land. Even if he takes in the entire crop and stores it in his barn it is no proof of ownership since possibly he pays the landlord from the proceeds of the sale of some of the crop.</i> have no claim of undisturbed possession. A husband has no claim of undisturbed possession in his wife’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is his duty to take care of her property and he is entitled to its produce, his activities cannot establish a claim of ownership.</i>, nor a wife in her husband’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband is obligated to feed his wife, it would be legal for him to give her one of his fields to work on and live off its yield; but her taking care of the property and collecting all its yield cannot establish a claim of ownership.</i>, nor a father in his son’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the son inherited property from his maternal grandfather, the father is obligated to take care of it without acquiring property rights.</i>, nor the son in his father’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If he takes care of his aging father’s property.</i>. When has this been said? For one who claims undisturbed possession. But if one is given a gift, or brothers distributed the estate, one takes possession of a convert’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Who died without leaving a will or legal heirs.</i>, when he locked, fenced in, or made any breach that is a legal form of taking in possession<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">This חֲזָקָה has a completely different meaning from the one presupposed in the preceding Mishnaiot. It describes the act of taking possession after acquiring ownership, as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.2-14"><i>Qiddušin,</i> Halakhah 1:5</a>. This can be any act only the proprietor would perform, not a visitor.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Partners and sharecroppers,” etc. “A partner.” Samuel said, “partner” is not mentioned here<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">A different treatment of the same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 42b" href="/Bava_Batra.42b">Babli, 42b</a>. Samuel deletes mention of partners from the Mishnah.</i>. Does the partner not have a claim to undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This is the version of L. E: שׁוּתָף יֵשׁ לוֹ חֲזָקָה The partner has a claim to undisturbed possession.</i>? Did not Samuel say that the partner who went and planted, planted with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">If a partner took possession, worked the property and reaped all its yield, one has to assume that there was an agreement between the partners to split the property. Therefore, if another partner disputes the existence of such an agreement after three years of undisturned possession, he is a claimant and the burden of proof is on him.</i>? When you say, the partner who went and planted, planted with permission, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The other partner, who can make an appearance and take part of the yield.</i> was there. When you say, the partner has no claim of undisturbed possession, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">If the other partner did not live at that place, the presumption is that the partners also bought property at the other place where the other partner takes all the yield, but no presumption is created of sole proprietorship at either place. Any partner who claims dissolution of the partnership has to bring proof.</i> was not there. What does he include in his stead<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah states “<i>and</i> sharecroppers,” it is clear that some word must precede the statement.</i>? Artisans<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This is part of the Mishnah in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss.</i> and robbers<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The extortionist who claims that the former owner handed over the property to avoid being murdered may be truthful but the argument is not heard in court. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 47a" href="/Bava_Batra.47a">Babli 47a</a>.</i> have no claim of undisturbed possession. <sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta 2:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 45b" href="/Bava_Batra.45b">Babli 45b</a>.</i>“If somebody saw his slave at an artisan’s, or his garments at the washer’s, and told him, give me my slave, give me my garments, but he answered him, you gave him to me as a gift; you sold him to me; this is no claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The artisan, while in possession, is the claimant who has to bring proof since usually he is not the owner of what he is working on. As S. Lieberman explains, if the owner decided to sell a young slave it was customary to send him to be an apprentice in order to increase his value; also vessels and garments to be sold may be given to be refurbished to increase their value.</i>. You told me to sell him<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The teacher who trained the slave then should find a buyer; this was customary.</i>; you instructed<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">A third person who can testify to the fact.</i> to give him to me as a gift, this is a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The former owner is the claimant who has to bring proof.</i>.”
“Sharecroppers.” Rav Huna said, a permanent sharecropper<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">He is no sharecropper in the modern sense but rather a tenant farmer in perpetuity, usually paying his rent in kind. It was stated earlier (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.3.6">Note 51</a>) that the proof of ownership of agricultural land is the storing of its yield. In this paragraph, a “claim of undisturbed possession” would be testimony that for three consecutive years the farmer collected all the harvest and stored it in his own barn without separating the landlord’s portion before storage.</i>, but a temporary sharecropper<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Who has a contract renewable periodically. Since in this case it is unlikely that the landlord’s share should not be delivered promptly, three years on non-delivery create a presumption of ownership; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 47a" href="/Bava_Batra.47a">Babli 47a</a>.</i> has a claim of undisturbed possession. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both teach that even a temporary sharecropper has no claim of undisturbed possession, for we may say that he<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The landlord.</i> is satisfied with him and let him<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The sharecropper’s son.</i> have it. There, they say that a sharecropper has no claim of undisturbed possession but a sharecropper’s son has a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of Maimonides, <i>Ṭo‘en weniṭ‘an</i> 14:2, this refers only to the case that the son claims to have bought the property. But if he claims that the father bought it and he inherited, the burden of proof is on him and he cannot claim undisturbed possession since the father could not have claimed it.</i>; Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish say, neither a sharecropper nor a sharecropper’s son have a claim of undisturbed possession, for we may say that he<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The landlord.</i> was satisfied with his father and let the son have it. As Rebbi Joḥanan said, a sharecropper who engaged another sharecropper has no claim of undisturbed possession since it is common for sharecroppers to engage sub-sharecroppers<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement is found in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 46a" href="/Bava_Batra.46a">Babli, 46a</a>.</i>.
In truth, should a man supervising his wife’s properties have a claim of undisturbed possession, since usually husbands supervise their wife’s properties? “A husband has no claim of undisturbed possession in his wife’s property” during her lifetime. But after her death he has a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Based on his right of inheritance.</i>. “Nor a wife in her husband’s property” during his lifetime. But after his death she has a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">If she claims the property as her <i>ketubah.</i></i>. “Nor a son in the father’s property” during his lifetime. But after the father’s death he has a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">As his part of the father’s estate.</i>. “Nor the father in his son’s property” during his lifetime. But after the son’s death he has a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">If the son died without children and the father is his heir.</i>.
Rav said, if he lowered it below ten [handbreadths] or increased above ten<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence of the Mishnah. A person taking possession of land he bought in the opinion of Rav must take some legally important action. A fence is legally a separation only if it is at least ten handbreadths high. Samuel holds that the kind of action is irrelevant as long as it is of the kind only a proprietor would take. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 53a" href="/Bava_Batra.53a">Babli, 53a</a>, the attributions are switched.</i>. Samuel said, even if he made a breach at a place where a breach was irrelevant or a fence where a fence was irrelevant, it is legally taking possession.
<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.5.1">Mishnah 5</a> und proves that actually taking possession is necessary in addition to acquiring ownership.</i> As we have stated: If somebody sells a field to another but the buyer was lazy and did not enter into possession when the seller went and legally took possession, the taking possession invalidates the sale or the gift.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Two testify for him that he ate its yield for three years; if they are found perjured they pay everything to him<sup class="footnote-marker">102*</sup><i class="footnote">Since they intended to make the owner lose his property, they have to pay the current value of the property as a fine, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:19" href="/Deuteronomy.19.19"><i>Deut.</i> 19:19</a>.</i>. Two for the first [year], two for the second, two for the third, pay together<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Each witness has to pay 1/6 of the fine.</i>. Three brothers and another joins them; these are three testimonies<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Two relatives cannot testify together. If each brother testifies about a different year and the outsider testifies with each of them, there is valid testimony about three years of claim of undisturbed possession.</i> which count as one testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owner of the land can lose his property only by the total of the testimonies, for the law of perjury the three testimonies are one and each person pays 1/6 of the fine for his testimony, which means that the single outsider pays 3/6 = 1/2 and each brother pays 1/6.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Two testify for him,” etc. One said to another “what are you doing in my field?” “For I have the years of undisturbed possession of it.” He went and brought witnesses that he had the years of undisturbed possession of it. The first one went and proved the witnesses of the second perjured. He has to give him the field and the yield of three years<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owner of the land can lose his property only by the total of the testimonies, for the law of perjury the three testimonies are one and each person pays 1/6 of the fine for his testimony, which means that the single outsider pays 3/6 = 1/2 and each brother pays 1/6.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.3.2"><i>Bava qamma</i> 7:4, Note 46</a>. The fact of perjury, rather than conviction by the court, disqualifies the perjurer; his testimony is nonexistent even if the fact is not realized by the court at the time. Therefore, if the testimony about the first year was perjured, the true testimony about years 2 and 3 becomes void; there is no testimony about three years of undisturbed possession, and nobody has to pay.</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira said, this implies that the perjurer is not disqualified by the court.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following count for a claim of undisturbed possession and the following do not count for a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">For non-agricultural properties where there is no direct equivalent of storing the harvest in one’s silo.</i>. If one put his animals into a courtyard, an oven, a cooking stove, or a grindstone, or raises chickens, or puts his manure in a courtyard, it does not count for a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since each of the co-owners of the courtyard does this, nobody can be assumed to check whether the person doing this really be entitled to it.</i>. But if he made for his animals an enclosure ten [handbreadths] high, or did it for an oven, a cooking stove, or a grindstone, brought chickens into the house, or made a place for his manure three [handbreadths] low or three high: this counts for a claim of undisturbed possession<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Since people will complain if he does not have property rights to do what he is doing.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following count for a claim of undisturbed possession,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said, co-owners usually permit chickens to one another<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">One does not have to ask for the other’s permission to raise chickens in the common courtyard.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, a Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.1.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 5:1 (Note 4</a>): Co-owners who mutually made vows not to have usufruct from one another are forbidden to raise chickens in the common courtyard.</i> says otherwise: “Both of them are forbidden to put up there a grindstone or an oven, or to raise chickens there.” Rebbi Eleazar said, if somebody raised chickens in a courtyard which is not his, it is taking in possession<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">If the owners of the courtyard do not hinder the raising of chickens from the start, later they become claimants and bear the burden of proof to remove the chickens.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this is correct. As you take it, if he has permission to raise chickens there, he raises them. If he does not have permission to raise, it would be legally taking in possession.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The drain pipe does not represent a right of possession; its location represents a right of possession<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">חֲזָקָה here has a third meaning, <i>viz.,</i> a right possessed by one property owner which restricts the property rights of another. In all cases mentioned in the Mishnah, it is presumed that the house was built in conformity with the rules and without protest by its neighbors.<br>The מַזְחֵילָה “leader, drain pipe” is the fixed tube which brings the rainwater from the roof to the ground; מַרְזֵב is a small movable pipe which takes the water coming out of the leader away from the house into the courtyard. (In modern Hebrew, מַזְחֶלֶת is a sled, מַרְזֵב is the leader, drain pipe.) If the leader ends on another owner’s property, this owner may move the direction of the <i>marzeb</i> but may not remove it.</i>. The leader represents a right of possession<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">If the leader was built on the outside of a house which is in the airspace of the adjacent courtyard, it may not be removed by this courtyard’s owners.</i>. An Egyptian ladder<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">A small movable ladder giving access to the upper floor apartment directly from the courtyard.</i> does not represent a right of possession; a Tyrian one<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">A wide wooden ladder permanently attached to the house.</i> represents a right of possession. An Egyptian window does not represent a right of possession; a Tyrian one represents a right of possession<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the adjacent lot may build a wall in front of an Egyptian window and make it useless. But he may not build within 4 cubits of a Tyrian window (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.4.1">Mishnah 2:4</a>). A Tyrian window is defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.14">Tosephta 2:14</a> as being wide enough for a man’s head and having a wooden frame.</i>. What is an Egyptian window? Any where a man’s head cannot enter. Rebbi Jehudah says, if it has a wooden frame it represents a right of possession even if a man’s head cannot enter.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The drain pipe does not represent a right of possession.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, to lengthen and to widen it<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the house does not have the right to lengthen or widen his drain pipe on another’s property. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 58b" href="/Bava_Batra.58b">Babli, 58b</a>, R. Ḥanina holds that the owner of the other property has the right to shorten the drain pipe but not to remove it.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, in all of that direction<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">While the owner of the other property has the right to move the drain pipe, he cannot move its spout away from before the wall to which it belongs.</i>, as we have stated: the leader has a domain of four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">If a drain pipe legally is built to drain a roof, the owner of the house has the right to put up a ladder in front of the pipe to clean and repair it.</i>. You have to say, in all this direction<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Anywhere in front of this roof.</i>. Similarly, the drain tube does not represent a right of possession in all of this direction. It was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta 2:13</a>.</i>: “The location of the drain tube does represent a right of possession, the location of its spout does not represent a right of possession.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text, with different attributions, is in <i>Šabbat</i> 3, 6c l. 56.</i> In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, up to three steps it is like a chair<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">A ladder having no more than three steps goes after the rules of chairs.</i>. Rebbi La said in the name of the House of Rebbi Yannai, this was said referring to impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">A chair which is made to sit on can become impure by a person whose impurity is generated by bodily excretions (such as gonorrhea or menstrual flow) who sits on it even if he never touches it (e. g., if it is completely covered by material not subject to impurity.) A ladder can become impure only by touch or by the impurity of a corpse in a “tent”.</i>. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said in the name of the House of Rebbi Yannai, this was said referring to rights of possession<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">A short ladder, even if built wide like a Tyrian, never can represent rights of possession.</i>. Rebbi Yose in the name of the House of Rebbi Yannai, this was said referring to the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Erubin</i> 7:1 it is stated that two courtyards separated by a wall higher than 10 handbreadths are two separate domains for the rules of the Sabbath. If the wall is higher, it is possible to make the two courtyards into one domain by putting up ladders, even Egyptian ones, if only the remaining height is less than 10 handbreadths. But the ladder has to have at least 4 steps.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar said, they said this<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">It is not too clear to what this refers. The least unlikely suggestion is due to <i>Pene Moshe,</i> where it is referred to the remark in <i>Yebamot</i> 12:3 (Notes 93,94) that one may open an Egyption window looking out on somebody else’s courtyard without asking permission on condition that the opening be higher than 4 cubits above the ground. In general, the rules of privacy do not allow the owner of a house to open windows that give him the possibility to see his neighbors’ activities which before he could not see. R. Hoshaia states that the rule from <i>Yebamot</i> holds for narrow windows opening on a courtyard which cannot be used to observe the courtyard if they are high enough. But if the new window is built in the wall of an upper storey level with the roof of a house bordering on the courtyard, the argument does not apply and the owner of that house can prohibit the opening of the window by claiming “damage by looking”.<br>The damage feared may be from the evil eye; cf. Latin <i>invideo</i> “to look in; to cast an evil eye”, from which <i>invidia, ae</i> “envy, grudge” (E. G.).</i> only for a courtyard. But for roofs<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בְּגַגּוֹת for ms. בגנות “in gardens”, parallel to the reading in the last sentence (which, however, is due to the second hand in L.). Since the error is found both in L and in E, it must be copied from an old source.</i> even higher than four cubits he may prevent him. <sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Text from the second hand in E; it or a similar text are required by the last sentence in the paragraph.</i>[Rebbi Isaac said this before Rebbi Ḥanina, Rebbi Ḥanina said this before Rebbi Phineas bar Ḥama: They said this only for a courtyard, but for roofs<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בְּגַגּוֹת for ms. בגנות “in gardens”, parallel to the reading in the last sentence (which, however, is due to the second hand in L.). Since the error is found both in L and in E, it must be copied from an old source.</i> even higher than four cubits he may prevent him.] Rebbi Hoshaia heard this and was offended. He said, I formulated this teaching but you did not say it in my name: They said this only for a courtyard, but for roofs even higher than four cubits he may prevent him.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A protrusion<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">An extension of the roof protruding outside the wall of the house. If it is built over a neighbor’s courtyard and extends from the roof at least by one handbreadth, the neighbor may forbid it but if it was built without objection it cannot be challenged later. If it extends less, the neighbor cannot object but does not have to take it for granted; he may build a new wall incorporating the molding.</i> down to one handbreadth establishes a right of possession but he can object; less than a handbreadth does not establish a right of possession but he cannot object.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A protrusion down to one handbreadth establishes a right of possession,” etc. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Yannai: If the molding is [up to] one handbreadth wide he may extend it all the way, to draw it all the way<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The inserted word is from E. It seems to mean that if a house was built with a protruding mold along part of the wall with the neighbor’s acquiescence, the protrusion can be extended along the entire length of the wall without requiring a new permit.</i>. Nathan<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Correct in E: R. Nathan in the name of R. Hoshaiah.</i>, Rebbi Hoshaia: About one who divides his house<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this sentence belongs to the next Halakhah, where it is explained that a person may transform a one-family house into a multi-family one as long as he constructs an interior staircase and does not increase the number of doors leading from the house into the courtyard.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person may not open windows into the common courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Once the house is built, nobody may make any changes to his house which infringes on the privacy rights of the co-owners without the explicit agreement of all co-owners. In particular one may not add windows which would enable him to see activities of his neighbors which he could not see without them.</i>. If he bought a house in another courtyard he may not open it into the common courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">If he bought a house whose back wall borders on the condominium courtyard where his other house stands, he may not open a door directly from the common courtyard into that house since the door would acquire rights to the four adjacent cubits to the detriment of the other owners and increase traffic in the courtyard. If he wants to build such a door, he must buy the building rights from his co-owners.</i>. If he built a second floor on top of his house he may not open it into the common courtyard but he may build a room inside his house or a second floor on top of his house on condition that the access to it be from within his house<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this sentence belongs to the next Halakhah, where it is explained that a person may transform a one-family house into a multi-family one as long as he constructs an interior staircase and does not increase the number of doors leading from the house into the courtyard.</i>.<br>In a common courtyard a person may not open a door opposite a door, a window opposite a window. If it was small, he may not enlarge it. If it was one, he may not make it double<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">All these activities allow him to see more of his neighbors’ activities.</i>. But he may open into the public domain a door opposite a door and a window opposite a window. If it was small, he may enlarge it. If it was one, he may make it double<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Since building a door or a window open towards the public domain implies a waiver of privacy rights.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person may not open windows,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The following explanation is based on Rashba, <i>Responsa</i> vol. 5, #106.</i> Here you say, a door opposite a door is permitted, and there you say, a door opposite a door is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah forbids making doors opposite doors in a courtyard but allows them on a public street. It does not say anything about an alley, a narrow dead-end street frequented only by the inhabitants of the courtyards opening into the alley and their visitors, and usually bordered by windowless walls. The status of the alley is not defined in the Mishnah. If opposite doors only be forbidden in a courtyard, they would be allowed in an alley; if they only be allowed in a thoroughfare they would be forbidden in an alley.</i>. Where you say it is permitted, in an alley<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The alley is part of the public domain.</i>. And where you say it is forbidden, in a condominium courtyard. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta 2:15</a>.</i>: “Just as the inhabitants of a courtyard may object to one another, so the inhabitants of an alley may object to one another”? Rebbi La said, here when permission was given, there when permission was not given<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">In principle, R. La accepts the explanation given earlier but he holds that for opening a door directly into the alley one needs the agreement of the other inhabitants of the alley. This agreement, once given, is irrevocable. But in a courtyard any co-owner may retract his assent at any time since according to the Mishnah the door should not have been built in the first place.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is a difference because gardens are usually dug into<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text of L. It does not refer to the question raised in the text but to a later statement in the same Tosephta, that if a brook passes through somebody’s vegetable garden, he may divert its water at will for his irrigation needs. This seems to contradict the entire tenor of the present Chapter which denies the individual the right to infringe upon pre-existing rights of others. R. Joḥanan holds that if the brook was treated as private property, in that the farmer’s lot was taxed as one vegetable garden rather than two properties separated by a river, the owner must be permitted to use his property without restriction.<br>E reads here: שַׁנְייָא בַּגַּגּוֹת שֶׁנִּיתְּנוּ לְהַפְרִיח “there is a difference because <i>roofs</i> are usually <i>made to protrude</i>.” That would refer to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9">Halakhah 9 (Note 133</a>) but probably is a corruption.</i>. Rebbi Nasa said, are ruins not to be rebuilt<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Sometimes the door built later forces a door built earlier to be walled up. If an earlier house collapsed, its right to have a door into the courtyard has not disappeared; no door can be built opposite the future door of a rebuilt house.</i>?
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said that there<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>, one states: If somebody opens a window in the wall of somebody else’s courtyard in the presence of the other (four cubits)<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L, a corruption.</i>, [Rebbi says]<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, accepted as correct.</i> he takes down<sup class="footnote-marker">149*</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps one should read פותח “he opens” instead of פוחת “he takes down”.</i> with his left hand and closes with his right<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">He has to wall it up again faster than he opened it. The wall’s owner retains an unconditional right to demand the walling up of the window.</i>. Think of it, was he not standing there? He may tell him, I wanted to enjoy myself. If he handed him stones<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Helping with building material.</i>? He may tell him, I was making fun of this<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בָּאוּ (vocalization of the ms.) as בְּהַהוּא (S. Lieberman, <i>Tarbiz</i> 5, 1934, pp. 100–101.)</i> man.
If there were five openings one inside another, the measure of all of them is a drill hole. This supports what the builders say, <i>viz.,</i> that a window open above a stone bench<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Which is inconvenient to reach.</i> is made only to let light in. Since it cannot represent a right of possession, it cannot bring impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">This is rather elliptic; it refers to the rules of impurity of the dead, the most important rule of which is that any person or vessels under one “tent” with a corpse becomes impure. If the corpse is in a stoa of which a wall belongs to a house, any window in that wall brings the impurity into the entire house. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 13:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.13.1">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i> 13:1</a> states that if the window was made for light only, the minimal size which makes it a conduit of impurity is the size of the drill-hole of a builder’s drill, which in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:12" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.12"><i>Kelim</i> 17:12</a> is said to be equal to the size of a Neronian tetradrachma or a circular disk of about 25mm diameter. A window made for use to bring vessels to or from the building must be at least one handbreadth square; for people it must let a person crawl through. Since five successive windows of minimal size do not let light in, they are not made for light and do not transmit impurity. Then it is stated that a window which does not transmit impurity is an Egyptian window which cannot represent a right of possession (J. Benvenisti in <i>Śede Yehoshua</i>.)</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One makes no hollow under the public domain, cisterns, ditches, and caves. Rebbi Eliezer permits if a cart filled with stones can pass over it<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Even for private use. A person who owns two parcels separated by a road may connect them by a tunnel on condition that he build a bridge strong enough to carry all expected traffic (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.1.1.7"><i>Bikkurim</i> 1:1, Note 20</a>).</i>. One does not build protrusions and balconies<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐξώστρα, Latin <i>exostra</i> “gallery, lobby, balcony”. The Babylonian spelling is גזוזטרא. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 14:1" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.14.1">Mishnah Ahilut 14:1</a> defines a זיז as protrusion without a railing, גיזרא as one with a railing.</i> above the public domain but if he wishes he builds inside his own property and adds them<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">If the balconies do not extend over his property line, he may build on his property anything he wishes.</i>. If somebody bought a courtyard and there are protrusions and balconies, that remains a right of possession<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer may assume that originally the property extended into what is now public domain and the balconies were built legally; he does not have to investigate their legality.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One makes no hollow,” etc. If they collapsed and he wants to rebuild<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second part of the Mishnah. A person built a balcony above the public domain, i. e., he built inside his property and gave over the ground under the balcony to public use.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he gave up his rights<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Since the strip which he surrendered is now in public use, if the balcony collapses the wall now borders the public domain; the Mishnah prohibits him from rebuilding. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 60b" href="/Bava_Batra.60b">Babli 60b</a> agrees with the characterization of the opinions of R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he did not give up his rights. A Mishnah supports one, a <i>baraita</i> supports the other. A Mishnah supports Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:7</a>.</i>: “If a public road went through somebody’s field and he took it and gave them on the side<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">He ploughed the road under and made a new road on the side of his field. The public has not lost its right of way through his field.</i>. What he gave, he gave, but his own did not come to him.” A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:17" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.17">Tosephta 2:17</a>.</i>: “If somebody bought a courtyard and there are protrusions and balconies, if they collapsed he rebuilds them based on his right of possession.”

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who sells a house<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">“House” here means “apartment, ground floor apartment”, cf. <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 10:1, Note 1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 7:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.7.4.2"><i>Nedarim</i> 7:4, Note 47</a>. This and the next three Chapters spell out the standard conditions of a contract. Any intended deviation from these has to be written into the sales contract.</i> did not sell the shed<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A building ancillary to the main building (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:5" href="/I_Kings.6.5"><i>1K.</i> 6:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:6" href="/I_Kings.6.6">6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:10" href="/I_Kings.6.10">10</a>). According to Maimonides it is a paved space surrounding the building.</i> even if it opened into it, nor the room which is in its interior<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A storage room in the central court.</i>, nor the roof if it has a railing ten handbreadths high<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Then it is accessible to be used (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:8" href="/Deuteronomy.22.8"><i>Deut.</i> 22:8</a>). It may be accessed from outside the house by a ladder.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, if its entry point is shaped like a door it is not sold even if its railing is not ten handbreadths high.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who sells a house,” etc. Rebbi Naḥum in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: Only if it<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A shed not covering at least 16 square cubits is not considered an independent building; it would be included in a contract to sell the adjacent apartment.<br>In measurements, feminine numbers refer to cubits, masculine to handbreadths.</i> is four by four [cubits] and ten [handbreadths] high, and only if it is roofed and waterproofed<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">So it can be used year-round.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira told them, did Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba explain all this to you<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 61a" href="/Bava_Batra.61a">Babli, 61a</a>, Mar Zuṭra only stipulates the first of R. Ḥiyya bar Abba’s four conditions.</i>?
“Nor the roof if it has a railing ten handbreadths high.” Is a railing the last word? If it was surrounded by back walls of houses, if there were pillars and wooden superstructures<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Possibly Latin <i>columnas,</i> acc. pl. of <i>columna</i>, “projecting object, column, pillar, post” (Krauss).</i> on top of them<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If the railing is unnecessary because it is already provided automatically by the surrounding buildings.</i>? Let us hear from the following: “Rebbi Jehudah says, if its entry point is shaped like a door it is not sold even if its railing is not ten handbreadths high.” They said, only if it has a railing ten handbreadths high<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since the anonymous majority rejects R. Jehudah’s rule it follows that for them a usable roof is sold with the house below if and only if it is accessible from the house by a door.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Neither the cistern nor the cellar<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This is a continuation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>. A sale contract for a house which does not mention either the cistern or the cellar, a masonry cavity used for storing wine or grain, does not include these items.</i> even though he wrote over to him “its bottom and height,” and he has to buy himself access<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller wants to use cistern or cellar, he has to buy access from the buyer, the new owner of the surrounding area.</i>, the words of Rebbi Aqiba, but the Sages say that he does not have to buy himself access<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that nobody wants to make a contract which will hurt himself.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba agrees that if he said “except these”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If cistern and/or cellar are mentioned as excluded in the sales contract.</i>, he does not have to buy himself access. If he sold them<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A person sold cistern or cellar to a third party while retaining ownership of his house and courtyard. This part of the Mishnah is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.7.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:7, Notes 112–115</a>.</i> to another person, Rebbi Aqiba said, he does not have to buy access<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In this situation, he adopts the reasoning of the Sages in the preceding case.</i>, but the Sages say, he has to buy access<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that (particularly in an inflationary environment) a person may buy real estate purely as an investment without intention of using it personally. Therefore, nothing is sold which is not spelled out in the contract.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Neither the cistern,” etc. Then why did he write “its bottom and height”? That if he wants to dig down, he may dig down and if he wants to increase its height, he may increase its height<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta 3:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 63b" href="/Bava_Batra.63b">Babli 63b</a>. It is necessary to convey the rights to the ground and the airspace separately. Compare Greek contracts which mention κάτω, ἄνω “downwards, upwards” cf. A. Gulak, <i>Das Urkundenwesen im Talmud im Lichte der griechisch-aegyptischen Papyri und des griechischen und römischen Rechts,</i> Jerusalem 1935 (Hebrew edition 1994 by Ranon Katzoff).</i>. Rav Abba bar Rav Huna in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Aqiba in our version which is following the rabbis in Rebbi Ḥiyya’s version. Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Aqiba in Rebbi Ḥiyya’s version which is following the rabbis in the Babylonians’ version<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The same statements by Rav Huna and Rav Jeremiah are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 64b-65a" href="/Bava_Batra.64b-65a">Babli, 64b/65a</a>, which notes that there are opposite traditions about the possible requirement of providing separate contracts for access. The problem of access is not mentioned in the Tosephta, so it is not clear which version our Mishnah represents.<br>E formulates: “R. Ze‘ura in the name of Rav: Rebbi Aqiba and Rebbi Ḥiyya parallel the Babylonian rabbis.”</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This is a shortened version of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.7.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:7, Notes 111–116</a>. The Mishnah there refers to the case when the access path to a field disappeared in the surrounding property of a single owner while this field’s owner was on a prolonged absence overseas; Admon holds that the owner may choose for himself a short path through the other’s domain while the Sages hold that he has to buy access or fly through the air.</i> There, they said: Admon and Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi La said, they disagree when nothing was specified. Where do we hold? If it is obvious that access was included, everybody agrees that he does not have to pay for access. If it is obvious that access was not included, everybody agrees that he has to pay for access. But we must hold that nothing was specified. Then Rebbi Aqiba said, he does not have to buy access, but the rabbis say, he does have to buy access.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sold a house sold the door<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The door, while movable, is permanently connected to the building. But the key is removable. Anything built-in is sold, anything movable is not sold.</i> but not the key. He sold a built-in mortar but not a movable one. He sold the pine cone but not the enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">These two terms, referring to the grain mill, are explained in Chapter 2, Notes 17,18.</i>. He sold the oven and the cooking stove<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Yerushalmi, Maimonides’s autograph, and the Mishnah mss. in the Maimonides tradition. In the Babli and the Mishnah mss. of the Babylonian tradition: “Neither the oven nor the cooking stove.” As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:1:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.1.2-4">Halakhah 2:1</a>, a (movable) oven or stove may be used inside a house only if it sits on a masonry basis. Therefore it is clear that the bases for oven or stove are sold; oven and stove themselves are not sold. Both versions lead to the same result.</i>. But if he said to him, “it and all it contains,” all is sold.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sold a house,” etc. So is the Mishnah: He sold the hewn mortar but not the affixed one<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The same text is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 65b" href="/Bava_Batra.65b">Babli, 65b</a>.
