There, the commentary ascribed to R. Gershom gives the correct interpretation. A mortar hewn into rock is clearly part of the house and cannot be removed. Any mortar brought from the outside, even if it was permanently affixed to the house, can be removed and therefore is not part of the building which was sold.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sells a courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If a person be able to sell a courtyard, clearly he is the sole proprietor. In common speech, if one refers to “a courtyard” one understands the compound, all the houses accessible from the courtyard.</i> sold houses, cisterns, ditches, and caves but nothing movable. But if he said to him, “it and all it contains,” all is sold. In neither case did he sell the bath house or the olive press standing in it<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since very few courtyards contain either a bathhouse or an olive press, the person acquiring the courtyard will not think of these unless they are mentioned in the contract.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, he who sells a courtyard only sold its airspace<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This means, the area exposed to the sky, nothing built-up, since only the open area is called “courtyard” in exact speech. M. Gil [(1977) 46 תרביץ] reads the sentence as: “He who sells a compound only sells its door.”</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sells a courtyard,” etc. Rebbi Isaac asked: According to the rabbis, he who sells a courtyard without specifications sold houses, cisterns, ditches, and caves but nothing movable. Or maybe he also sold movables<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">He asks how the Mishnah should be understood. Is it to be understood the way it was translated, or does it mean that if he indicated that he was selling the courtyard together with the buildings bordering on it, then by implication he excluded movables, but if he mentioned only the courtyard, everything, including movables, was included? The same question may be asked about the formulation of R. Eliezer’s statement.</i>? Rebbi Isaac asked: According to Rebbi Eliezer, he who sells a courtyard without specifications only sold the airspace. But if he said, “it and all it contains,” he sold houses, cisterns, ditches, and caves but nothing movable. Or maybe he also sold movables? Rebbi Joḥanan said, the following I brought from the House of Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 3:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).3.1">Tosephta 3:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 67a" href="/Bava_Batra.67a">Babli 67a</a>.</i>: “If there were (courtyards)<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L.</i> [stores]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, the Constantinople edition, the Tosephta, and the Babli; required by the context.</i> open to the inside<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Accessible to buyers only from the courtyard.</i>, they are sold; to the outside, they are not sold; to the inside and the outside, they are all sold.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sells an olive-press building sold the lower stone of the olive mill<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">It is called "sea” similar to the “sea” constructed by Solomon in his Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 7:44" href="/I_Kings.7.44"><i>1K.</i> 7:44</a>), shaped like a bowl to collect the oil which will drip from the olives.</i>, the upper stone<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The technical terms in the Mishnah are translated following Maimonides’s Mishnah Commentary.</i>, and the scaffolding for the beam of the press, but he sold neither the planks,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides reads הכידין, the Babli העכירין. The planks cover the olives and are pressed down by the beam moved by the wheel.</i> nor the wheel, nor the beam. But if he said to him, “it and all it contains,” all is sold. Rebbi Eliezer said, he who sells an olive-press building sold the beam<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since without a beam there is no olive press as stated in the Halakhah (and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 67b" href="/Bava_Batra.67b">Babli, 67b</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sells an olive-press,” etc. In fact, if it does not have a beam, it is not called an olive press<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since without a beam there is no olive press as stated in the Halakhah (and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 67b" href="/Bava_Batra.67b">Babli, 67b</a>).</i>. You would say, who stated this? Rebbi Eliezer, since it was stated in the name of Rebbi Eliezer: <sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).3.2">Tosephta 3:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 67b" href="/Bava_Batra.67b">Babli 67b</a>; anonymous in both sources.</i>“He who sells an olive-press building sold the vats, the basket weaves<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Explanation of Rashbam. These are mats strung around the olive press to hold the olives together during the crushing process.</i>, and the breakstones<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 67b" href="/Bava_Batra.67b">Babli, 67b</a>, מפרכת is synonymous with ממל.</i>, and the lower millstone but not the upper one<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Built-in mills are sold, movable ones not.</i>, nor the sacks nor the bags<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μάρσιππος, ὁ, “bag, pouch”, Latin <i>marsupium, marsuppium, -ii.</i></i> it contains. But if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains,…’<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">One has to add: all is sold.</i> In neither case did he sell the paved space surrounding the building<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A building ancillary to the main building (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:5" href="/I_Kings.6.5"><i>1K.</i> 6:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:6" href="/I_Kings.6.6">6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:10" href="/I_Kings.6.10">10</a>). According to Maimonides it is a paved space surrounding the building.</i>, or the [ditches]<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read שיחין for שבוין and החדותין for החרורין, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> (S. Lieberman).</i>, or the [cellars]<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read שיחין for שבוין and החדותין for החרורין, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> (S. Lieberman).</i>.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sells a bathhouse sold neither the planks<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashbam, the planks to walk on in the wet heated sauna. According to Maimonides, planks to deposit one’s clothes.</i> nor the stools<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>subsellium,</i> to sit on in the sauna.</i>, nor the bath utensils<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>balnearia, -orum,</i> “utensils, implements for bathing”.</i>. But if he said to him, “it and all it contains,” all is sold. In neither case did he sell the storage ponds of water or the stores of wood<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">To heat the thermal bath.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sells a bathhouse,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).3.3">Tosephta 3:3</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 67b" href="/Bava_Batra.67b">Babli 67b</a>.</i>: “He who sells a bathhouse sold the inner and the outer rooms<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In the inner rooms, people are naked; in the outer rooms they are dressed.</i>, the basilica<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">A large room supported by pillars.</i>, the heating chamber, and the storekeeper’s<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>horrearius</i> “superintendent of the storehouse, magazine” (E. G.).<br>In the Tosephta: האוליירין<i>olearii</i> “the oil sellers”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.3.6"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:1, Note 94</a>. In the Babli בית הוילאות “room of curtains”. All commentators read the Tosephta into the Yerushalmi.</i> chamber, but not the chests nor the boiler<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">E adds: רִבִּי שִׁמְעוֹן בֶּן אֶלְעָזָר אוֹמֵר מָכַר אֶת הַיּוֹרֶה. “Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, he sold the boiler.”</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, the place of the bath attendant<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>balneator</i> “bathkeeper”, who collects the fee and hands out the bath towels.</i> is sold; the place of the storekeeper is not sold. But if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains’? Even if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains’<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the required formula is different. In the Tosephta, the wording is: But if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains’, <i>all is sold.</i> Even if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains’ he did not sell the water ponds …</i>. In neither case did he sell the water ponds which he uses both in summer and in winter or the room in which wood is stored. But if he said to him, ‘it and all its appurtenances,’ all these are sold.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sells a village sold houses, cisterns, ditches and caverns, bath houses and dovecots, the oil press and irrigated lots but not movables. But if he said to him, it and all that is in it, even if there were animals and slaves there, all of them are sold. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, he who sells a village sold the steward.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sells a village,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta 3:5</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 68b" href="/Bava_Batra.68b">Babli 68b</a>.</i>: “He who sells a village. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, he who sells a village sold the steward but did not sell the administrator<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">A slave trained in bookkeeping is too valuable to be sold as part of a package; cf. <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 9:1, Note 11. (In E: האונקומוס).</i>. But if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains’? Even if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains’<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the required formula is different. In the Tosephta, the wording is: But if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains’, <i>all is sold.</i> Even if he said to him, ‘it and all it contains’ he did not sell the water ponds …</i>. In neither case did he sell <i>ŝirah</i> and its dependencies, and hard rocks hewn from it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Some commentators read with Babli and Tosephta : וְהַחֳרָשִׁים הַמּוּקְצִין מִמֶּנָּה “forests separated from it.”</i>, and preserves <sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>vivarium.</i></i> of wild animals, birds, and fish.” <i>Ŝirah</i> means outlying houses<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The LXX translate <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 15:28" href="/Joshua.15.28"><i>Jos.</i> 15:28</a>: וּבְאֵר שֶׁבַע וּבִזְיוֹתְיָה as καὶ Βηρσαβεε καὶ αἱ κῶμαι αὐτῶν καὶ αἱ ἐπαύλεις αὐτῶν “and Beer Sheba and its walls and its isolated houses.” If וּבִזְיוֹתֶיהָ is taken as an Aramaism, no emendation of the consonantal text is needed.</i>, dependencies are hamlets. Its part in ocean or river<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Ocean beach or fishing rights in the river.</i>, there are Tannaїm who state, it is sold, and there are Tannaїm who state, it is not sold. Rav Ḥisda said, he who says, it is sold, as long as it is within its domain<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">In the formulation of the Babli, whether doors open to beach or river or do not.</i>; he who says, it is not sold, if it is not within its domain.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sells a field sold the stones which are needed for it<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For fencing.</i>, and the posts in a vineyard which are needed for it<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">To support the vines.</i>, and grain standing on the ground, and a reed fence taking up less than a <i>bet rova‘,</i> and a watchman’s hut not made with mortar, and a carob tree not yet grafted, and a virgin sycamore tree<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The last two are young trees whose roots have not yet spread; therefore they are not included if their place was not sold. But an older carob tree grafted for better yield and a sycamore already cut once and now held for the production of logs (an “anvil sycamore”) have large systems of roots spreading wide; they become part of the field.</i>.<br>But he sold neither the stones which are not needed for it, nor the posts in a vineyard which are not needed for it, nor grain harvested from the ground. But if he said to him, “it and all it contains,” all is sold. In no case did he sell either a reed fence taking up more than a <i>bet rova‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">114+ square cubits; the reeds themselves become a cash crop.</i>, or a watchman’s hut made with mortar<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Which is valuable as dwelling by itself.</i>, or a grafted carob tree, or an anvil sycamore tree<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The last two are young trees whose roots have not yet spread; therefore they are not included if their place was not sold. But an older carob tree grafted for better yield and a sycamore already cut once and now held for the production of logs (an “anvil sycamore”) have large systems of roots spreading wide; they become part of the field.</i>.<br>Neither the cistern, nor the wine press, nor a dovecote<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">All of these are not intrinsically connected with a field.</i> whether deserted or intact; and he has to buy himself access<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller wants to use cistern or cellar, he has to buy access from the buyer, the new owner of the surrounding area.</i>, the words of Rebbi Aqiba; but the Sages say that he does not not have to buy himself access<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that nobody wants to make a contract which will hurt himself.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba agrees that if he said “except these”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If cistern and/or cellar are mentioned as excluded in the sales contract.</i>, he does not not have to buy himself access. If he sold them<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A person sold cistern or cellar to a third party while retaining ownership of his house and courtyard. This part of the Mishnah is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:7:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.7.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:7, Notes 112–115</a>.</i> to another person, Rebbi Aqiba said, he does not have to buy access<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In this situation, he adopts the reasoning of the Sages in the preceding case.</i>, but the Sages say, he does have to buy access<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that (particularly in an inflationary environment) a person may buy real estate purely as an investment without intention of using it personally. Therefore, nothing is sold which is not spelled out in the contract.</i>.<br>When has this been said? For the seller. But one who gives a gift, gives everything<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">A gift is supposed to be given in a magnanimous spirit.</i>. Brothers who divided [an inheritance]: if one acquired a field he acquired everything<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Family relations are helped by clean divisions of property.</i>. Somebody taking possession of a proselyte’s property: if he took possession of a field, he took possession of everything<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since the proselyte died without relatives who could have inherited from him, the person who takes possession of his estate has no competitors with whom to share.</i>. If somebody dedicated a field to the Temple, he dedicated everything; Rebbi Simeon says that he who dedicated a field to the Temple only dedicated a grafted carob tree and an anvil sycamore tree<sup class="footnote-marker">64,</sup><i class="footnote">The last two are young trees whose roots have not yet spread; therefore they are not included if their place was not sold. But an older carob tree grafted for better yield and a sycamore already cut once and now held for the production of logs (an “anvil sycamore”) have large systems of roots spreading wide; they become part of the field.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">But nothing not included in the definition of “a field”.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> 8: “He who sells a field,” etc. <b>Halakhah 9:</b> “But he sold neither,” etc. <b><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:8:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.8.2-4">Halakhah 10</a>:</b> “Neither the cistern,” etc. <b>Halakhah 11:</b> “If somebody dedicated a field to the Temple,” etc. In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, poles for vines<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">This explains what is meant by “posts in a vineyard” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.8.1">Mishnah 8</a>; the same explanation is given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 69a" href="/Bava_Batra.69a">Babli, 69a</a>, where Rashbam explains that these poles in French are called <i>palis.</i></i>. The Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya asked, if there were split rings to select?<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is unintelligible. The thrust of the other two questions is very clear. If walls were covered with marble plates or windows framed with wood frames, the covering and the frames would be sold with the house. But what is the status of material prepared for installation which was not yet installed at the time of the sale? R. Ḥiyya seems to ask a similar question relative to agricultural property. חוּליה means “ring”, from the root חלל, חול “to describe a circle”, but it is not clear that חליה is the same as חוליה.The translation in the text follows R. Eliahu Fulda, not to leave the sentence untranslated. He reads חליות מחולקות as “half circles” formed by pliable twigs, such as willow branches, with both ends planted on the ground to support branches of fruit trees heavy with fruit. He also derives the <i>hapax</i> ברות from ברר “to select”. [Starting with the Constantinople edition (1622), all printed editions read לכרות “to cut off”, which replaces an unknown word by one well-known but devoid of sense in the context.] But one could as well read ברות as Arabic بُرَاة “hunter’s hut” and refer the question to the watchman’s hut and ask about the status of such a hut built with split rings formed of any material. One also could read חַלִּיּוֹת מְחוּלָּקוֹת לְבָרוֹת “chopped herbs (خَلٌى) for healing”, whether these have the status of cut grain. Moïse Schwab translates: “S’il y a des morceaux de joncs divisées pour séparer les vignes, sont-ils tous considérés comme nécessaires”; it is difficult to read this into the text.</i> Rebbi Isaac bar Tebelai asked, if there were marble plates<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τάβλα, Latin <i>tabula.</i></i> for walls<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 69a" href="/Bava_Batra.69a">Babli, 69a</a>, a similar question is asked about stones prepared to be used to make a fence around an agricultural property but not yet displayed; it is determined that their status in a sale depends on a difference of opinion between R. Meїr and the anonymous majority.</i>? Rebbi Yudan ben Rebbi Ismael asked: If there were split rods to frame windows<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 69a" href="/Bava_Batra.69a">Babli, 69a</a>, the question is asked by R. Ze‘ira about finished wood frames for windows (slits in the wall without glass) which are not structural but purely ornamental; the question is not answered.</i>?
Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: A vegetable bed which is six by six [handbreadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 69a" href="/Bava_Batra.69a">Babli, 69a</a>, the question is asked by R. Ze‘ira about finished wood frames for windows (slits in the wall without glass) which are not structural but purely ornamental; the question is not answered.</i> and has its own name<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">For example, the name of the spice which usually is grown there such as “the bed of fenugreek”.</i> is not sold<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Unless it is specifically mentioned in a sales contract of agricultural property.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said: There is disagreement between Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose and the rabbis about one who bought an anvil sycamore<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The last two are young trees whose roots have not yet spread; therefore they are not included if their place was not sold. But an older carob tree grafted for better yield and a sycamore already cut once and now held for the production of logs (an “anvil sycamore”) have large systems of roots spreading wide; they become part of the field.</i> on another’s property. Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose says, he acquired the land. The rabbis say, he did not acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.8.3">Note 64</a>, the anvil sycamore spreads its root widely. If the roots of the tree were restricted to the earth on which it stands, it could not live.<br>In practical terms, if the owner of the tree is the owner of the land, he may plant a new tree on its spot if the original tree dies; if he is not the owner of the land, he cannot plant a replacement. In view of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.2.1">Mishnah 5:5</a>, there really should be no problem about a single tree.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba asked: If somebody sold all trees but reserved for himself an anvil sycamore. In the opinion of Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose, he acquired the land<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He did not acquire it but never gave up his ownership.</i>. In the opinion of the rabbis, he did not acquire the land<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">He gave up ownership.</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Tebelai asked: If somebody sold all trees and an olive tree at place X, are all olive trees at that place sold<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If an unspecified tree was sold and the seller had more than one tree at place X, the buyer might lay claim to any one of them. The treatment of the case is in dispute between R. Meïr and R. Yose, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:8:2-10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.8.2-10.2"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:10</a>.</i>? Rebbi Yudan ben Rebbi Ismael asked: If somebody bought three groups of reeds, did he buy the greatest cedar in the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">If he bought reeds that cover more than a <i>bet rova‘,</i> did he acquire only the reeds or everything that grows among them?</i>?
“When has this been said? For the seller. But one who gives a gift gives everything.” What is the difference between a seller and the giver of a gift? Rebbi Abba bar Ṭebelai in the name of Rav: Because the giver of a gift usually is magnanimous<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 71a" href="/Bava_Batra.71a">Babli, 71a</a>, this is the conclusion reached after discussion.</i>. Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because buyers care about minutiae<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since the buyer gives the money, he can be specific and insist to spell out in the contract what he wants, but the recipient of a gift has to accept what he is given. Therefore, a sales contract has to be interpreted exactly but in the case of a gift it is up to the court to guess the donor’s intention.</i>. What is the difference between these opinions? A dedication to the Temple. He who says, because the giver of a gift usually is magnanimous, also the person vowing to the Temple usually is magnanimous. But he who says, because buyers care about minutiae, what does he say about a dedication to the Temple? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">E here has an additional text: נִיִשְׁמְעִינָהּ מֵהָדָא. הָאַחִין שֶׁחָֽלְקוּ זָכוּ בַשָּׂדֶה זָכוּ בְכוּלָּן. רִבִּי לָֽעְזָר שְׁאִיל. עַל כָּל־הַפֶּרֶק הוּשְּׁבָה אוֹ עַל הָרִאשׁוֹנָה הוּשְּׁבָה. “Let us hear from the following: ‘Brothers who divided [an inheritance], if they acquired a field they acquired everything.’ Rebbi Eleazar asked: Does this refer to the entire Chapter or only to the first mention?”</i>: “Brothers who divided [an inheritance], if one acquired a field he acquired everything. Somebody taking possession of the property of a convert aquired it; if he took possession of a field, he took possession of everything. If somebody dedicated a field to the Temple, he dedicated everything. Rebbi Simeon says that he who dedicated a field to the Temple, only dedicated a grafted carob tree and an old sycamore tree.” Because they nurse from Temple property<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 72b" href="/Bava_Batra.72b">Babli 72b</a>. He holds that a gift to the Temple has to be interpreted narrowly, like a sale. The exeption are trees with widespread roots which must be included, even against the giver’s wish, because of the severity of the sin of larceny from Temple property which would be committed if the donor profited from growth of the tree by the nutrients which its roots absorb from Temple property.</i>. Because he reserved access to himself? If he had not reserved access for himself, would they not feed from Temple property<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The brother whose inheritance is accessible only through another brother’s property has the right of passage automatically, without specifically insisting on it at the time of the distribution, just as the anvil sycamore is part of Temple property even if the donor to the Temple does not specify it. Since the majority disagree with R. Simeon, they must hold that the donor to the Temple also is magnanimous.</i>?

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sells a ship sold the mast, and the sail, and the anchors, and all steering gear, but he neither sold the slaves, nor the storage bags<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.5.2">Chapter 4, Note 41</a>. Containers in which the load is stored.</i>, nor the load<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἐνθήκη.</i>. If he said, “it and all that is in it,” all is sold.<br>He who sold a truck<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κάρρος, κάρρον, Latin <i>carrus, carrum</i> “two-wheeled cart”.</i> did not sell the mules; he who sold the mules did not sell the truck. He who sold the yoke did not sell the cattle<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For ploughing.</i>; he who sold the cattle did not sell the yoke. Rebbi Jehudah says, the price is an indicator. How is that? If he said to him, sell me your yoke and harness for 200 denars<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In his time a sum whose possession disqualified one from receiving public assistance; Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 8:8.</i>, it is obvious that yoke and harness are not worth 200 denars. But the Sages say, the price is no indicator.<br>He who sells a donkey did not sell its gear<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The gear needed to fasten the animal.</i>. Nahum the Mede says, he sold the gear. Rebbi Jehudah says, sometimes it is sold, sometimes it is not sold. How is this? If a donkey was standing there with his gear on it and he said, sell me <i>this</i> donkey, his gear is sold; your donkey, for this one the gear is not sold.<br>He who sells a donkey sold the foal<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the foal is dependent on its mother it cannot be separated from her.</i>; he who sold a cow did not sell its young<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since a calf can be slaughtered, it represents an independent value.</i>. He who sold a dungheap sold the manure; he who sold a cistern sold its water. He who sold a beehive sold the bees; he who sold the dovecote sold the pigeons. He who bought the yield of a dovecote leaves the first brood to fledge, of a beehive takes three swarms and then intermittently<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In order to preserve the population he takes only the second and all the odd-numbered swarms.</i>, honeycombs leaves two honeycombs<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For the bees to survive the winter.</i>, olive trees to cut down leaves two handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Interpretation of Maimonides. One might translate “two twigs” but it is difficult to see how the tree could be cut down and two twigs be left standing. But on a trunk extending two handbreadths above the ground a new olive tree can be grafted.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sells a ship.” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 4:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).4.1">Tosephta 4:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 73a" href="/Bava_Batra.73a">Babli 73a</a>.</i>: He who sells a ship sold the ladder<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>scalae, -arum, f.;</i> to enter the ship.</i> and the water tank in it but did not sell the sleeping gear, nor the mats, nor the barge<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σκάφος “ship; hull of a ship”.</i> nor the rowboat<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A small boat used to reach the shore where there was no secure harbor. According to Rava in the Babli (<i>l. c.</i>), rowboat and fishing boat are one and the same, except that בוצית is Babylonian and דוגית Palestinian usage..</i>. Symmachos says, he sold the fishing boat.
<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This is a shortened reformulation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.3.4">Halakhah <i>Kilaim</i> 2:5, Notes 73–89</a>. The conditions of land use attributed to Joshua are discussed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 80b-82a" href="/Bava_Kamma.80b-82a">Babli in <i>Bava qamma</i> 80b–82a</a>. Since the distribution of land by Joshua was irrelevant for the Second commonwealth, a reference to “conditions of Joshua” is simply one to common law. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).8">Tosephta <i>Bava qamma</i> Chapter 8</a>, Joshua is not mentioned.</i> The conditions which Joshua imposed. Rebbi Levi ben Birai in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: four. One may collect grasses<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As animal feed.</i> everywhere except from a field of fenugreek where it is forbidden because of robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Fenugreek stems are very thin and valuable; it is impossible to tear out weeds without also damaging some stems, which inflicts a loss on the owner of the field. If the owner harvests his field, he might use the weeds to tie bundles of fenugreek for retail sale.</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 2:5</a>.</i>, we have stated: “Or fenugreek where grasses appeared, one does not require him to weed.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid two different kinds growing on the field, which would be sinful.</i>” So is the Mishnah: One does not require him to uproot. As one of Joshua’s conditions you say, one may collect grasses everywhere except from a field of fenugreek where it is forbidden because of robbery. This implies that he does not want them. He said, there if he sowed it to make sheaves, but here when he sowed it for seeds<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">If the fenugreek seeds are sold not as spice but as seeds, they are not sold on the stalk and, therefore, nothing is needed to bind the stalks; the weeds are unwanted in every respect and leaving them is not trespasssing the prohibition of <i>kilaim.</i></i>. Did Joshua made conditions for the benefit of sinners<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since he forbade taking weeds from fenugreek even if they caused <i>kilaim.</i></i>? Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: He would like it if they were uprooted and lying before him<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">No farmer likes weeds in his field. The removal of weeds from fenugreek is impossible as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.1.3">Note 18</a>.</i>. And he mentions a vineyard field, as Rebbi Yannai<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Kilaim:</i> R. Zeriqan in the name of the House of R. Yannai.</i> said: all spontaneous growth<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In a Sabbaticall year; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:5" href="/Leviticus.25.5"><i>Lev.</i>25:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:11" href="/Leviticus.25.11">11</a>.</i> is forbidden except for what appears on a fallow field, or a field ready to be ploughed under, a vineyard or a field grown for seeds<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The last category is not mentioned in <i>Kilaim.</i></i>. A fallow field, for he does not care. A field ready to be ploughed under, for he wants to improve his field<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The ploughed-under aftergrowth will act as fertilizer.</i>. A vineyard, not to forbid his vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since produce growing in a vineyard will condemn the entire vineyard (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Kilaim</i>.)</i>. A field grown for seeds, for he does not want it; and if you say he does, he would like it if they were uprooted and lying before him.
<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel but quite different treatment of this subject is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.3.5"><i>Nazir</i> 9:3, Notes 119–134</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 81a" href="/Bava_Kamma.81a">Babli (<i>Bava qamma</i> 81a</a>) notes that one of Joshua’s stipulations was that a “corpse of obligation”, an unattended corpse found far from any organized settlement, must be buried on the spot it was found.</i> There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.3.1">Mishnah <i>Nazir</i> 9:3</a>.</i>: “If somebody find a corpse in original position, lying as usual<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">With his limbs stretched out, not in a compressed position which would characterize the corpse as Gentile.</i>, he takes him and his surroundings.” Rav Ḥisda said, this implies that it is permitted to remove a corpse of obligation, since we have stated: “he takes him<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">An unqualified statement.</i> and his surroundings.” And how much? Rebbi Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">He is R. Samuel ben Rav Naḥman.</i> in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Up to three finger-breadths, the place where fluid penetrates. Rebbi Ze‘ira says, this is not reasonable; it is forbidden to remove a corpse of obligation. If you would say, it is permitted to remove him, since you are going to ruin the whole field<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">By digging a rather deep hole in the middle of somebody else’s field.</i>, would it be permitted to remove him? Since it is obvious for the Tanna that it is forbidden to remove a corpse of obligation, therefore, one needs the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah tells one to remove the corpse, it must be done with minimal disturbance of the field, which cannot refer to a corpse of obligation.</i>. Rav Ḥisda said, it follows him who said, it is permitted to remove a corpse of obligation; I am saying that he was buried on a permitted path<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">A “permitted path”, also mentioned in the next condition imposed by Joshua, is a path through a grain field between harvest and the early rains, when the field is empty and traditionally everybody has permission to walk through the field. R. Ḥisda now holds that the Mishnah does not prove anything about a corpse of obligation; it could have been that a person was buried there during the time of permission with the intention of removing it later to a cemetary, but that something then intervened; the people knowing about the corpse died, and the provisional grave was forgotten.</i>. But whould one not have to worry that maybe he <i>was</i> a corpse of obligation? Corpses of obligation are infrequent<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Practically they never happen; the Mishnah certainly is not formulated for them.</i>.
One walks on permitted paths<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">A “permitted path”, also mentioned in the next condition imposed by Joshua, is a path through a grain field between harvest and the early rains, when the field is empty and traditionally everybody has permission to walk through the field. R. Ḥisda now holds that the Mishnah does not prove anything about a corpse of obligation; it could have been that a person was buried there during the time of permission with the intention of removing it later to a cemetary, but that something then intervened; the people knowing about the corpse died, and the provisional grave was forgotten.</i> from the moment the harvest is cleared up to the second rainfall<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">When the ploughing season starts (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 6b" href="/Taanit.6b">Babli <i>Ta‘anit</i> 6b</a>).</i>, and one may take shoots to plant from everywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">To start new trees.</i> except from olive trees and vines. From olives trees from the very young and the very old<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Which do not bear olives. Cutting a twig of such a tree does not cause a loss to the tree’s owner.</i>, but if it was like a <i>ṭrgwl</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">One might read פְרַגוֹל “whip”, Latin <i>flagellum,</i> for טרגול (Krauss). Possibly טרגול is forbidden as looking like something used at the Athenian festival of the <i>Thargelia,</i> at which first fruits and humans were sacrificed (E. G.)?</i> even from the very young it is forbidden.
<sup class="footnote-marker">42a</sup><i class="footnote">This is an alternative version of the conditions imposed by Joshua; cf. Note 16.</i> Rebbi Tanḥum from Kefar Gun<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">He is R. Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya from Kefar Agin (Umm Jumia S. of Lake Genezareth), a third generation Amora.</i> in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose: Four. One collects grasses from everywhere on condition that one not uproot. And one relieves himself behind a wall.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In an agricultural area, even if the field is sown up to the wall; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 81a" href="/Bava_Kamma.81a">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 81a</a>.</i> Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Tanḥum<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The names here have to be switched; the last generation Tanna Eleazar ben R. Yose cannot speak in the name of the third generation Amora R. Tanḥum.</i>: Up to a place where he lets wind and is not heard<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">From the road.</i>. And one grazes in forests, even the tribe of Jehudah in the tribe of Naftali, and one gives Naftali an entire region South of the lake, as it is said, “lake and South of it he inherits,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:23" href="/Deuteronomy.33.23"><i>Deut.</i> 33:23</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 8:18" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).8.18">Tosephta <i>Bava qamma</i> 8:18</a>, <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 355, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 81b" href="/Bava_Kamma.81b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 81b</a>. Since the territory of Naftali was the North-East corner of the Land of Israel, Joshua could not have understood the verse to mean: “West and South he inherited.” The location of Naftali therefore is validly discussed among the rules attributed to Joshua.</i>” the words of Rebbi Yose the Galilean. Rebbi Aqiba says, “lake” is Lake Samokhonites<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Lake Huleh.</i>, “and South” is Lake Tiberias. <sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid obstacles on the road, mainly in the rainy season on an unimproved road: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.9.3"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:9, Notes 347–353</a>.</i> And one may step to one side. Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: Even into a field full of saffron. How? Does one have to pay or not? Since Rav Jehudah said, even into a field full of saffron, that means, one pays<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L. E: “one does <i>not</i> pay.” The next sentence shows that the reading of L is correct. The argument here is that saffron is so valuable that any stepping into the field causes considerable damage to the farmer; it is unconscionable that he should not be indemnified.</i>. Rebbi La said, since Rav Jehudah said, even into a field full of saffron, that means, one does not pay<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">If it is a right in common law, it cannot be disputed or subjected to payment. He holds that a very valuable crop should never be planted adjacent to a public road without leaving an ample shoulder at the side of the road.</i>. But one cannot deviate more than necessary. Let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid obstacles on the road, mainly in the rainy season on an unimproved road: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.9.3"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:9, Notes 347–353</a>.</i> Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Joshua were on the road [and stepped aside because of obstacles on the road]<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">From E, supported by the text in <i>Berakhot.</i></i> when they saw Jehudah ben Pappos, who was sinking, coming towards them. Rabban Gamliël said to Rebbi Joshua: Who is this one who makes an exhibition of himself? He answered: This is Jehudah ben Pappos, all whose deeds are done for Heaven’s sake.
Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: If one wants to build a new village, one gives him<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The district administration is required to build roads to connect the village to the existing network of roads.</i> four roads to the four directions of the compass. Rebbi Ḥanina asked before Rebbi Mana, how? From four cubits to eight<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A private road (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:7</a>).</i> or from eight to sixteen<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A highway. A road 16 cubits wide is the definition of “public domain” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:7</a>).</i>? He told him, from eight to sixteen so that one truck may leave and the other come.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who buys two trees on another’s property did not acquire the land<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">On which the trees stand.</i>; Rebbi Meїr says, he acquired the land. If they grew, he need not cut them<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Anything that grows from the tree, neither new shoots nor expanding crowns which extend beyond the range they had at the time of sale.</i>. What grows from the stem is his, from the roots<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">A new tree coming out of the earth.</i> is the owner’s of the ground. If they died, he has no land<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.8.3">Chapter 4, Note 79</a>.</i>.<br>If he bought three, he bought the land. If they grew, he has to cut them<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Anything which will grow into the original owner’s land is no different from trees growing in one orchard and overhanging another owner’s property (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:11:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.11.2-5">Halakhah 2:14</a>).</i>. What grows from the roots is his. If they died, he has the land.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who buys two trees on another’s property,” etc. (The words)<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L.</i> [In the House of]<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E.</i> Rebbi Yannai they said, what sees the shadow is root, what sees the sun is stem<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 82a" href="/Bava_Batra.82a">Babli 82a</a>, in the name of R. Joḥanan. The question is, what is meant by “growth from the roots”? The entire tree grows from the roots. On the other hand, unless lifted by man, most roots will feed just one stem. Where does one draw the line between stem and roots at the bottom of the stem?</i>. Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose: What grows from its roots and its stem is root, from its stem but not its roots is tree<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Anything grown as a branch is the buyer’s; anything else is the landlord’s.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 82a" href="/Bava_Batra.82a">Babli 82a</a>. It is understood that this only applies to trees which qualify for an orchard; i. e. that the distance between two trees be no larger than √250 = 15.81 cubits (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.1.2.2"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 1:2, Note 18</a>).</i>, he who buys three trees buys the land in between them, under them, and outside of them the width of the harvester and his bag<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Four cubits around each tree (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.10.1">Chapter 2, Note 101</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.1.2.2"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 1:2 Note 19</a>)</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, while he has no access<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Unless the trees are directly accessible from the road, buying the trees does not buy access. (But the person who only buys two trees rents the land and has free access as a renter; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 82b" href="/Bava_Batra.82b">Babli 82b</a>.)</i>, he has the width of the harvester and his bag outside of them.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sells the head of a large animal<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Cattle.</i> did not sell the feet; if he sold the feet he did not sell the head. If he sold the windpipe<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">With the lungs attached. {The use of קָנֶה “stick” for “reed, windpipe” might be induced by Latin <i>canalis animae</i> “windpipe” (Pliny) (E. G.)}</i> he did not sell the liver; if he sold the liver he did not sell the windpipe. But for a small animal<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Sheep or goats.</i>, if he sold the head he sold the feet<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the feet do not fetch much, it was customary to give them as a bonus to the buyer of the head. The upper part of the right leg had to be given to the Cohen in any case (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:3" href="/Deuteronomy.18.3"><i>Deut.</i>18:3</a>).</i>; if he sold the feet he did not sell the head<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The cheaper item is a gratuity for the more expensive one but not vice-versa.</i>. If he sold the windpipe he sold the liver; if he sold the liver he did not sell the windpipe.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sells the head of a large animal,” etc. Rebbi Isaac asked: If he sold half of the head, did he sell half of the legs? If he sold half of the windpipe, did he sell half of the liver? [If he sold half of the liver,]<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, missing in L, required by the context.</i> did he sell half of the windpipe? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, missing in L, required by the context.</i>: “If he sold him the entire foot, the entire head, the entire intestines, he gives to the Cohen and does not deduct from the price<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chullin 9:2" href="/Tosefta_Chullin.9.2">Tosephta <i>Ḥulin</i> 9:8–9</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 132a" href="/Chullin.132a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 132a</a>.</i>. If he bought by weight, he gives to the Cohen and deducts from the price.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If parts of the animal were separately sold by the piece, not by weight, the price is a lump sum for what is usable for the buyer. Therefore, if some of the obligatory gifts to the Cohen (the jaw, the upper leg, and the first stomach) were parts of the animal sold, the price was never intended to cover these gifts; nothing can be deducted.</i>”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Four situations for sellers. If one contracted to sell high quality wheat and it turned out to be low quality, the buyer may annul the contract. Low quality and it turned out to be high quality, the seller may annul the contract. Low quality and it turned out to be low quality, high quality and it turned out to be high quality, neither of them may annul the contract<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller is unable to fill the contract as written, the party which would be disadvantaged can annul the contract without penalty or censure.</i>. Brown grain and it turned out to be white grain, white grain and it turned out to be brown grain<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">White grain is better quality than brown; the former is human food, the latter animal feed. If the delivery does not satisfy the buter’s needs, he need not accept delivery.</i>, olive wood and it turned out to be sycamore wood, sycamore wood and it turned out to be olive wood<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Olive wood is more valuable than sycamore wood, but olive wood is for furniture and sycamore wood for building.</i>, wine and it turned out to be vinegar, vinegar and it turned out to be wine, either of them may annul the contract.<br>If somebody sold produce to another and this one moved it without measuring, he took possession<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Moving merchandise after taking title always gives possession, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a>; <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 4:2.</i>. If he was measuring without moving, he did not take possession. If he is intelligent, he rents its place<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">If he is worried whether the seller may annul the sale, since real estate always conveys possession of what was acquired by its owner, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a>.</i>. If somebody buys flax from another he he did not take possession until he moved it from place to place;<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If he starts moving it, i. e., lifting it.</i> but if it still was standing on the ground and he plucked anything, he took possession.<br>If somebody sold wine or oil to another<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">And the contract specified that it was sold at the market price on delivery.</i> and it rose or fell in price, if it was before the measure was filled it is for the seller, after the measure was filled for the buyer. If a broker was involved<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Who transports the amphora from the seller to the buyer. A standard contract implies delivery f.o.b.</i> and the amphora broke, it broke for the broker. He<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If less than an entire barrel is sold, the seller has to empty the measuring vessel into the buyer’s vessel and after the flow has stopped has to wait until three single drops drip into the buyer’s vessel. The fluid which then still clings to the seller’s vessel remains his property.</i> is obligated to let it drip three drops. If he tilted and emptied it, [the remainder] is for the seller. The grocer does not have to let it drip three drops<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">If he has many clients and the time spent in waiting for the three drops would seriously interfere with his business. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.3.8.1">Mishnah <i>Yom Tov</i> 3:8</a> reports that a grocer who was a holy man never emptied measuring cups into his client’s vessel but gave them his measuring vessel so they could pour themselves and take all the time to empty the vessel to the last drop.)</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, Friday evenings close to sundown he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The retail grocer is required to wait until three drops drip exept for Fridays late in the afternoon when everybody is trying to get home quickly for the Sabbath.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Four situations for sellers.,” etc. How is this? If the measuring vessel belonged to the seller, he has the property rights, if to the buyer, he has the property rights<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.4.1">Mishnah 10</a>. Why should it make any difference when the measuring vessel was full? As long as the fluid is in the seller’s vessel it is not delivered.</i>. Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This has to be read here.</i> Jehudah in the name of Samuel, Rebbi La<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 97a" href="/Bava_Batra.97a">Babli, 97a</a>, he identifies the third person as the broker. The passage is missing in E.</i> in the name of the Great Rebbi Jehudah, so is the Mishnah: If the measuring vessel belonged to a third person. It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah says, Friday evenings close to sundown he is not liable since he has permission, but the Sages say, in any case he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> contradicts the Mishnah which frees the retail grocer from the obligation to wait for three drops.</i>. What is the reason for what Rebbi Jehudah said? “If he is hired, it is paid for by his wages.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:14" href="/Exodus.22.14"><i>Ex.</i> 22:14</a>. The quote has nothing to do with the preceding <i>baraita</i> but refers to the sentence in the Mishnah which holds the broker responsible for the delivery; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta 5:2</a> this is R. Jehudah’s opinion. The verse quoted states that if a hired animal dies, the fee paid for it represents indemnification. The fee paid to the broker is indemnification for the risk which he assumes. Pseudo-Jonathan translates the verse: “If he works for a fee, his loss is covered by his fee.”</i>”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody sent his son to a grocer who measured him oil for an <i>assarius</i> and he gave him the <i>assarius</i>; if he<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The child. The majority hold that if a child is sent to the grocer, the sender only intends to give the grocer a message that he should deliver the goods to the sender’s house, not give them to the child.</i> broke the flask and lost the <i>assarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to see where the coin comes in. Rashbam reports that his grandfather Rashi changed the text of the Mishnah to the effect that the child was sent with a <i>dupondius,</i> to get oil for an <i>assarius</i> and an <i>assarius</i> in change. But the ms. evidence and Maimonides’s text do not support the emendation.</i>, the grocer is liable. Rebbi Jehudah holds him not liable since for that he sent him<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 5:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).5.2">Tosephta, 5:2</a>, R. Jehudah explains that anybody sending money and a breakable vessel by a child expects the money to be lost and the vessel broken.</i>. The Sages agree with Rebbi Jehudah that if the flask was in the child’s hand and the grocer measured into the flask, the grocer is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If he never took the vessel into his hand, he never became responsible.</i>.<br>The wolesaler cleans his measuring vessels once every thirty days, the householder every twelve months. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, it is the other way around<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the wholesaler uses his measuring vessels all the time; nothing will stick and, since each vessel is always used for the same commodity, no customer is disadvantaged. But the householder who only sells sporadically must clean his vessels lest some leftover fluid clot and diminish the volume.</i>. The retail grocer cleans his measuring vessels twice a week, wipes his weights clean once a week, and cleans his scales after each weighing.<br>Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, when has this been said? For fluids, but for dry matter it is not necessary; he has to let [the scales] bend down a handbreadth<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The scales holding the merchandise have to be visibly lower than the weights.</i>. If he weighed strictly by eyesight<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The lever of the balance being exactly parallel to the ground.</i> he has to give him surplus, one tenth for fluids and one twentieth for dry products<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Which method is chosen will depend on local usage.</i>.<br>At a place where one is used to small measures he should not use large ones, to large measures he should not use small ones, to level one must not heap, to heap one must not level.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody sent his son to a grocer,” etc. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, for the tenths he has to let bend down a handbreadth<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 88b" href="/Bava_Batra.88b">Babli, 88b</a>, R. Abba bar Mamal explains that the bending down of the scales should correspond to 1/10 of a pound for every 10 pounds, or 1%.</i>. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:36" href="/Leviticus.19.36"><i>Lev.</i> 19:36</a>.</i>: “Fair scales, fair weights.” From here<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">It is not from here but from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:15" href="/Deuteronomy.25.15"><i>Deut.</i> 25:15</a>: “A full and fair weightstone you shall have, a full and fair <i>ephah</i> you shall have, that your days be prolonged on the land which the Eternal, your God, gives to you.”</i> the Sages said that any commandment whose reward is noted, the court is not warned about. “You shall have,<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:15" href="/Deuteronomy.25.15"><i>Deut.</i> 25:15</a>; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:36" href="/Leviticus.19.36"><i>Lev.</i> 19:36</a> the plural is used, יִהְיֶה לָכֶם. It is a generally accepted talmudic principle of interpretation that a commandment formulated in the plural is addressed to every individual. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:36" href="/Leviticus.19.36"><i>Lev.</i> 19:36</a>, the verse ends: I am the Eternal, your God, I Who took you out from the land of Egypt, on which <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Chapter 8 10" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Chapter_8.10"><i>Sifra Qedošim Pereq</i>8(10</a>) notes: “On condition that you accept the obligation of fair measures, for every person who accepts the obligation of fair measures confirms the Exodus, and everyone who violates the obligation of fair measures negates the Exodus.” But commandments in the singular are considered as commandments in the collective, addressed to the community.</i>” appoint market overseers<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀγορανόμος. In <i>Demay</i>2:1 (23c l. 26, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.1.3">Note 22</a>) the transliteration is אגורנימום.</i> over this<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 89a" href="/Bava_Batra.89a">Babli 89a</a>, <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 294. This inference clearly contradicts the prior statement that oversight over measures is not a public duty.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, so is the <i>baraita</i>: Any commandment whose reward is noted the court is not punished about. Rav was appointed market overseer by the Head of the Diaspora<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Over Jewish markets in Babylonia.</i>; he intervened about measuring vessels but not prices<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">In the Roman Empire, price control was introduced by Diocletian. But Rav was trained in Galilee under the Severans, almost a century before Diocletian. In <i>Demay</i> also, control of prices is described as an anomaly.</i>. The Head of the Diaspora jailed him. Rav Qarna went to see him and said, the market overseer about whom they spoke was for measures but not prices. He answered, but you had stated: the market overseer is for both measures and prices<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 89a" href="/Bava_Batra.89a">Babli, 89a</a>, it is reported that Qarna taught this against the explicit instructions of Samuel; and that in consequence a horn grew on his forehead for which he was called Qarna.</i>. He said to him, go out and tell them, the market overseer is for measures but not prices. He went out and said to them, a person who teaches hidden things they send to jail!
At a place where one is used to small measures he should not use large ones, to large measures he should not use small ones, to level one must not heap, to heap one must not level.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sells grain to another and it did not sprout, or even flax seed, he is not responsible for it<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since he may say that he sold it as food.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, for garden seeds which are not edible he is responsible<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The prior argument does not apply.</i>. If somebody sells produce<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Wholesale.</i> to another he has to accept a quarter [<i>qab</i>] of chaff per <i>se’ah</i>; for figs he has to accept ten worm-bitten ones per hundred; with a wine cellar he has to accept ten sour ones per hundred; with vessels in the Sharon<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Local product. <i>Sharon</i> does not necessarily refer to the Palestinian plain, Latin <i>sarannus</i> may mean “Tyrian” or “Karthaginean”.</i> he has to accept ten <i>pitassaot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4*</sup><i class="footnote">In several Tosephtot (<i>Kelim Bava qamma</i> 4:16, <i>Ahilut</i> 10:3,15:11) פיטס means πίθος, “wine barrel, large vessel.” This is the equivalent of Hebrew קַנְקָן; it is not relevant for the Mishnah.<br>Rabbenu Ḥananel explains the word פיטסאות here as a noun, קנקנים דשחלי “junk barrels”. Since the Yerushalmi does not explain the Mishnah, one must have recourse to the Babylonian sources. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 6:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).6.4">Tosephta 6:4</a> supports Rabbenu Hananel’s explanation of פיטסאות as “junk”: “If somebody buys amphoras and they turn out to be פיטסות and broke, the seller has to return the price of the amphoras but not the value of the wine.” The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 97b" href="/Bava_Batra.97b">Babli, 97b</a>, is somewhat ambiguous: תאנא פיטסות נאות ומגופרות “It was stated: <i>pitassot</i> half-baked and sulphured.” It is not clear whether פיטסות is a noun or an adjective. An addition in the Munich ms. of the Babli, referred to by Rashbam, explains that instead of being fired in a kiln, the inner walls of the clay vessel were lined with sulphur, which then was lit, burned off, and gave the impression of a finished vessel while it was only superficially baked.<br>The three sources of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 6:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).6.3">Tosephta 6:3</a> have different texts:<br>Vienna ms.: קנקנים מקבל עליו עשר נאות פיטסות מגופרות למאה<br>Erfurt ms.: למאה קנקנים מקבל עליו עשר פוטסות נאות מגופרות<br>Editio princeps: קנקנים מקבל עליו עשר נאות פינוסות פוניסות מגופרות למאה<br>If the ms. before the Venice printer was none too clear, it was easy to misread ט as נו; therefore the <i>editio princeps</i> may count as confirmation of the Vienna text which clearly defines פיטסות as an adjective: “Barrels, he has to accept ten half-baked, <i>pytswt,</i> sulphured per hundred.” S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta kiFshutah Bava batra</i> p. 396) explains פיטסאות as Greek πισσωταί “pitched”. Since Tosephta <i>Abodah zarah</i> 8:1 uses standard Hebrew זפות for “pitched”, there is no reason why a Greek word should be used here. A clay vessel used for storage of fluids must be waterproofed; good vessels also are pitched. Therefore, פיטס is a word of unknown etymology, an adjective which denotes inferior quality which upon close inspection could have been detected. {But also cf. Greek ἐπιτάσσω “put upon, enjoin; place beside, order”; ἐπίταξις “injunction, assessment”. An <i>imposition</i> of 10% amphoras of lesser quality? (E. G.)}</i> per hundred.<br>If somebody sells wine to another and it turned into vinegar, he is not responsible for it. But if it was known that his wine turns into vinegar, it is a buy in error<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The seller would have had to warn the buyer that the wine was only for immediate use; if he failed to do so, the buyer might return the merchandise for a full refund.</i>. If he told him, I am selling you spice wine, it has to keep until Pentecost<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The wine has to keep from the harvest in the fall to the next Pentecost.</i>. “Old” is from the year before, “aged” from three years<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">These are common trade terms.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody sells grain to another,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 9:16.</i>: “If somebody sells grain to another and it did not sprout, even flax seed, he is not responsible for it. But if he made it conditional from the start as seeds<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If the grain was sold as seed grain, rather than food.</i>, he is liable. What does he pay him? The value of the seeds. Some say, he gives him his expenses<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">All expenses to prepare the field for sowing; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 93b" href="/Bava_Batra.93b">Babli <i>Bava batra</i> 93b</a>.</i>.”
Here, he has to accept it, but there<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1.1">Mishnah <i>Kilaim</i> 2:1</a>. While in commercial transactions 1/24 of contamination is permitted and only more than 1/24 is cause for rejection, for seed grain a contamination with 1/24 of another kind of seeds must be rejected and only less than 1/24 is acceptable.</i> it should be diminished. Here only chaff, but there another kind. Here only the way he touched it, but there only the way he carried it<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1.5"><i>Kilaim</i> 2:1, Notes 20–24</a>, that the contamination with other seeds occurs during transport of the harvest to storage. But the chaff found in grain comes from the winnowing process, when the grain is touched during threshing.</i>. Here Rebbi Simeon agrees, but there, Rebbi Simeon says they are two kinds and do not count together<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In commercial transactions, anything which is not of the kind contracted for is a contamination; they all add together up to the statutory limit of 1/24. But in matters of illicit mixing of seeds, R. Simeon counts each kind as a separate contamination.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba asked: One <i>se’ah</i> of wheat which fell into one hundred of barley, do the Cohen and the Israel split the increase in value? Rebbi Isaac bar Tebelai asked: One <i>se’ah</i> of heave which fell into one hundred of profane grain, do the seller and the buyer split the remainder<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that in the first question, one has to read “seller and buyer” and in the second “Cohen and Israel”. The first question is about a sale of grain. If some of the more valuable wheat fell into a large quantity of barley, the value of the barley is increased. Who benefits from that increase? It naturally is balanced by the loss incurred by the owner of the wheat which now is contaminated with barley.<br>The second question is about heave, which is forbidden to everybody except a Cohen in a state of purity. If one part of heave falls into more than 100 parts of profane grain, everything becomes profane but 1 part of the profane mixture has to be separated and given to a Cohen (Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 4:8). But if there were only 100 parts it is <i>dema‘</i> and the entire mixture must be sold to a Cohen to be eaten in purity. Here then it is a question of repartition of the loss.</i>? Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 93b-94a" href="/Bava_Batra.93b-94a">Babli 93b/94a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1.3"><i>Kilaim</i> 2:1, Note 14</a>. Since the farmer could have sold the contaminated grain, the person who cleaned it caused him monetary loss. As a fine, the person responsible is not permitted to put the contaminants back. As application to the questions asked, the person responsible for the mess-up is responsible for its financial consequences.</i>: He who takes out impurities from his neighbor’s heap has to give him good grain in their stead.
Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said, if he said, collect and acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement in the Mishnah that the buyer of figs has to accept ten percent worm-infested ones.</i>. But if it was collected from the start, this does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">For stored figs, the acceptable amount of bad ones is much smaller.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 6:8-9" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).6.8-9">Tosephta 6:8–9</a>. A slightly different text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 95a" href="/Bava_Batra.95a">Babli, 95a</a>.</i>: “100 amphoras<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">If these were wine amphoras.</i> I am selling to you, he has to deliver wine in the quality which is customarily sold at this place. 100 amphoras of wine I am selling to you, he has to deliver wine in the quality which is customarily sold at this store<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli holds that if wine was mentioned explicitly, best quality wine must be delivered.</i>. These 100 amphoras I am selling to you, even if they were vinegar they are delivered.” Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: If the vessels belonged to the buyer, he may tell him: I sold to you for [immediate] consumption<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the correct version is the expanded text of E:<br>רִבִּי חִייָה בַּר װָה. בְּשֶּׁהָיוּ הַקַּנְקַנִּים שֶׁל<br>מוֹכֵר. אֲבָל אִם הָיוּ קַנְקַנִּים שֶׁל לוֹקֵחַ יְכִיל<br>מֵימַר לֵיהּ. קַנקַנֶּיךָ הֶחֱמִיצוּ הַיַּיִן. וְרִבִּי יוֹסֵי בֶּן<br>חֲנִינָה אוֹמֵר. אֲפִילוּ קַנְקַנִּים שֶׁל מוֹכֵר יְכִיל מֵימַר <br>לֵיהּ. לִשְּׁתִּייָה מָכַרְתִּיו לָךְ.<br>Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: If the vessels belonged to the <i>seller. But if the vessels belonged to the</i> buyer, he may tell him: <i>Your vessels turned the wine into vinegar. But Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, even if the vessels belonged to the seller, he may tell him</i>: I sold to you for [immediate] consumption.<br>They hold that if the wine turned into vinegar in the buyer’s possession, the seller cannot he held responsible unless he guaranteed a certain quality.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.8.2-5"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:8</a>, explained there in Notes 166–177 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 96a" href="/Bava_Batra.96a">Babli <i>Bava batra</i>96a</a>). The formulation here gives the impression of Notes which a lecturer made for himself to outline the argument to be presented in class.</i> If one checked an amphora to continuously give heave from it<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">At the beginning of the wine making season the vintner sets aside an amphora to give heave for all the wine he is going to produce. It is forbidden to give vinegar as heave for wine. Therefore, the amphora should periodically be inspected to make sure it still contains wine. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.8.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i>3:8</a>, R. Jehudah specifies three dates when wine might turn into vinegar because of damaging meteorological phenomena; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:8:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.8.2-4">Halakhah 3:8</a> also states that in the first 40 days of winemaking no vinegar is created.</i>. Up to three days it is certain, after that in doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Joḥanan’s opinion.
