If the barrel was first checked and it contained wine, then rechecked and contained vinegar, any heave given during the first three days after checking is legitimate, the remainder questionable (but not unacceptable).</i> Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The first three days it certainly is wine, the last vinegar<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Heave for any wine produced then must be given anew.</i>, the middle ones are in doubt. Rebbi Abbahu said, I heard this from him. What does and says Rebbi Joḥanan? For three days it is certain, after that in doubt. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, Rebbi Yasa in the name of the rabbis, who come and hear from the House of Study of Bar Iṭyan, similar to the statement of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi about an amphora. If he lost a smooth needle and then found it rusty<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A broken vessel or tool cannot be impure; a rusty needle is not touched by the laws of impurity.</i>. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: The first three days it certainly is impure<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Not actually impure but subject to the laws of impurity.</i>, the last pure, the middle ones are in doubt. Rebbi Abbahu said, I also heard that from him. What does and says Rebbi Joḥanan? The first three days it is certain, after that in doubt. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba asked: If he came at the end of (a week)<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L.</i> [40 days]<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E. The reading of E is more probable but both are possibly correct.</i> and found it clearly to be vinegar, [did it clearly become vinegar immediately]<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, required by the context.</i> or only from that moment in the future<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This question can be asked only for R. Joḥanan since R. Joshua ben Levi asserts retroactivity for three days.</i>? Rebbi Isaac asked: If the crucial time passed at the end of forty days, did the importance of the crucial time invalidate that of forty days or did the importance of forty days invalidate that of the crucial time<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">If a meteorologically dangerous day occurred during the first 40 days of wine making, does one have to worry or not?</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This is a truncated copy of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.8.5"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:8, Notes 178–185</a>.</i> Rebbi Crispus asked: Does he check every year or only once every three years<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Jehudah, after meteorologically dangerous days.</i>? Let us hear from the following: If somebody sells wine for a year, he has to warrant it until after Tabernacles. Rebbi Yudan said, explain it for those Galileans who only harvest after Tabernacles and you cannot deduce anything. That is what was said, “old from the preceding year, aged three years”, should we say because of the tasting<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The correct spelling is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:8:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.8.2-5"><i>Giṭṭin</i>3:8</a>.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">There is a rather large lacuna here, compared to the text in <i>Giṭṭin,</i> which leaves the text here with a logical lacuna; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.1.7">Note 22</a>.</i> May one check one amphora on which all others depend [or] does he have to check every single one whether they would not turn into vinegar? Rebbi Shammai said, there are people who knock on the barrel at the top and know what is inside<sup class="footnote-marker">36a</sup><i class="footnote">Vintners do not have to open the amphoras and taste; they learn how to check quickly and accurately; the rules of warranty do not require an undue amount of work.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sells a plot to another to build a house<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A standard room, in the absence of specifications.</i>, or he contracts to build him a wedding building for his son<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">For his son to live in after his marriage when he still is dependent on his father.</i>, a widow’s seat for his daughter, builds four by six cubits, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Ismael says, this is a cowshed<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">No human should be forced to live in such a small space.</i>. If one wants to build a cowshed, he builds four by six cubits, a small room six by eight, a large one eight by ten, a dining hall<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>triclinium,</i> a dining room for three couches.</i> ten by ten, and its height equal to half its length and width<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Height = (length + width)/2.</i>; the reason is the Temple and its walls. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, a building like the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">It is presumptuous to try to imitate the architecture of the Temple for profane use.</i>?
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody sells a plot to another,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 6:24" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).6.24">Tosephta 6:24</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 98b" href="/Bava_Batra.98b">Babli 98b</a>.</i>: “For a large house<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic قَنُطَرَة “large building, bridge”; root قنطر “to abandon Bedouin way of life”.</i> he must provide him with twelve by twelve [cubits].”
Rav Hamnuna the scribe asked Rebbi Ḥanina: One verse says, “its height thirty cubits,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:2" href="/I_Kings.6.2"><i>1K.</i> 6:2</a>.</i>” and another verse says, “its height twenty cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:20" href="/I_Kings.6.20"><i>1K.</i> 6:20</a>.</i>” He had never heard this and they could not answer him anything. He asked Rebbi Jeremiah, who told him, from the ground up thirty cubits, from the inner room up twenty cubits. Rebbi Abbahu said, destroy the inner room<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The explanation given by R. Jeremiah is impossible; the inner room (the Holiest of Holies) was level with the Temple hall.</i>! The inner room was standing from the ground to the beams, as it is written, “covered with cedar from the ground to the beams.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such verse. A close verse is <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 7:7" href="/I_Kings.7.7"><i>1K.</i> 7:7</a>, “covered with cedar from ground to ground,” but it refers to Solomon’s court, not the Temple. Possibly the reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:16" href="/I_Kings.6.16"><i>1K.</i> 6:16</a>: “He built twenty cubits from the side of the Temple cedar siding from the ground to the walls,” meaning that walls were constructed differently above and below twenty cubits’ height.</i>” But from the ground up thirty cubits, from the Cherub up twenty cubits. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, it is an aggadic tradition that the space in the inner room not be counted. Rebbi Levi said, the space occupied by the Ark is not counted<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">What he wants to say is that the bodies of the Cherubim were not counted since v. 24 states that each wing of the Cherubim was 5 cubits, and v. 27 that the total span from one extreme wingtip to the other was 20 cubits, leaving no room for the bodies of the Cherubim.</i>. Rebbi Levi said, and it was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Rebbi Illai, the Ark stands in the middle and divides the room, ten cubits in every direction of the compass.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody has a cistern inside another’s house, he may enter at times when people usually enter<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Regular office hours only.</i> and must leave when people usually leave. He cannot bring his animal and let it drink from his cistern but draws water and lets it drink outside. Each of them makes a lock for himself<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The cistern’s owner makes a lock so the owner of the house cannot draw water from his cistern. The owner of the house puts a lock on the cistern so its owner cannot draw water without his knowledge.</i>.<br>If somebody has a vegetable garden inside another’s vegetable garden, he may enter at times when people usually enter and must leave when people usually leave. He cannot bring traders inside<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">To sell them his vegetables.</i> and cannot use it to go to another field; the outside owner may sow on the path<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owner of the inside plot may have a right of passage but has no property rights.</i>.<br>If he was allowed a path on the side by common accord, he may enter any time he wishes and leave any time he wishes; he can bring traders inside but cannot use it to go to another field; neither owner may sow on it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody has a cistern,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.3.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>: The outer one cannot sow on the path for the other can tell him, I would walk on slippery ground<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">I would have to tiptoe around your plants which is impossible for a person carrying agricultural tools.</i>. The inner one cannot sow on the path for the other can tell him, you would take care of yours and step on mine<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid stepping on your plants you will step on mine.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a public way was passing through somebody’s field, he took it away and gave them [a road] to the side of his property, what he gave, he gave, but what he wanted for himself he did not get<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">An unpaved public way passing between two fields cannot be changed.</i>. A private way is four cubits wide<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases where there is a right of access, the access road has to be a minimum of four cubits.</i>, a public way sixteen cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Not only for civil law but also for the rules of the Sabbath.</i>; a king’s highway<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">A paved road, government built.</i> has no limit; the way to the cemetary has no limit<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">A funeral procession may overflow into adjacent areas.</i>. The place for funeral orations, the judges of Sepphoris say an area of four <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Two thirds of a <i>bet se’ah</i> of 2500 square cubits or 1666 2/3 square cubits.</i>.<br>If somebody sells a plot to another to build a grave<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">A rock cemetery.</i>, or he contracts to build him a grave makes the cavity of the cavern four cubits by six<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">4 cubits wide by 6 deep.</i> and opens into it eight sepulchral chambers, three on each side and two at the back wall. The chambers are four cubits long, seven [handbreadths] high and six [handbreadths] wide<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since each chamber is one cubit wide, the chambers are spaced one cubit apart and the extreme ones half a cubit from the corner.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, he makes the cavity of the cavern six by eight [cubits] and opens into it thirteen sepulcral chambers, four on each side, three at the back wall, one to the right of the entrance and one to its left<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the Halakhah.</i>. In front of the cavern he excavates a courtyard of six by six [cubits] for the bier and its carriers. He opens into it two caverns, one at each side. Rebbi Simeon says four, each to each direction of the compass<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">His courtyard could not be excavated in front of the rock; it would have to be in the middle of a rock, accessible by ladders. No such construction was ever excavated.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, all depends on the rock<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The number of caves, of funeral chambers, their dimensions and spacing.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a public way was passing through somebody’s,” etc., to the end of the Mishnah. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph said, he makes them like keyholes<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">He explains what R. Simeon means when he requires an extra chamber each right and left of the door. Would they not also be on the side walls? He proposes to build the two additional chambers vertically and to bury the dead standing up. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 101a" href="/Bava_Batra.101a">Babli, 101a</a>, this opinion is attributed to R. Yose ben R. Ḥanina; there also it is rejected by R. Joḥanan.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan told him, but dogs are not buried in that way! What does he do? He builds inside as if outside<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">In the corners between the back wall and the sides, opposite the entrance, in a 45° angle to both walls meeting at the corner.</i>. Do the crossings not interfere with one another<sup class="footnote-marker">71,</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to another problem with R. Simeon’s plan. If four burial caves are dug from one center platform, by necessity the four cubits long burial chambers close to the entrance of each cave will intersect with the one dug for the cave at a right angle to it; the entire plan cannot be executed.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">E reads עבד, עובדות for עבר, עוברות. This may be a scribal error.</i>? They do not cross; he builds one higher and one lower<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The burial caverns have to be at least four cubits high so that the chambers in different caverns can be dug at different levels lest they intersect; or the caverns themselves have to be dug at different levels.</i>.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says to another: I am selling you a <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">30 <i>bet se’ah</i> or 75’000 square cubits.</i> of dust<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Agricultural land.</i>, if there were depressions ten handbreadths deep or rocks ten handbreadths high<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">They cannot be cultivated with the rest.</i>, they are not measured with it<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">To measure the exact size of the area delivered.</i>; less than that they are measured with it. But if he said, about a <i>bet kor</i> of dust, if there were depressions deeper than ten handbreadths or rocks higher than ten handbreadths, they are measured with it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says to another: I am selling you a <i>bet kor</i> of dust,” etc. Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Only if it<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The depressions or rocky areas.</i> is the smaller part of his field and enclosed in it<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The total of the area that is difficult or impossible to cultivate must be less than 50% of the total area and cannot be directly accessible from the boundary of the field.</i>. And a rock with a total surface area of a <i>bet rova‘</i> is not measured with it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">No single bad area may be more than 1/24 of a <i>bet se’ah</i> or 104 1/6 square cubits. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 103a" href="/Bava_Batra.103a">Babli, 103a</a>, restricts the total area of bad spots in a <i>bet kor</i> to 4 <i>batte rova‘</i>.</i>. If it is in the middle, it is measured, at the side it is not measured. What is a side and what is in the middle? In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, any place surrounded by a ploughed area is in the middle; any place not surrounded by a ploughed area<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">“In the middle” means at least four cubits removed from the boundary line of the property. Four cubits is the customary space needed for the plough and the cattle which draw it.</i> is to the side. Rebbi Yose came in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it was a majority on one side, but if you spread it, it becomes a smaller part, and it is measured with it<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of L. Since later the opposite conclusion is reached by an argument, the readings of L are consistent with one another. The reading of E is: “is <i>not</i> measured.” Then at the end the reading will be “is measured”. This is the reading of <i>Šiṭṭah Mequbeṣet Bava batra</i> 102b, which always quotes from mss.<br>The question is whether the sale is legitimate if an entire area of the field is not usable for agriculture because of many unusable spots when each in isolation would be insignificant.</i>. How wide<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, while specifying depth or height of an unacceptable obstacle, fails to indicate length or width.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, up to four cubits. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, up to ten handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Šiṭṭah Mequbeṣet</i> reads: R. Yannai says, 10 handbreadths, R. Yose bar Abun says, up to 4 cubits.</i>. If there was a depression ten handbreadths deep but less than four<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The mss., using letters as numerals, fail to indicate whether one refers to handbreadths or cubits. The printer of the <i>editio princeps,</i> spelling out the numeral as a word, by choosing the masculine form is referring to <i>handbreadths.</i> But <i>Šiṭṭah Mequbeṣet</i>(following Rashba and Ritba) quotes the question as about “4 by 4” and this in both Talmudim always refers to 4 by 4 <i>cubits</i> which characterizes the minimum size of a piece of land for which separate title can be acquired. Then it is possible to read the question as referring to depressions less than 10 handbreadths deep but more than 16 square cubits wide, which as a separate area may be rejected by the buyer as a separate field.</i> wide, what is the rule? Let us hear from the following, what Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: “Only if it is the smaller part of his field and enclosed in it. And a rock with a total surface area of a <i>bet rova‘</i> is not measured with it. If it was split, it is measured with it.” This means that if the greater part of it was on one side, but if you spread it, it becomes the smaller part and is not measured with it<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Šiṭṭah Mequbeṣet</i> reads: “is measured with it.” This is the logical consequence of the quote that the rocks be measured with the field if the total area of the rocky surface was more than a <i>bet rova‘</i> but no single piece of rock was of that size.</i>. If there was a long depression which added up to a <i>bet rova‘</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The question already was answered in the Mishnah, <i>viz.,</i> that this cannot be part of the area sold.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba asked: If it was shaped like sticks, is it measured with it or not? Rebbi Yudan bar Ismael asked: If it was like marble plates, is it measured with it or not?<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what these questions mean.</i> Rebbi Isaac bar Tebelai asked: If it was formed like chairs, does he measure from the place of its wall or does he measure from its declivity<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah does not specify how the area of depression has to be measured, whether from the upper rim or only on the bottom.</i>? Rav Huna in the name of Rav, [Rebbi] Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Only if the total agricultural area was four <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The remedy of the Mishnah, that the seller has to give the buyer additional land to make up for the bad spots, only works if there be enough adjacent agricultural land at the seller’s disposal. If the buyer would have to accept a replacement plot separate from the main field, the sale would be annulled.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am selling you a <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">30 <i>bet se’ah</i> or 75’000 square cubits.</i> of dust measured by the surveyor’s measuring tape,” if a little bit be missing, he deducts, if it be a little more, he has to refund<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer pays exactly for the square cubits he received in land based on the fixed purchase price per <i>bet kor</i>.</i>. If he said “more or less,” even if it was deficient by a quarter [<i>qab</i>] per <i>se’ah</i> or redundant by a quarter [<i>qab</i>] per <i>se’ah,</i> he received it<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">A sale “more or less” allows a margin of ±4.1667%.</i>. For more than that one makes a computation<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The sale price is proportional to the agreed price for 75’000 square cubits.</i>.<br>What does he return<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer who received more than 25/24 of the area agreed to for the stated purchase price.</i>? Money. But if he<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The seller.</i> agreed, he returns land to him. Then why did they say, he returns money to him? To empower the seller<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">To protect the seller; to avoid his becoming the owner of a parcel too small for agricultural use which practically would be worthless.</i>, for if this one<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The seller.</i> retained for himself a space for nine <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller retained at least 3750 square cubits of land for extensive agriculture or 208 1/3 square cubits of land for intensive agriculture, the seller automatically has a legal claim to receive the excess amount in land, rather than money.</i>, or in a vegetable garden the space for half a <i>qab</i> (or following Rebbi Aqiba a <i>bet rova‘</i>) he returns land to him. Not only the quarter [<i>qab</i>] he has to return but all the excess.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “I am selling you a <i>bet kor</i> of dust,” etc. Here, you say that “he has received it.” But there, you say that “not only the quarter [<i>qab</i>] he has to return but all the excess”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.7.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> it is stated that if the deviation of the delivered real estate from the contracted amount be less than 1/24, the contract was validly satisfied. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.7.2.1">Mishnah 3</a> it is stated that if the difference was more than 1/24, the entire excess beyond the contracted amount must be restituted, not only the excess beyond 104.167% over the contracted amount.</i>? They said, since you exclude him from the rule of “less<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Short for “more or less than ..”.</i> than measured by the surveyor’s measuring tape” you return him to the rules of “by the surveyor’s measuring tape.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “Measured by the surveyor’s measuring tape, more or less;<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The seller made two contradictory statements, <i>viz.,</i> that the amount delivered will be exactly as stipulated, but it might not exactly be the amount stipulated. Ben Nannas holds that the last statement always is the operative one. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 105a" href="/Bava_Batra.105a">Babli in 105a</a> and the Yerushalmi in <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 8:10 (Note 48) decide that practice does not follow Ben Nannas.</i>” “more or less” invalidated “measured by the surveyor’s measuring tape”. “More or less, measured by the surveyor’s measuring tape;” “measured by the surveyor’s measuring tape” invalidated “more or less”, the words of Ben Nannas. “By its description and its boundary strips,”<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to stating the surface area to be sold, the seller gave a topographical description of the field. If the latter disagrees with the former, less than a 16 2/3% error does not change the contract. An error of exactly 16 2/3% requires a monetary adjustment; a larger error invalidates the entire transaction.</i> less than one sixth he received it, up to a sixth he shall deduct.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What does he return? Money,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 4:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).4.7">Tosephta 4:7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 92b" href="/Bava_Batra.92b">Babli 92b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 60b" href="/Ketubot.60b"><i>Ketubot</i>60b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 11a" href="/Kiddushin.11a"><i>Qiddušin</i> 11a</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: If somebody sell a slave to another and he turns out to be a thief or a gambler<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κυβευτής “dicer, gambler.”</i>, he received him. But if he was a robber<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λῃστής. In contrast to the thief, a robber is armed and subject to capital punishment. He already is considered as dead; the sale of a dead slave is invalid.</i> or proscribed by the Government<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">A warrant is out for his arrest. The sale is invalid.</i>, he did not receive him.
“If somebody say to another: I am selling you half a field,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This should be in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 7:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.7.3.2">Halakhah 3</a>; it refers to the end of Mishnah 4, Note 28. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 106a" href="/Bava_Batra.106a">Babli, 106a</a>, <i>Rav</i> Huna declares that an error of one sixth follows the rules of errors less than one sixth; i. e., he holds that monetary adjustment is needed for any deviation from the area stated in the contract. It is not evident that <i>Rebbi</i> Huna here is a scribal error for <i>Rav</i> Huna even though <i>Rav</i> Huna is quoted reasonably frequently in <i>Neziqin</i> but <i>Rebbi</i> Huna is only mentioned three times, and this in <i>Bava meṣia‘</i>.</i> Rebbi Huna said, the sixth itself he shall deduct.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody say to another: I am selling you half a field, they estimate in common<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">They together appoint the appraiser who will determine what exactly constitutes half a field in value.</i> and he takes half of his field. Its Southern part I am selling to you, they estimate in common and he takes the Southern half. He has to accept the place of the wall<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">If it is necessary to fence in the property, the owner of the fenced-in property also has to provide for the wall and the trench separating the wall from the field.</i>, the trench or the small trench. How much is a trench? Six handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Width.</i>. And the small trench, three.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: If somebody says to another: I am selling you half a field, and X and Y and half of Z surround you. Rav Huna, and Rav Jehudah, and Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian who in the Babli usually is called Rav Jeremiah.</i>. One said, he gives him [an area shaped] like a <i>pandur</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πανδοῦρα “three-stringed lute.”</i>. And one said, he gives him something like a strip of cloth. And one said, he gives him something like patches<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear which shape of a field is meant. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 62" href="/Bava_Batra.62">Babli, 62a/b</a>, the question is about a Gamma-shaped field or one given in separate pieces. If one assumes the original field to be rectangular, the indication of 2½ boundary strips would indicate a surface composed of two rectangles forming a Γ. A “field in the form of patches” probably is a field of irregular shape, or even composed of disjoint triangles, discussed in <i>Kilaim</i> as “ox head” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.5"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:9, Note 202</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.10.1">2:7 Note 101</a>). For this, one has to assume that one of the sides is not contiguous with the other two.</i>.

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Some inherit and bequeath; inherit but do not bequeath, bequeath but do not inherit, neither inherit nor bequeath<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Among relatives.</i>. The following inherit and bequeath: The father from the sons, and the sons from the father, and the paternal brothers inherit and bequeath<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The father inherits from his childless son. The sons inherit from their father. The brothers inherit from their childless brother after the father’s death.</i>. A man from his mother, a man from his wife, and the sons of sisters inherit but do not bequeath<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A son inherits from his widowed or divorced mother. A husband inherits from his wife. The sons of sisters inherit from an uncle who died without children or brothers. But women do not inherit except daughters of a man who died without sons.</i>. A woman to her son, a woman to her husband, and the mother’s brothers bequeath but do not inherit<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A son inherits from his widowed or divorced mother. A husband inherits from his wife. The sons of sisters inherit from an uncle who died without children or brothers. But women do not inherit except daughters of a man who died without sons.</i>. But the maternal brothers neither inherit nor bequeath<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Maternal halfbrothers are not considered relatives for the law of inheritance since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:55" href="/Numbers.26.55"><i>Num.</i> 26:55</a> restricts inheritance to “the paternal tribe.”</i>.<br>The order of inheritances is the following: “If a man die without a son, you shall transfer his estate to his daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:8" href="/Numbers.27.8"><i>Num.</i> 27:8</a>. Biblical law of inheritance is derived mainly from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:8-11" href="/Numbers.27.8-11"><i>Num.</i> 27:8–11</a>.</i>.” The son precedes the daughter and all the son’s descendants come before the daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If at the death of the father a daughter is alive and also a predeceased son’s daughter, the granddaughter will inherit but not the daughter. On the other hand, the daughter has a claim on the estate for support or dowry, cf. Mishnah 11, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:3:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.3.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:3</a>.</i>. The daughter precedes the brothers and all the daughter’s descendants precede the brothers. The brothers precede the father’s brothers and all their descendants precede the father’s brothers. This is the principle: For every one preceding in inheritance, his descendants precede<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Only agnates inherit; all claims to inheritance are valid <i>per stirpes.</i></i>. But the father precedes all his descendants<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">But not his grandchildren. If a son dies during his father’s lifetime, the father inherits only if the son left no descendants. Since the brothers only could inherit as their father’s descendants, they cannot inherit if the father is alive.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Some inherit and bequeath,” etc. It is written: “If a man die without a son, you shall transfer his estate to his daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:8" href="/Numbers.27.8"><i>Num.</i> 27:8</a>. Biblical law of inheritance is derived mainly from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:8-11" href="/Numbers.27.8-11"><i>Num.</i> 27:8–11</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Ismael stated: The verse distinguished this inheritance from all other inheritances mentioned in the Torah, since for all of them it is written “you shall give,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The inheritance of the agnates, verses <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:9-11" href="/Numbers.27.9-11"><i>Num.</i> 27:9–11</a>.</i>” but here is written: “you shall transfer.” It is an extension of the law<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the <i>hiph‘il</i> “to transfer” in the meaning of <i>pi‘el</i> “to be pregnant”, to express an exception to the usual rules. As stated at the end of the paragraph, the inheritance of the daughter implies a transfer of the property to her sons, who belong to her husband’s family, not her father’s.</i> that the daughter shall inherit. The Gentile Sages say, son and daughter are equal<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is the rule in Roman and Egyptian native law while in Egyptian Greek law the daughter inherited only if her dowry had not been paid (cf. R. Taubenschlag, <i>The Law of Greco-Roman Egypt in the Light of the Papyri,</i> New York 1944, §11.)</i>, for they explain “if he have no son;” therefore, if he has one both are equal. One objected, is it not written “if he have no daughter;<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:10" href="/Numbers.27.10"><i>Num.</i> 27:10</a>.</i>” therefore, if he has one are both equal<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Would a daughter have to share her inheritance with the agnate uncles?</i>? And you agree, if there is no child<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A man’s brothers only inherit in the absence of <i>stirpes.</i></i>, here also, if there is no son<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A daughter only inherits in the absence of sons.</i>. The Sadducees say, the son’s daughter and the daughter are equal<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 116b" href="/Bava_Batra.116b">Babli 116b</a>.</i>, for they explain: Since my son’s daughter who comes by force of her father does inherit from me, would it not be logical that my daughter who comes by force of myself should inherit from me? One told them, no. If you mention the son’s daughter who only inherits by the power of the brothers, what could you say about the daughter who only inherits by the power of the old man<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The deceased son’s daughter inherits her father’s share in her grandfather’s estate; her claim is the same as that of any of her uncles. But a daughter who has brothers has no claim whatsoever on her father’s estate; she only has a lien on the estate for her dowry if at her father’s death she was not yet married. The principles of the claims of a son’s daughter and a daughter are different. <i>Sifry Num.</i>134.</i>? The verse says, “any daughter who is an heiress of the tribes<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 36:8" href="/Numbers.36.8"><i>Num.</i> 36:8</a>. The verse really reads: Any daughter, heiress of property, of the tribes of the Children of Israel, … But the word מִמַּטּוֹת carries a (minor) dividing accent which may justify the truncation in the quote.</i>.” How is it possible for a daughter to inherit from two tribes? But explain it if her father was from one tribe and her mother from another<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The same text in <i>Ta‘aniot</i> 4:11, 69c l. 37 and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111a" href="/Bava_Batra.111a">Babli 111a</a>.</i>.
So far the son [inherits from] the father. The father from the son? Since the son only inherits by the father’s power, is it not logical that the father on whom the son depends inherit from him? The verse says, “close<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:11" href="/Numbers.27.11"><i>Num.</i> 27:11</a>.</i>”; the closer relative has precedence<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The argument presented would imply that the father has precedence over his grandchildren. Therefore, the argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> has to be rejected and the rules all must be found in the verse. It is asserted that a person’s closest relatives are his children. The verse then also justifies the rule of the Mishnah that the heir is the agnate connected to the bequeather by a minimum of ascents in the genealogical tree.</i>.
So far a daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In all paragraphs dealing with the laws of inheritance, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:6-11" href="/Numbers.27.6-11"><i>Num.</i> 27:6–11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 36:8-9" href="/Numbers.36.8-9">36:8–9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:15-17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.15-17"><i>Deut.</i> 21:15–17</a>, only the father is mentioned. One might infer that these laws do not apply to the mother’s estate. Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 36:8" href="/Numbers.36.8"><i>Num.</i> 36:8</a> mentions the daughter as heiress, one infers that the daughter inherits from her mother (<i>Sifry Num.</i> 134).</i>. A son? Since the daughter, whose power is diminished regarding the father’s property, has her power increased regarding the mother’s property, should the son’s power not be increased regarding the mother’s property, since his power is increased regarding the father’s property? It turns out that for the daughter one infers from the verse and for the son from an argument <i>de minore ad majus.</i> Does the son precede the daughter? Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said in the name of Rebbi Zachariah the butcher’s son: So did Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah say in the name of Rebbi Simeon: Both son and daughter are equal for the mother’s tribe<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111a" href="/Bava_Batra.111a">Babli 111a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:10" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.10">Tosephta 7:10</a>.</i> Rebbi Mallukh in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Practice follows Rebbi Zachariah. Rebbi Yannai the Kappadokian had a case; the judges were Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read <i>Rebbi</i> Huna; the second generation Babylonian Rav Huna could not sit in a court together with two Galilean fourth generation judges, even though in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111a" href="/Bava_Batra.111a">Babli, 111a</a>, <i>Rav</i> Huna is reported to have erroneously followed R. Zachariah.</i>, Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi, and Rebbi Aḥa. Rebbi Aḥa told them: Our brothers outside the Land are unqualified and err in practice; in particular they rely on Rebbi Mallukh in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, but it is not so. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Practice does not follow Rebbi Zachariah. Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Practice does not follow Rebbi Zachariah. Rebbi Eleazar the father of Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">From here it seems that “bar Naḥman” was his family name.</i> in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah: Practice does not follow Rebbi Zachariah. <sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111a" href="/Bava_Batra.111a">Babli 111a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:10" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.10">Tosephta 7:10</a>.</i> Rebbi Yannai and Rebbi Joḥanan were sitting when Rebbi Yudan the Prince came and asked concerning: “Any daughter who is an heiress of the tribes,” what is the rule? He<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yannai. the senior authority.</i> said to him, it binds the father’s tribe together with the mother’s tribe. Since from the father’s tribe there is nothing for the daughter when there is a son, so also from the mother’s tribe there is nothing for the daughter when there is a son. Or is it the other way around? Since from the mother’s tribe there is something for the daughter when there is a son, so also from the father’s tribe is there something for the daughter when there is a son<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Yudan the Prince’s objection.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read, parallel to the Babli: He said to R. Joḥanan; since the student R. Joḥanan could not tell his teacher R. Yannai to insult the patriarch to his face.</i>, let us leave; that man does not want to listen to words of instruction.
“A man from his mother, a man from his wife.” Is “a man from his mother” not the same as “a man from his wife”<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Neither rule has a direct scriptural root. The inheritance of a man from his mother was earlier derived from a biblical expression (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.1.2">Note 20</a>). The inheritance of a man from his wife is not implied by this. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111b" href="/Bava_Batra.111b">Babli 111b</a> and <i>Sifry Num.</i> 134 the husband’s inheritance is inferred from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 24:33" href="/Joshua.24.33"><i>Jos.</i> 24:33</a>; one explains that the “property of Phineas in the Mountains of Ephraim” must have come to him as inheritance from his wife since as a priest he would have been barred from receiving property outside of Levitic cities.</i>? Rebbi Isaac: he wanted to enumerate all cases<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The word פנסטא in L’s text defies explanation. E’s reading פנטסה is read by S. Lieberman as πάντοσε “in all ways, in any way”. The Mishnah often is formulated with redundancies, if the enumeration of all cases helps memorization.<br>In the Yerushalmi, the husband’s right to his deceased wife’s estate possibly remains a matter of common law [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:5:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.5.2-6">Halakhah 6</a>, followed by Maimonides (<i>Neḥalot</i> 1:8, <i>Iššut</i> 12:3); rejected in the Babli as minority opinion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 83b" href="/Ketubot.83b"><i>Ketubot</i> 83b</a>)]. Cf. M. A. Friedman, <i>Jewish Marriage in Palestine,</i> Tel-Aviv and New York 1980, p. 391 ff.</i> and did not find them, so he formulated the rule twofold.
<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 115a" href="/Bava_Batra.115a">Babli 115a</a>. The argument is intended to show a biblical source for the claim that the rules of inheritance are to be interpreted <i>per stirpes.</i></i>“Son.” Not only a son, from where a daughter’s son? The verse says, “a son<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">No definite article is used in the paragraph, to allow maximum freedom of interpretation.</i>”, from anywhere. “Daughter.” Not only a daughter, from where a daughter’s son, a son’s daughter, daughter’s daughter, a son’s son? The verse says, “a daughter”, from anywhere. “Brothers.” Not only brothers, from where brothers’ sons, brothers’ daughters, brothers’ sons’ daughters? The verse says, “his flesh’s relatives”, from anywhere<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">As long as an agnate exists, no matter how many generations one would have to go back, he becomes the heir. Only the male ancestors are considered since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:55" href="/Numbers.26.55"><i>Num.</i> 26:55</a> requires that inheritance be governed by “the names of the <i>fathers’</i> tribes.”</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Zelophehad’s daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">He had five daughters but no sons, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:1-7" href="/Numbers.27.1-7"><i>Num.</i> 27:1–7</a>.</i> took three parts<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Cities with the names of the daughters dominate the entire Southern part of Cisjordan Manasseh. The problem to be discussed is how to compute the distribution of land by Joshua according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:52-56" href="/Numbers.26.52-56"><i>Num.</i> 26:52–56</a> which prescribes first that the land be distributed to “those”, i. e., those mentioned in the second desert census, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:5-51" href="/Numbers.26.5-51"><i>Num.</i> 26:5–51</a>, but computed “by the names of their fathers’ tribes” which means the names of those who left Egypt.</i> in inheritance: Their father’s who was of those who left Egypt, his part among his brothers in the estate of Hepher<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Who was supposed to have been alive at the time of the Exodus.</i>, and because he was firstborn he was taking two parts<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Zelophehad’s daughters,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 119b" href="/Bava_Batra.119b">Babli 119b</a>; Tanḥuma <i>Pineḥas</i> 6, 9 ,Tanḥuma Buber <i>Pineḥas</i> 8, 9, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 11" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.11"><i>Num. rabba Pineḥas</i> 11</a>.</i> Zelophehad’s daughters said before our teacher Moses: If we are Zelophehad’s daughters, we should inherit from our father. If we are not Zelophehad’s daughters, let our mother enter levirate marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">If daughters cannot inherit then the widow of a man who dies without sons should be treated like the widow of a childless man, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:5-9" href="/Deuteronomy.25.5-9"><i>Deut.</i> 25:5–9</a>. Since the latter conclusion obviously is impossible, so is the hypothesis from which it follows.</i>. Immediately, “<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:5" href="/Numbers.27.5"><i>Num.</i> 27:5</a>.</i> he brought their suit before the Eternal.” The Holy One, praise to Him, told him, “<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:7-8" href="/Numbers.27.7-8"><i>Num.</i> 27:7–8</a>.</i> Zelophehad’s daughters speak correctly. Give them possession of inheritance,” give them real estate, give them movables, give them their father’s part among their father’s brothers.
<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 117a" href="/Bava_Batra.117a">Babli 117a</a>; a different version <i>Tanḥuma Pineḥas</i> 5 <i>= Num. rabba</i>21(9).</i> Rebbi Joshiah said, the Land was distributed to those who left Egypt, as it is written: “By the names of the fathers’ tribes they shall inherit.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:55" href="/Numbers.26.55"><i>Num.</i> 26:55</a>, referring to the census <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 1" href="/Numbers.1"><i>Num.</i> 1</a>.</i>” Then why does the verse say: “To those<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:53" href="/Numbers.26.53"><i>Num.</i> 26:53</a>. The verse asserts that the distribution of land has to be based on the census of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26" href="/Numbers.26"><i>Num.</i> 26</a>.</i>”? Only because of women<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The daughters of Zelophehad.</i> and because of children<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">As will be explained later, Notes 63 ff.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read with E and the Babli “Jonathan” as contemporary of R. Joshiah.</i> said, the Land was distributed to those who entered the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Each of the persons counted in the second census became a candidate for a piece of land similar in value to that of any other.</i>, as it was said, “to those it shall be distributed<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:53" href="/Numbers.26.53"><i>Num.</i> 26:53</a>. The verse asserts that the distribution of land has to be based on the census of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26" href="/Numbers.26"><i>Num.</i> 26</a>.</i>.” Then why does the verse say: “By the names of the fathers’ tribes”? This inheritance was different from all other inheritances in the world. For in all other inheritances in the world the living inherit from the dead but here the dead from the living<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">While the number of lots was determined by the second census, the final allotment was made through the first census as explained by Rebbi in the section after the next.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">117a/b (R. Simeon ben Eleazar), slightly differently <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.8">Tosephta 7:8</a> (R. Eliezer ben Jacob).</i>: Rebbi Joshua ben Qorha said, the Land was distributed to those who left Egypt and to those who stood in the plains of Moab. How is that? If one was both of those who left Egypt and those who stood in the plains of Moab, he took two parts<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: It was <i>given</i> to those who left Egypt and <i>distributed</i> to those who stood in the plains of Moab.</i>. Of those who left Egypt but not of those who stood in the plains of Moab, or of those who stood in the plains of Moab but not of those who left Egypt, he took one part. The daughters of Zelophehad took five parts: their part with those who left Egypt<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The decree that those who left Egypt could not enter the Land applied neither to women nor to Levites.</i> and their part with those who stood in the plains of Moab. And because he<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Zelophehad. Even though the rule that the firstborn takes a double portion does not apply to expected acquisitions of the estate, here it is considered already acquired because of God’s promise in Egypt (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 6:8" href="/Exodus.6.8"><i>Ex.</i> 6:8</a>).</i> was a firstborn they took two parts. And their father’s part among his brothers. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says otherwise<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">They only received three parts. The Mishnah can only be interpreted following R. Joshiah.</i>. But “giving you shall give them inheritance among their father’s brothers.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:7-8" href="/Numbers.27.7-8"><i>Num.</i> 27:7–8</a>.</i>” That is what is written: “Ten measures fell to Manasseh<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 17:5" href="/Joshua.17.5"><i>Jos.</i> 17:5</a>. It is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 17:2" href="/Joshua.17.2"><i>Jos.</i>17:2</a> that Gilead and Bashan were given to the seven male clans of Manasseh, and the Cisjordan territory to the five female clans. If together this made ten districts, it follows that the five females in the plains of Moab, while representing only one person of the Exodus, received 3/10 of the entire territory of Manasseh.</i>”.
Joshua and Caleb took three parts: Their part with those who left Egypt and their part with those who stood in the plains of Moab<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This applies only to Caleb and Joshua.</i>; in addition they took the parts of the spies<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 117b" href="/Bava_Batra.117b">Babli 117b</a>.</i>. This is what is written: “Joshua bin Nun and Caleb ben Yephuneh lived off the men, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 14:38" href="/Numbers.14.38"><i>Num.</i> 14:38</a>.</i>” But the part of the complainers<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 11:1" href="/Numbers.11.1"><i>Num.</i> 11:1</a> ff.</i> and Korah’s gang became common property; their sons [inherited] from their paternal and maternal grandfathers. That is what is written: “Korah’s sons did not die.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:11" href="/Numbers.26.11"><i>Num.</i> 26:11</a>. They were Levites not involved in the distribution of land.</i>”
Rebbi explained it by a parable; to what can this be compared? To two brothers, co-owners who left Egypt. One had nine sons, the other a single son. They inherited ten <i>bet kor.</i> Each of them takes a <i>letekh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Half a <i>bet kor</i>.</i>. They return it to their fathers and then split. It turns out that the single son takes half and the sons of the other take half<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 117a" href="/Bava_Batra.117a">Babli, 117a</a>, Rebbi is credited with the explanation by the parable credited here to R. Dositheos ben Jehudah.<br>The text here is inconsistent in several places. If together they took ten <i>bet kor,</i> each of them took a <i>bet kor</i> rather than a <i>letekh,</i> half a <i>bet kor.</i> If the fathers were above the age of twenty at the Exodus, there is no reason why the sons of one should be co-owners with the son of the other. If they were below age twenty at the Exodus, they should have survived and the entire question could not be raised. Therefore, one has to assume that the grandfather was alive at the time of the Exodus and the entire allotment was returned to the <i>grandfather,</i> from whom each son inherited the claim to half the estate which was then split for the ten grandsons.</i>.
Rebbi Dositheos ben Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">A contemporary of Rebbi.</i> explained it by a parable; to what can this be compared? To two brothers, Cohanim and co-owners<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Both are still living at their father’s house.</i> who stood by a threshing floor<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">To ask for heave.</i>; one had nine sons and the other had one. They took (the area of)<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This has to be deleted since one refers here to volumes, not areas.</i> ten <i>qab.</i> They returned it to their father and split<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">One has to assume that after they collected the heave, the grandfather and the fathers died, since otherwise the grandfather, on whose property the entire family was living, would have received everything for distribution among his dependants. Parables, situated in never-never land, cannot be subjected to stringent logical analysis.</i>. It turns out that the son of one takes half and the sons of the other take half.
Rebbi Joḥanan objected: “I shall bring you to your forefathers’ land, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">A misquote of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 6:8" href="/Exodus.6.8"><i>Ex.</i> 6:8</a>. The verse ends: “I shall <i>give</i> it to you as an <i>inheritance,</i> I, the Eternal.” <i>Giving</i> implies a voluntary act, a <i>gift,</i> whereas <i>inheritance</i> is automatic by law.</i>” If it was a gift, why an inheritance? And if an inheritance, why a gift? But after he gave it to them as a gift, he turned around and gave it as inheritance<sup class="footnote-marker">70,</sup><i class="footnote">The rights of the people of the Exodus were established as <i>gift</i>; then those of the generation of the conquest already were an <i>inheritance</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 117b" href="/Bava_Batra.117b">Babli 117b</a>).</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">E here has a parallel version:<br>רִבִּי יִצְחָק בַּר מַרְיוֹן בְּשֵׁם רִבִּי יוֹחָנָן. כָּתוּב וְטַפְּכֶם אֲשֶׁר אֲמַרְתֶּם לָבַז יִהְיֶה וּבְנֵיכֶם אֲשֶׁר לֹא יָֽדְעוּ הַיּוֹם טוֹב וָרַע הֵמָּה יָבֹאוּ שָׁמָּה וְלָהֶם אֶתְּנֶנֶּהּ וְהֵם יְרָשׁוּהָ. אִם מַתָּנָה לָמָּה יְרוּשָׁה. וְאִם יְרוּשָׁה לָמָּה מַתָּנָה. אֶלָּא מֵאַחַר שֶׁנְּתָנָהּ לָהֶן בִּלְשׁוֹן מַתָּנָה חָזַר וּנְתָנָהּ לָהֶן בִּלְשׁוֹן יְרוּשָׁה.<br>Rebbi Isaac bar Marion in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 1:39" href="/Deuteronomy.1.39"><i>Deut.</i> 1:39</a>): “Your little ones, of whom you said, they will be taken as prey, and your children who today do not know good or bad, they will come there, to them I shall <i>give</i> it, and they will <i>inherit</i> it.” If it was a gift, why an inheritance? And if an inheritance, why a gift? But after he gave it to them <i>in the language of</i> a gift, he turned around and gave it <i>in the language of</i> inheritance.”</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia said, anywhere one mentions מוֹרָשָה it means weariness<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">It is unclear from which root he derives the word since the only candidate besides <i>yrš</i> “to inherit” would be <i>rwš</i> “to be poor.”</i>. They objected, is it not written, “an inheritance (מוֹרָשָׁה) of the congregation of Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:4" href="/Deuteronomy.33.4"><i>Deut.</i> 33:4</a>: “Moses commended to us Torah, an inheritance …” This verse should not have a negative connotation.</i>”? He said, there is no weariness greater than this. He who studies forgets everything<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kohelet Rabbah 1:13" href="/Kohelet_Rabbah.1.13"><i>Eccl. rabba</i> on 1:13</a>: “For his own benefit a person studies Torah and forgets it, for if a person could study Torah and not forget it, he would study it for two or three years and then never think of it again” and miss all rewards for the study of Torah.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Both son and daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If there are no sons, the rules of inheritance apply to daughters as if they were sons.</i> are equal for the rules of inheritance, except that the son take a double portion of the father’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.17"><i>Deut.</i> 21:17</a>. The entire paragraph only deals with father and son; since the mother is neither required nor empowered to recognize a child as hers, it cannot refer to the mother’s inheritance.<br>It probably is correct to read פִּי שְׁנַיִם as “double portion” rather than “two thirds” which would read פִּים (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 13:21" href="/I_Samuel.13.21"><i>1S.</i>13:21</a>); cf. <i>Sifry Deut.</i> #217. The double portion of male first-borns is also found in the Egyptian native law both in Ptolemaic and in Roman times and the Syro-Roman law book.</i> but no double portion of the mother’s property. And the daughters can claim sustenance from the father’s property<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">After the father’s death; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.1.1">Mishnah 9:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:8:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.8.2-15"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:8</a>.</i> but not from the mother’s property.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Both son and daughter are equal for the rules of inheritance,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">76,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.17"><i>Deut.</i> 21:17</a>. The entire paragraph only deals with father and son; since the mother is neither required nor empowered to recognize a child as hers, it cannot refer to the mother’s inheritance.<br>It probably is correct to read פִּי שְׁנַיִם as “double portion” rather than “two thirds” which would read פִּים (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 13:21" href="/I_Samuel.13.21"><i>1S.</i>13:21</a>); cf. <i>Sifry Deut.</i> #217. The double portion of male first-borns is also found in the Egyptian native law both in Ptolemaic and in Roman times and the Syro-Roman law book.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The verse makes it clear that only property actually <i>at hand</i> in the estate at the moment of the father’s death is subject to the double portion privilege of the firstborn son, but not <i>expected</i> income (<i>Sifry Deut.</i> 217).</i>: “Of anything which will be found with him,” etc. How could he inherit the expectancy like existing property? <sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.7">Tosephta 7:7</a>, <i>Bekhorot</i> 6:18.</i> How? If his father died during his father’s father’s lifetime, he takes a double portion of his father’s estate but not a double portion of his grandfather’s estate. But if his father was a firstborn, just as he takes from his father’s estate so he takes from his grandfather’s estate<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">If the grandfather dies after the father, the grandson can take a double portion of the former’s estate only if he is the only son of a firstborn son. If his father had been a firstborn but has brothers, the rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> implies that their father’s estate inherits a double portion in the grandfather’s estate. Since this is future income, all brothers inherit equally. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 52b" href="/Bekhorot.52b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i> 52b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Abba [Cohen]<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E; this is the name usually quoted.</i> bar Delaiah: It was said “a rule of law<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.17"><i>Deut.</i> 21:17</a>. The entire paragraph only deals with father and son; since the mother is neither required nor empowered to recognize a child as hers, it cannot refer to the mother’s inheritance.<br>It probably is correct to read פִּי שְׁנַיִם as “double portion” rather than “two thirds” which would read פִּים (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 13:21" href="/I_Samuel.13.21"><i>1S.</i>13:21</a>); cf. <i>Sifry Deut.</i> #217. The double portion of male first-borns is also found in the Egyptian native law both in Ptolemaic and in Roman times and the Syro-Roman law book.</i>” in the matter of a double portion just as it was said “a rule of law<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:11" href="/Numbers.27.11"><i>Num.</i> 27:11</a>.</i>” in the matter of a single portion. Since for a single portion one considers the son as if he were alive to take his father’s single portion<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Only agnates inherit; all claims to inheritance are valid <i>per stirpes.</i></i> so for a double portion one considers the son as if he were alive to take his father’s double portion<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.1.1">Mishnah 2</a> applies to double portions as well as single ones.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who says, “my firstborn X shall not take a double portion,” [or] “my son Y shall not inherit together with his brothers,” did not say anything since he stipulated against what is written in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 7:14. The rules based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:6-11" href="/Numbers.27.6-11"><i>Num.</i> 27:6–11</a> are prescriptive, rather than eventual rules in the intestate case.</i>. One who distributes his property orally<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If he makes the oral declaration of the will of a critically ill person.</i>, if he increased for one, or diminished for another, or made the firstborn equal to the others, his words are upheld; except if, however, he formulated it in terms of inheritance<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">If he wrote the entire will in terms of inheritance, the will is void.</i>, he did not say anything. But if he wrote in terms of gift, whether at the start, or in the middle, or at the end, his words are upheld<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In a document one has to follow the formal rules. If he used the language of gift but also mentioned inheritance, the will is valid as explained by R. Hoshaia in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who says, “my firstborn X shall not take a double portion,’ ” etc. Rebbi La made the right of the firstborn equal to that of the brothers<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">He approved a will which gave the firstborn only what the other brothers received.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said to them: Is that not a verse, “he shall not be able to declare as firstborn.”<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">He thought that Rebbi La acted in error.</i><br><sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Midrash Tanna'im (Midrash Haggadol Deut. 21:16)</i>; Sifry Deut. 216.</i>Rebbi Eliezer said, by the Temple Service! He is able, but he is not permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">What is missing here (compared to the other sources) is the statement that any action to deprive the firstborn of his inheritance be invalid. In general we hold that if a certain action is biblically forbidden, if it is done anyway it is valid in law since, if it were invalid, it could not be punishable. But an action by the father to deprive his firstborn of his double portion is not punishable; therefore, it is invalid.</i>. Except that he is able designating it as a gift<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In a document one has to follow the formal rules. If he used the language of gift but also mentioned inheritance, the will is valid as explained by R. Hoshaia in the Halakhah.</i>.
“But if he wrote in terms of gift, whether at the start, or the middle, or the end, his words are upheld.” Rebbi Hoshaia said: “There <i>shall be given</i> to Mr. X the inheritance I want him to inherit.” “X should inherit <i>the gift</i> I want him to inherit.” “X shall inherit the inheritance which I <i>gave to him</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">These are examples of acceptable formulations in a will essentially formulated in the language of inheritance but with mention of <i>giving</i> or <i>gift</i> either before, or in between, or after mentioning inheritance. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 129a" href="/Bava_Batra.129a">Babli 129a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:17" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.17">Tosephta 7:17</a>.</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is a reformulation of one in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:8-6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.8-6.3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5, Notes 495–499</a>.</i>“Write and give field X to person Y.” Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Simeon ben Yaqim brought a case before Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">An oral death-bed will which is valid as if written during the bequeather’s lifetime.</i> He told them, if it is documentation, then write and deliver. If it is to transfer property, everybody agrees that nobody can transfer property after his death<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">If the testator had said, I am giving it to him, you go and deliver the document, it is valid. If it were not a death-bed will, it would need an act of transfer, but the act of transfer is understood from the order given by the dying person. But if it is clear from the language of the testator that transfer of property should be effected by the delivery of the deed and he died in the meantime, delivery is impossible since a person’s power over his property ends with his death because the biblical laws of inheritance are prescriptive (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 135b" href="/Bava_Batra.135b">Babli 135b</a>).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">A reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.8"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5, Notes 490–492</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 152a" href="/Bava_Batra.152a">Babli 152a</a>.</i> Samuel asked Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">To inquire about Rav’s opinion after the latter’s death.</i>: if one wrote a gift in the language of a sale<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">A will formulated not as a true gift, as required by the Mishnah, but as a fictitious sale.</i>? He answered, he made it ride on two racing horses; Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, they only said that one brings two white horses and makes it ride on both. One goes in one direction and the other in another; it turns out that he caught nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">For Rav, the will is valid; for Samuel it is invalid since gifts and sales follow different rules.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who says, Mr. X shall inherit from me while he has a daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">But no son.</i>, or, my daughter shall inherit from me while he has a son, did not say anything since he stipulated against what is written in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 7:14. The rules based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:6-11" href="/Numbers.27.6-11"><i>Num.</i> 27:6–11</a> are prescriptive, rather than eventual rules in the intestate case.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqah said, if he said that about anybody who can be his heir, his words stand; about anybody who cannot be his heir, his words do not stand<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:18" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.18">Tosephta 7:18</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who says, Mr. X shall inherit from me,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Both following paragraphs are reformulations of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:11:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.11.3"><i>Ketubot</i>4:12, Notes 239–248</a>. The statement of R. Joḥanan paraphrases <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:18" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.18">Tosephta 7:18</a>. It is clear that the Tosephta was unknown to the editors, if any, of this Yerushalmi. The Yerushalmi mentions neither the restriction of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 130a" href="/Bava_Batra.130a">Babli (130a</a>) by R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa touching the privilege of the firstborn nor the extension of the Tosephta which includes grandchildren.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa said only this about a son among sons or a daughter among daughters, but not a daughter among brothers<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The daughter among the father’s brothers is also mentioned in <i>Ketubot</i>, but naturally the father’s brothers do not inherit if there be a daughter as stated in the same sentence. The two clauses “daughter among brothers,” “brother among daughters” mean the same.</i> or a brother among daughters. Rebbi Joḥanan said, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa. Rebbi asked Rebbi Nathan<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ketubot:</i> Nathan the Babylonian.</i>; bar Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ketubot:</i> Rebbi Abba.</i> said, so was the question: What is Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa’s reason? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, so was the question: What did they see to say that practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa? He told him, did you not teach us so<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.11.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 4:12</a> states that in a polygamous family the sons of one wife inherit her <i>ketubah</i> in addition to their part in the father’s estate. This shows that while the formulation of the biblical law of inheritance presupposes equal parts for every son, there are situations where by common law inheritances are distributed unevenly.</i>: “they shall inherit”? So is the Mishnah: “they shall take.”<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since the distribution of the deceased mother’s <i>ketubah</i> is an obligation which the father accepted at his marriage, it is not an <i>inheritance</i> from the father (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 131a" href="/Bava_Batra.131a">Babli 131a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 52b" href="/Ketubot.52b"><i>Ketubot</i>52b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 55a" href="/Ketubot.55a">55a</a>).</i> [Rebbi]<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E. In <i>Ketubot</i> R. Ze‘ira.</i> La said, even following him who said “inherit”, is not the power of the court strengthened<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The inheritance of the sons in a polygamous family is not a private contract but common law interpreted as an ordinance of the Court which promulgated the rules of <i>ketubah</i>.</i>? As you say, nobody can transfer property using the language of gift; here he may do it<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Property can be transferred by a gift document but not by words unless accompanied by an act of acquisition. The rule is waived for a death-bed will.</i>. Similarly, nobody can transfer property using the language of inheritance; here he may do it<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Again, if the language of inheritance is used exclusively, the will is valid only as a death-bed will. Otherwise the terminology of giving must be used.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">A reformulation of texts in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:1:7-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.1.7-8"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:1, Notes 44–57</a>.</i> There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 9:1</a>. If a couple write a prenuptial agreement reserving the wife’s property to her and her heirs, it is unconditionally valid for the rabbis but Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel denies the validity of the inheritance clause.</i>: “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, if she dies, he should inherit from her since he made a condition contradicting what is written in the Torah.” Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: “Since he made a condition contradicting what is written in the Torah and anybody’s condition contradicting what is written in the Torah is invalid”? Conditions other than for money matters. But here we are dealing with a money matter!<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees with Rabban Simeon that the inheritance clause is invalid but questions his reasoning since this is a money matter where stipulations are permitted which deviate from biblical rules; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 19b</a>.</i> What is Rav’s reason? Because in the end he acquired it<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 84a" href="/Ketubot.84a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 84a</a>, it is stated explicitly that for Rav the husband’s right to his wife’s estate is rabbinic. Therefore his acquisition of the estate is a court decree.
