(In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 8:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.8.5.2">Yerushalmi, <i>Ketubot</i> 8:5 Note 44</a>, this is ascribed to R. Simeon ben Laqish.)</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">One is tempted to read instead: R. Joḥanan said, practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, since the reason given in the next sentence has nothing to do with R. Joḥanan ben Beroqa’s statement. This is the approach taken by all classical commentaries but it is impossible today since the text here and later is confirmed by E.<br>As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.5.2">Note 109</a>, R. Joḥanan holds that the rule of R, Joḥanan ben Beroqa is common law modifying biblical rules. Therefore, he also must hold that an acceptance of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel’s rule cannot be based on the latter’s reason. His explanation essentially is identical with that ascribed to Rav in the preceding sentence.</i>, for Rebbi Joḥanan said, if she sold or gave away<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Her paraphernalia property, to which the husband has no property rights during her lifetime.</i>, the rule should be that it is valid. Then why did they say that her sale is invalid? Lest she smuggle away her properties from her husband and say, they are mine. Rebbi Justinus had a case before the rabbis and they obligated him<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">He and his wife lived under a regimen of separation of properties. When she died, the local court told him to surrender his wife’s properties to her family.</i>. He came before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who told him, enter your properties<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">He decided following Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel as rabbinic ordinance.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Did not Rav say, because in the end he acquired it. Did not Rebbi Joḥanan say, practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa? Did not Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish say, go and enter your properties? Who are these rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The greatest authorities of the first two Amoraic generations follow Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Which court could decide against him?</i>? The rabbis of Rebbi Justinus<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Courts of first instance sometimes do err.</i>.
“And he shall inherit from her<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 26:11" href="/Numbers.26.11"><i>Num.</i> 26:11</a>: “If his father had no brothers, transfer his estate to his relative who is closest to him of his family; <i>he shall inherit (from) her</i>.” In the verse, the feminine <i>her</i> refers to the estate. The identification of <i>her</i> with the wife is R. Aqiba’s (<i>Sifry Num.</i>134). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18" href="/Leviticus.18"><i>Lev.</i> 18</a>, the <i>masculine</i> word שְׁאֵר “relative” is used exclusively for <i>female</i> relatives.</i>.” I could think that just as he inherits from her, she should inherit from him<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111b" href="/Bava_Batra.111b">Babli 111b</a>.</i>. The verse says, “from her.” He inherits from her, she does not inherit from him<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">He explains the emphasis, “he shall inherit <i>her</i>” when the same could have been expressed by the shorter, וְיָֽרְשָׁהּ. The additional word used for <i>her</i> implies <i>not him</i>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the words of the Sages are that her father inherits from her, her brothers inherit from her<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">They reject R. Aqiba’s derivation. <i>Her</i> in the verse refers to the estate; by biblical law her clan should be her heirs. The inheritance of the husband is purely rabbinical.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal objected: If you say that the inheritance status of a woman is not from the Torah, should he not inherit from his preliminarily wedded wife<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">There really is no basis for this question since it is generally accepted that preliminary marriage be only relevant for criminal law whereas definitive marriage activates all financial aspects of a marriage (cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Qiddušin</i>.) The question is rather whether there be a biblical basis for the common law practice that the husband’s claim on the wife’s property only starts with definitive marriage, when the couple starts living together.</i>? As you say there, “the one close to him,<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read הַקָּרוֹב both times for הַקְּרוֹבָה. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:2" href="/Leviticus.21.2"><i>Lev.</i> 21:2</a> the expression שְׁאֵרוֹ הַקָּרֹב אֵלָיו “the relative close to him” is explained as “his wife who is close to him,” i. e., his definitively wedded wife, since all other closely related relatives are enumerated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:2-3" href="/Leviticus.21.2-3">vv. 2–3</a>. The mention of closeness excludes the preliminarily wedded one since intimacy with her is forbidden to him [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor" href="/Sifra,_Emor"><i>Sifra Emor</i> (4</a>)].</i>” not the preliminarily wedded one, so also here, “the one close to him,” not the preliminarily wedded one. Rav Hamnuna objected: If you say that a woman does not inherit by the word of the Torah, should not the husband inherit the expectancy like existing property<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">If the heiress wife dies before her father, his estate will go to her childen. Since in general common law gives her inheritance to her husband, why not in this case also?</i>? Rebbi Yose said, so did Rebbi teach: The husband does not take the expectancy like that which is at hand<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">This is a statement of fact: Moneys due to the wife after her death go to her children, rather than her surviving husband. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 125b" href="/Bava_Batra.125b">Babli 125b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there is the first-born whose inheritance is from the Torah but he does not take the expectancy like that which is at hand<sup class="footnote-marker">79,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.7">Tosephta 7:7</a>, <i>Bekhorot</i> 6:18.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">There is biblical precedent to exclude future income from present distribution.</i>.
Rebbi Isaac said: About those who write, if she should die without children, all that was hers shall revert to her father. This is a stipulation about money; the stipulation is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">This is repeated from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.1.13"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:1, Note 87</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is R. Ismael’s argument to show that the husband’s inheriting his deceased wife’s properties, if not explicit in the Torah, at least was established practice already in the time of Joshua (<i>Sifry Num.</i> 134.) A different explanation of the verses quoted here is given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 113a" href="/Bava_Batra.113a">Babli, 113a</a>.</i>“Segub fathered Jair,<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 2:22" href="/I_Chronicles.2.22"><i>1Chr.</i> 2:22</a>. The verse states that Jair, a Calebite, had 23 villages in the land of Gilead (Manasseh).</i>” etc. From where did Jair have cities on Mount Gilead? But he married a woman from the daughters of Manasse who died and he inherited from her. If you would say that the inheritance from a wife was not a word of the Torah it should not say that “Jair had” but that “Segub had.<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">In v. 21 it is reported that Jair’s grandfather Hezron married a Gileadite wife. If the property came through her, the verse should have attributed ownership to her son Segub, rather than her grandson Jair.</i>” Similarly, “Eleazar ben Aaron died.<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 24:33" href="/Joshua.24.33"><i>Jos.</i> 24:33</a>, “they buried him on his son Phineas’s hill which had been given to him on the Mountain of Ephraim.”</i>” From where did Phineas have property on the Mountain of Ephraim? But he married a woman from the daughters of Ephraim and inherited from her. If you would say that the inheritance from a wife was not a word of the Torah it should say that “Eleazar had” not that “Phineas had.<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">As Rashi <i>ad. loc.</i> intimates, one cannot say that the tribe of Ephraim gave Phineas property among themselves since the Torah explicitly excluded priests from receiving any land outside the Levitic cities (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:1" href="/Deuteronomy.18.1"><i>Deut.</i> 18:1</a>). Therefore, ownership of property outside such cities must be by inheritance from a non-priestly wife. The tribal affiliation of Eleazar’s father-in-law Puṭiel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 6:25" href="/Exodus.6.25"><i>Ex.</i> 6:25</a>) is not known.</i>”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one writes his properties over to others<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">As a gift “from today, effective after my death”.</i> while neglecting his children, what he did is done<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">If writing the document was accompanied by an act of acquisition on behalf of the beneficiary, it becomes irrevocable.</i> but the Sages do not approve of him. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if his children did not behave properly, let him be well remembered.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one writes his properties over to others,” etc. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, about one who writes his properties over to others while neglecting his children, the verse says: “their sins were on their bones<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 32:27" href="/Ezekiel.32.27"><i>Ez.</i> 32:27</a>. It is a sin which will cling to his bones even in the grave. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 133b" href="/Bava_Batra.133b">Babli, 133b</a>, Samuel told his student Rav Jehudah in his law practice never to help anybody to deprive a son of his inheritance.</i>.” As the following: A person deposited his properties with Rebbi Abba bar Mamal and told him, if my sons will be useful give them half and keep half. His sons came and took half. Later they came to appeal<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The root of מיעור is ערר “to launch an appeal.”</i> against him. He told them, your father said only: If my sons will be useful give them half and keep half. Now you are badly educated<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κακο-παιδευτοί. E reads קקפריגמיניין which Lieberman explains as κακοτραγμόνες “unworthy, useless”.</i>, return to me what I gave to you.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who says, this is my son, is to be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.17"><i>Deut.</i> 21:17</a> is interpreted to mean that the father has the right to recognize a son without submitting a proof; his statement has to be accepted by the court. There is no difference whether the son was born from a licit or an illicit union; as long as the mother was Jewish the son frees the childless wife from levirate marriage and is entitled to a full share in the inheritance (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.6.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 2:6</a>).</i>. This is my brother, he is not to be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">If he presents an otherwise unknown person as his paternal half-brother, he is required to provide proof since the half-brother would 1° reduce his other brothers’ share in their father’s estate and 2° require levirate marriage in case the deceased had no other brothers and died childless. Since he acts to the detriment of others, he cannot be believed without solid proof.</i> but he shall split his part with him<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Since a person can take an obligation on himself, by recognizing the stranger as his half-brother he acknowledged the latter as a co-heir. Since he cannot diminish the share of his other brothers, he must share his own inheritance with the stranger.</i>; if he dies the properties shall return to their origin<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">His heirs are not his own children but the sons of the man who recognized him as half-brother. Their father’s unsubstantiated recognition of the stranger cannot impair his son’s rights.</i>. If he received property from another source, his brother shall inherit with him<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">If the stranger accepted the status of paternal half-brother and then died without issue, the half-brother inherits.</i>. If somebody died and a will<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 1:7, Note 90.</i> was found tied to his hip, this is nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Since a person can give away property only when he is alive, a will is valid only if either it was a public document (a death-bed will) or contains a note that an act of transfer of property was executed during the testator’s lifetime. Without such a remark, the will is invalid</i>. If in it he transferred property to another<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">E. g., that it was signed by witnesses on behalf of the beneficiaries.</i>, whether of the heirs or not of the heirs, his words are confirmed<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Once part of a will is valid, the entire will is valid.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who says, this is my son, is to be believed,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">A materially different text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.3">Tosephta 7:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 4:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.4.7.2"><i>Qiddušin</i> 4:7 Notes 182–194</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 127b" href="/Bava_Batra.127b">Babli 127b</a> as Amoraic statement.</i> If it was the general belief that he was his son, but at the moment of his death he said, he is not my son, or about one not his son he said, he is my son, he is believed<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:17" href="/Deuteronomy.21.17"><i>Deut.</i> 21:17</a> requires the father to recognize his son “on the day he distributes his estate”, any statement he makes on his death bed in matters of recognizing sons must be believed without requiring proof.</i>. If he stood near the toll collector and said, he is my son<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">To pay only the low rate applicable to persons, not the high rate payable for slaves as merchandise.</i> and then changed and said, he is my slave, he is believed. He is my slave, and then said, he is my son, he cannot be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Since he acted to his own detriment.</i>. Some Tannaïm state, he is to be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 7:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).7.3">Tosephta 7:3</a>. If he always declares him a slave for toll collectors but never in other situations, he is believed but not otherwise.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, for example those Nabateans who particularly use their sons as slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">They may switch between <i>slave</i> and <i>son</i> without inconsistency.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">A reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:5:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.5.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 1:6</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:8:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.8.1-4">Notes 166–173</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 8:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).8.1">Tosephta 8:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 138a" href="/Bava_Batra.138a">Babli 138a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 39b" href="/Chullin.39b"><i>Ḥulin</i> 39b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 24b" href="/Keritot.24b"><i>Keritut</i> 24b</a>).</i>“If somebody said that all his property should be given to X, a Cohen, and [the estate] contained slaves, even if [the Cohen] said ‘I cannot have them’ they eat heave. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, since he said ‘I cannot have them’, the heirs acquired them.” Rebbi La said, they disagree if he did not specify. How do we hold? If it is clear that he wants them, everybody agrees that his slaves may eat heave. If it is clear that he does not want them, everybody agrees that the heirs acquired them. But we deal with the case that he did not specify, where the rabbis think that at the start he wanted them and then he changed his mind. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, since he said ‘it is impossible for me’, the heirs acquired them.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 8:10" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).8.10">Tosephta 8:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 152b" href="/Bava_Batra.152b">Babli 152b</a>, <i>Peah</i>3:9 (17d l. 66) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.8.4">Note 175</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon ben Gamiel says: A will invalidates a will, a gift does not invalidate a gift.” Rebbi Abba bar Ḥana: Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both are saying that in any case where he can invalidate his will if he recuperates, he also can repeal his gift<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">The gift of a sick person, even if accompanied by an act of acquisition, follows the rules of death-bed wills, rather than that of gifts among healthy persons.</i>. As the following: Rebbi Onias’s sister<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Who obviously lived under a regime of separation of properties.</i> wrote her property over to Rebbi Onias. She needed money and sold it to her husband. After she died, he<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">R. Onias came to protest against his brother-in-law’s taking his sister’s properties as his own.</i> came to protest against him. He asked him, why did you not claim it<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">If the gift was irrevocable, the sale was impossible and R. Onias should have intervened immediately.</i> during her lifetime? He answered, I did not want to cause her pain. Nevertheless, Rebbi Immi removed it<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The reader may choose his own interpretation of this sentence. R. Eliahu Fulda explains that the gift was irrevocable among healthy people, that R. Onias became the proprietor of the real estate but had to refund the sale price to his brother-in-law. <i>Pene Moshe</i> explains that the gift was a death-bed gift which became meaningless when the woman recovered; therefore R. Immi confirmed the husband in his possessions and took away the gift document from R. Onias. In either case would R. Immi have followed the instructions of his teacher R. Joḥanan.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who writes his properties over to his sons must write: “from today and after my death<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">“Today” for the transfer of title to the property, “after my death” for the transfer of usufruct.</i>,” the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Yose said, it is unnecessary<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Without an explicit disclaimer, a deed is always deemed to be valid from the day it was written; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.5"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:3 Note 67</a>.</i>. If one writes his properties over to his son for the time after his death, the father cannot sell them because they are written over to the son; the son cannot sell because they are in the father’s possession. If the father sold, they are sold until he dies. If the son sold, the buyer has no claim until the father dies. The father harvests and feeds anybody he pleases; what becomes part of his estate belongs to the heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">As movables, it is part of the estate, not only of the son inheriting the particular real estate.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who writes his properties over to his sons,” etc. Rebbi Simeon ben Yaqim brought a case before Rebbi Joḥanan. “From today after his death,” his gift is a gift. “From today and after death” it is no bill of divorce. <sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">A slight reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:3:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.3.2-6"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:3</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:2:6-3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.2.6-3.1">Notes 67–75</a>.</i> The colleagues say, so he told him: this is no condition. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Giṭṭin</i>: R. Ze‘ira. For reasons of chronology, this is the more convincing reading.</i> said, so he told him: It applies neither to bills of divorce nor to gifts. Rebbi La said, for a gift; since he said “from today”, the gift is clear. Why did he write “after death”? To reserve the yield to himself. But in bills of divorce, since he wrote “from today” in the bill, it would be a separation. Why did he write “after death”? To reserve her body to himself. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said before Rebbi La, not to reserve her earnings for himself? He answered, we do not find a woman married to one man and her earnings belonging to another. Rebbi Ze‘ira confirmed him<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read with the text in <i>Giṭṭin</i>: מְקַלֵּס לֵיהּ “he acclaimed him”.</i> and called him “son of the Torah.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">A slight reformulation of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:5:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.5.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 1:6</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:7:3-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.7.3-4">Notes 157–162</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 9:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).9.14">Tosephta 9:14</a>.</i>“If somebody says, I freed my slave Ṭabi, I freed him, I shall free him, he is free, this one is free; then he acquired.” Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: But only by a document. “He should be freed, Rebbi says, he acquired, but the Sages say, he did not acquire.”
If someone said, field X I gave to Y, it is given to him, it should be his, Y should inherit my property, Y should take possession of my property, he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Title to property cannot be transferred by simple declaration; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 9:12" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).9.12">Tosephta disagrees, 9:12</a>. The Tosephta text is explained away by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 40b" href="/Gittin.40b">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i>40b</a>.</i>. It should be given to him as a gift, Rebbi says, he acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">If this was a death-bed declaration.</i>, but the Sages say, he did not acquire; but one forces the heirs to fulfill the deceased’s words.<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 14b" href="/Gittin.14b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 14b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 15a" href="/Gittin.15a">15a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 40a" href="/Gittin.40a">40a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 70a" href="/Ketubot.70a"><i>Ketubot</i> 70a</a>.</i> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 9:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).9.14">Tosephta 9:14</a>.</i>: Rebbi Simeon ben Gamliel says, also if one writes διέθεμεν<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">“I disposed by will”, from Greek “to dispose” (H. M. Pineles). A. Gulak, <i>Tarbiz</i> 1 fasc. 4 (1931) 144–146 has noted that the expression τάδε διεθέμην is used in Egyptian Greek deeds; also cf. R. Taubenschlag, <i>The Law of Greco-Roman Egypt in the Light of the Papyri,</i> New York 1944, p. 143. Since the expression is a legal Greek term, its use characterizes a valid deed even though the corresponding use of the past in Hebrew was declared invalid as statement of a deed. (S. Lieberman, <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Bava batra</i> p. 441, wants to infer that Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel validates also the Hebrew נָתַתִּי; this seems unjustified.)</i> in Greek it is a gift. Rebbi Ḥanin in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: I turned to all linguists to know what is διέθεμεν and nobody told me anything<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">They were not acquainted with Greek legalese.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:1, Notes 66–85</a>. The text is partially problematic.</i> One who said, my property should be given to X, if X died to Y, if Y died to Z. The one who was mentioned earlier receives the property earlier. If the second died during the first’s lifetime, the first one has the usufruct; when he dies the property reverts to heirs of the bequeather<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Since Z has to receive the property from Y’s estate, if Y never got the property Z cannot get it. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 136b" href="/Bava_Batra.136b">Babli 136b</a>.</i>. If the third died during the second’s lifetime, the first<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read: The second.</i> one has the usufruct; when he dies the property reverts to heirs of the bequeather. If both the second and the third die during the first’s lifetime, the first has the usufruct and acquired the real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “my property should be given to X” is the language of a bequest. The bequest was conditional. If the condition becomes moot, the bequest becomes unconditional. In <i>Ketubot,</i> Rebbi is reported to permit the first holder to <i>sell</i> the real estate and use the proceeds for himself; then the second and third will receive nothing since the real estate for which the condition was formulated is no longer operative. Naturally, he may sell only if prior to the sale he had acquired the property. So here one should understand <i>acquired</i> as <i>acquired in order to sell</i>.</i>, the words of Rebbi. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, he only has the usufruct<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 137a" href="/Bava_Batra.137a">Babli 137a</a>.</i>. Ḥizqiah said, practice follows Rebbi. Rebbi Yannai said, Rebbi agrees that he cannot dispose of it by a death-bed will<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">This is intelligible only in the <i>Ketubot</i> version. Since a death-bed will becomes valid only at the moment of death, the lien formulated by the bequeather already became active; the property is Y’s before X’s death-bed will is activated.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, not even as a gift from a healthy person<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the property was given to X for his use; therefore he may sell it for profit but is barred from giving it away.</i>. As in the following: A woman wrote her properties over to a certain man<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">In her will. From the following it is clear that in this case, there was no separation of properties. During the woman’s lifetime, her husband has the usufruct of her properties. Therefore, she can neither sell them nor give them away.</i>. She needed money and sold it to her husband. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Madia<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear whether his name was <i>bar Madia</i> or <i>bar Maria</i>.</i> brought the case before Rebbi Yose. Did not Rebbi Yannai say, Rebbi agrees that he cannot dispose of it by a death-bed will? Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read: R. Yose; the story is dated three generations after R. Joḥanan and contradicts the latter’s statement above.</i> said to him, is that not the gift of a healthy person? Since the husband of this woman is obligated to provide for her food, is that not like a death-bed will? So far, he is obligated to provide for her bread and legumes. Maybe eggs? Maybe chicken? Since it was stated that the first has the usufruct and acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “my property should be given to X” is the language of a bequest. The bequest was conditional. If the condition becomes moot, the bequest becomes unconditional. In <i>Ketubot,</i> Rebbi is reported to permit the first holder to <i>sell</i> the real estate and use the proceeds for himself; then the second and third will receive nothing since the real estate for which the condition was formulated is no longer operative. Naturally, he may sell only if prior to the sale he had acquired the property. So here one should understand <i>acquired</i> as <i>acquired in order to sell</i>.</i> the real estate, that means that she may sell the property even in order to eat delicacies. Rebbi Aḥa bar Jacob said, it was stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 4:6</a>.</i> that her burial is part of her upkeep. If she did not have a burial site, since it was stated that the first has the usufruct and acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “my property should be given to X” is the language of a bequest. The bequest was conditional. If the condition becomes moot, the bequest becomes unconditional. In <i>Ketubot,</i> Rebbi is reported to permit the first holder to <i>sell</i> the real estate and use the proceeds for himself; then the second and third will receive nothing since the real estate for which the condition was formulated is no longer operative. Naturally, he may sell only if prior to the sale he had acquired the property. So here one should understand <i>acquired</i> as <i>acquired in order to sell</i>.</i> the real estate, it means that he may sell the real estate and acquire a burial site.
Rebbi Yose said: About those who write, “if she hates, if she is hated”. This is a stipulation about money; the rabbis upheld it<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">A prenuptial agreement about eventual divorce settlements disregarding rabbinic rules in the matter is valid. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.5.5">Note 131</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he left<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">A father who died intestate.</i> adult and underage sons, the adult ones cannot be provided for<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">With business suits and tools at the expense of the undistributed estate.</i> at the expense of the underaged, nor the underaged fed at the expense of the adults, but they split evenly<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the estate is not distributed, none of the brothers may receive more than any other.</i>. If the adult ones wed<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">If a brother marries after the father’s death and the wedding expenses are borne by the estate, any other yet unmarried brother has a claim on the estate equal to the expenses of the one who was the first to marry.</i>, the underaged may wed. But if the underaged said, we want to have weddings just as you had<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">During the father’s lifetime.</i>, one does not listen to them, because what their father gave them, he gave.<br>If he left adult and underage daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">If no sons survive the father.</i>, the adult ones cannot be provided for at the expense of the underaged, nor the underaged fed at the expense of the adults, but they split evenly. If the adult ones wed, the underaged may wed. But if the underaged said, we want to have weddings just as you had, one does not listen to them. It is a restriction about daughters which does not exist for sons, that daughters will be fed at the expense of sons<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">In any marriage, the daughters have a lien on the estate to be supported by the estate until married, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.12.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 4:13</a>. Sons may not inherit until the claims of the daughters are satisfied. But in the absence of sons, daughters of all ages have identical claims on the estate.</i> but not at the expense of daughters.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he left adult and underage sons” etc. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Could the older ones take 100 barrels, each worth twenty denars, and the younger ones take 100 barrels, each worth ten denars<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">What is the meaning of the “even split” of the estate required by the Mishnah? Is it by quantity or price. The answer is that everything is computed by money’s worth.</i>? He told him, what these take, those should be taking.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 13:1</a>, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.3.5">Notes 50–52</a>.</i> If somebody die and is survived by sons and daughters. If the estate is large, the sons inherit and the daughters will be sustained. If the estate is small, the daughters shall be sustained and the sons shall go begging. Admon said, because I am a male, shall I lose? Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, I am convinced by Admon’s statement.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody die and is survived by sons and daughters,” etc. So is the Mishnah: “the daughters shall be sustained and the sons shall go (begging) [peddling]<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parenthesis is from L, the one in brackets from E. Since the L text is identical with the Mishnah, it is clear that the critical remark “so is the Mishnah” refers to the E text or a similar reading as reported in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:3:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.3.5"><i>Ketubot</i> (13:4, Notes 74–76</a>). In contrast to the <i>Ketubot</i> text, here the alternate reading is endorsed.<br>According to the Mishnah and the L text, the sons are sent begging irrespective of their age. According to the revised version, small children covered by the decree of Usha (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.8.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:8, Notes 187–193</a>), who cannot earn money, must be supported with the daughters.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of a paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.3.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:3, Notes 53–58</a>.</i> Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: That there should be twelve months of sustenance for all of them. Samuel said, this is the opinion of Rabban Gamliel ben Rebbi, but the words of the Sages are, until they reach adulthood or are married. That is both a leniency and a restriction. Sometimes they are close to adulthood, sometimes far from adulthood. They asked before Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, what did you hear from Rebbi Joḥanan? He said to them, I did not hear anything from him concerning this matter except what Nathan bar Hoshaia asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: what if there was sustenance for twelve months but the estate diminished in value? He answered him, since they started with permission, they started.
<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Compare <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:3:3-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.3.3-4"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:3, Notes 59–71</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Mana. One said, only if at the end there be sustenance for twelve months. But the other said, even from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The question which is answered here is only stated in <i>Ketubot</i>: What is the definition of a large estate? In the first opinion the estate is large if after 12 months there still is left enough money to sustain the entire family, sons and daughters, for 12 months. In the other opinion it is enough that there be sufficient funds for the first 12 months.</i>. If there was not sustenance for all of them for twelve months but they increased it so that how there is sustenance for all of them for twelve months<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The sons could not be supported during the first 12 months. May they be supported now that the estate is large according to the first definition?</i>? Rebbi Immi the Babylonian said, let us hear from the following: If the heirs sold, they sold from their own property<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.3.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:3, Note 63</a>: If the male heirs sold real estate from a small estate, their sale is valid since they are the heirs in biblical law; the rights of the daughters are purely contractual. While the widow can take the real estate away from the buyer for her <i>ketubah</i> as prior mortgage holder, the daughters cannot since their claim was created only at their father’s death, when the sons already were legal owners of the property.</i>. So much more, if they increased it, they should be able to divide it<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The court-appointed guardian will have to include the sons for sustenance.</i>. If there was sustenance for all of them for twelve months and a widow to support, may the sons say to the daughters: you have sustenance only for twelve months?<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This question is difficult to understand since, as mentioned later, the widow’s claim is paramount and the value of the estate is computed only after all claims of the widow are satisfied. Therefore, the mention of the widow here has to be deleted. In <i>Ketubot,</i> the question is: If there was more than enough to sustain only the daughters but not enough for both daughters and sons, according to the second definition of a large estate may the sons distribute among themselves the monies exceeding the daughters’ claims for 12 months?</i> Rebbi Eudaimon said, let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A truncated version of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.2.1">Mishnah 10:2</a>. The Mishnah prohibits sons to manipulate the valuation of an estate in order to gain an advantage over their half-brothers. It is implied here that they cannot manipulate the estate to the detriment of their sisters.</i>“If the orphans said, we accept the properties of our father for the value of an extra denar, one does not listen to them.” Rav Ḥisda asked<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ketubot,</i> Rav Ḥisda asked the first question, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.1.4">Note 6</a>.</i>: if there was sustenance for all of them for twelve months and a widow to support, may the sons say to the daughters: you have sustenance only for twelve months?<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This question is difficult to understand since, as mentioned later, the widow’s claim is paramount and the value of the estate is computed only after all claims of the widow are satisfied. Therefore, the mention of the widow here has to be deleted. In <i>Ketubot,</i> the question is: If there was more than enough to sustain only the daughters but not enough for both daughters and sons, according to the second definition of a large estate may the sons distribute among themselves the monies exceeding the daughters’ claims for 12 months?</i> But this means, only if there was sustenance for all of them for twelve months, except for <i>ketubah</i> of the wife, except for the sustenance of the widow, except for the dowries of the daughters, except for loans by document, except for loans by witnesses, except for her burial<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The value of an estate can only be determined after all actual and contractual liabilities were deducted.</i>. The widow and the daughters are of equal rank<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 140b" href="/Bava_Batra.140b">Babli, 140b</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 43a" href="/Ketubot.43a"><i>Ketubot</i>43a</a>, the widow’s position is privileged. If there is not enough money to sustain both widow and daughters, the daughters are sent begging.</i>. The widow and the sons are of equal rank. The widow does not push the daughters aside and the daughters do not push the widow aside. Sometimes the widow pushes the daughters out by means of the sons. Just as the widow pushes out the daughters by means of the sons, should the widow not push out the sons<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that in the last two sentences, the places of “sons” and “daughters” should be switched. The question is whether the widow’s sustenance is privileged over the daughters’ claims to sustenance.</i>? She might claim her <i>ketubah</i> and lose her right to sustenance<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Her claim to sustenance is conditional; it cannot override the claim of the daughters which is absolute. The Babli disagrees.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A slight reformulation of the paragraph in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 3:4:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.3.4.11"><i>Soṭah</i> 3:4, Notes 143–148</a>. The paragraph is inserted to show that the widow may be manipulated to forgo her claim of sustenance.</i> As the following: Rebbi Sabbatai’s widow was wasting the estate. His sons came before Rebbi Eleazar. He said to them, what can one do for you; they are stupid. When they left, they said, let us ask the scribe what to do. He told them: Behave as if you would sell of the real estate and she will demand her <i>ketubah</i> and will no longer have support. After some time she came before Rebbi Eleazar. He said, It should come over me if I had told them anything. What can one do, this one was hit by predatory people.
<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">An almost complete copy of paragraphs in <i>Ketubot</i> 13:1 (Notes 36,37), 13:4 (Note 77); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 109a" href="/Ketubot.109a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>109a</a>.</i> Rav Ḥananel in the name of [Rav]<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in L, inserted from E; required by the parallel texts and reasons of chronology.</i>; Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Abba bar Jeremiah: Practice follows Ḥanan in his two statements; practice does not follow Admon in his seven statements. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda in the name of Rebbi Isaac bar Ḥaqula: In every case in which Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, I am convinced by Admon’s statement, practice follows Admon.
<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.1.6"><i>Yebamot</i> 4:1, Notes 24–40</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 141b-142a" href="/Bava_Batra.141b-142a">Babli <i>Bava batra</i> 141b/142a</a>.</i> Samuel said, one can transfer benefits to the fetus; Rebbi Eleazar said, one cannot transfer benefits to the fetus. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Eleazar. “If a proselyte died<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">He died without Jewish children; his estate became ownerless with his death. His Jewish wife, who is not an heir, may take everything she can lay her hands on. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.4">Halakhah 3:1, Notes 19</a>,23; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.6"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:4 Note 106</a>).</i> and Jews plundered his estate, then it became known that he had a son<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">A Jewish heir.</i> or that his wife was pregnant, everybody is required to return [what he took]<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The unborn baby is an heir by biblical decree, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:8" href="/Numbers.27.8"><i>Num.</i> 27:8</a>.</i>. If they returned everything and then the son died or his wife had a miscarriage, the last one in possession acquires it.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This <i>baraita</i> seems to follow Samuel. The unborn child acquired the inheritance; if he dies, the estate newly becomes ownerless and the last person to hold it acquires. Rebbi Eleazar should require that everything be returned to the first taker.</i>” Did the first group acquire or not? Explain it, because of hopelessness<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">When the court decreed that the proselyte had a legal heir and the first takers returned the property, they gave up hope of owning it and with it any claim of ownership. Cf. <i>Bava meṣia‘</i> 2:1 Note 6.</i> and it does not even disagree with Samuel. Or should the last one not have acquired it<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since he got it under a false assumption.</i>? Still it is because of hopelessness.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he is survived by sons, daughters, and a sexless child<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A child having neither male nor female sex characteristics (neither penis nor vagina).</i>, if the estate is large the males push him to the females; if the estate is small the females push him to the males<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sexless is a claimant in an inheritance case, the burden of proof would be on him to show that he belongs to the advantaged class.</i>. If somebody says, in case my wife gives birth to a male, he shall take a mina; if she gave birth to a male he takes a mina. But if a female 200, if she gave birth to a female she takes 200. If a male a mina, if a female 200, if she gave birth to a male and a female, the male takes a mina and the female 200<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">All these examples are only a preface to the statement that the sexless child has no claim on payments promised a child of a certain sex. Females need a larger trust fund at birth to pay for a future dowry.</i>.<br>If she gave birth to a sexless child, he does not take anything. If he said, any that my wife shall give birth to shall take, and if there is no heir but him, he inherits everything<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of siblings the sexless inherits, whether he really is a male or a female.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he is survived by sons, daughters,,” etc. It was stated: “If somebody says, he who brings me the news that my wife gave birth to a male shall take 200”: if his wife gave birth to a male he takes 200. If she gave birth to a female he shall take a mina”: if his wife gave birth to a female he takes a mina. If it was a male and a female, he only takes a mina<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 9:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).9.5">Tosephta 9:5</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Mana said, the pain about the daughter invalidates the joy about the son.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he is survived by adult and underage children and the adult ones improved the property<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Before the estate was distributed.</i>, the improvement goes to the estate. But if they said<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Before the court.</i>, make an inventory of what our father left<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">To determine the underage brothers’ share under the court’s supervision.</i> and we are going to work and profit from it, they improved it for themselves.<br>Similarly, a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A widow.</i> who improved the property improved for the estate<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">She acts as a guardian for the children as well as for herself.</i>. But if she said<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Before the court.</i>: see what my husband left for me, I shall work it and eat from it<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">She takes the property as her <i>ketubah</i> settlement.</i>; then she improved it for herself.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he is survived by adult and underage children,” etc. Rebbi La said, “if they said” in court. In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, even a jug.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>hirnea,</i> also <i>irnea, -ae,</i> “jug, pitcher” (E. G.).</i> Rav said, even a basket, even an axe. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: Even a needle, even a thread to tie down.
<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.1.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:1 (Notes 23–26</a>), 12:2 Note 41; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 144a" href="/Bava_Batra.144a">Babli 144a</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina said, if somebody definitively married off his son in a house, the latter acquired it<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This is qualified in <i>Ketubot</i> to apply only to a first marriage and in the Babli to the first marriage of the oldest son to a virgin, it being the first marriage of any of his children.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia stated, the house but not the movables<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:1:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.1.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:1</a>, R. Hoshaia is reported as saying “the movables but not the house”.</i>. Rebbi La divided the dining hall<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>triclinium</i>.</i> between himself and his son<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The newlyweds could use half the <i>triclinium</i> as their permanent abode.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked Rebbi Yose: If the ceremony was in the bedroom<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κοιτών, -ῶνος, m.</i> but the wedding party was in the dining room, what is the rule? He told him, one does not see him leaving<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">After the wedding the groom is expected to stay in the wedding chamber with his wife; he is not needed for the festivities and acquired only the bedroom.</i>.
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said: One divides for the underaged because of the adults<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If the adults want to divide the estate in order to work for themselves, the court is obligated to separate the underaged’s property and to transfer it to a guardian.</i>. But if the underaged found something additional and protested<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The underaged claim that the adults understated the value of the estate and that their portion should be greater.</i>? Rebbi Abbahu said, they<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The adults have to swear in court even though in general a claimant cannot make a defendant swear unless he produce proof by at least one witness.</i> have to swear for them. Rebbi Mana said, everywhere the burden of proof is on the claimant except in this case. The Great Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer Ha‘iṭṭur</i> I 42b reads: R. Ḥiyya bar Abba. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 53:2" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.53.2"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 53(2</a>), R. Abba. Our text is confirmed by Ravad <i>Temim De‘im</i> §58.</i> says, without contract brothers are co-owners up to three generations<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Without any party objecting, an estate may stay undivided for up to three generations. After that it would need a contract of association.</i>. Rebbi [Yose]<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in the mss. but understood if any “bar Abun” is mentioned last in any paragraph.</i> bar Abun said, also Rav Hamnuna did instruct in this sense.
Rebbi Immi said, if a son was seen separated during his father’s lifetime, what he accumulated, he accumulated for himself<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of the Mishnah only apply to sons living in their father’s household, not those who are known to earn their money independently.</i>. As the following: A person learned to be a barber<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to read סָפַר “scribe”.</i>. His brother wanted to share with him. The case came before Rebbi Immi who said, do we say that if a person found something, his brothers share with him<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Even if a person still lives with his extended family, money made through his personal skill is not common property.</i>? A person became an agent. His brother wanted to share with him. The case came before Rebbi Immi who said, do we say that if a person went into robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λῃστεία.</i>, his brothers share with him? A brother of Rebbi Horiana, brother of Rebbi Samuel ben Sosartai, wanted to share with him. He told him, brother Alexander, do you not know that our father left us thousands<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">He may claim only his share in the inheritance but nothing else.</i>?
“Similarly, a woman,” etc. Rebbi La said, “if she said” in court.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one of co-owning brothers was requisitioned<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">He was ordered by the government to fulfill certain duties at his own expense.</i>, he was requisitioned from the estate<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">If he was living off an undistributed estate, the estate had to bear the cost of government service.</i>. If he fell sick and was healed, the medical costs are his own. If some of the brothers together sent a wedding gift<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Of money or valuables.</i> during their father’s lifetime, if a corresponding gift was returned<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">By the recipient on his wedding day.</i>, it returned to them in common since wedding gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Of money or valuables.</i> can be sued for in court. But if somebody’s friend sent him pitchers of wine or oil<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Gifts of food are genuine gifts, not subject to a law of reciprocity.</i> they cannot be sued for since they represent deeds of charity.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one of co-owning brothers,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 10:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).10.5">Tosephta 10:5</a>.</i>: If one of co-owning brothers was requisitioned for government service. If this was because of the sharecroppper<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This makes no sense. The correct reading is given in <i>Šiṭṭah mequbbeṣet ad</i> 144b in the name of Ravad and Rashba <i>Novellae ad</i> 144b: brothers. Since only rich and well-known people were ordered to pay for the city services, if the estate was renowned for its riches, the estate had to pay for the cost of services. If it was because of the personal renown of the person appointed, he had to bear the cost.</i>, he takes because of the sharecropper<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This makes no sense. The correct reading is given in <i>Šiṭṭah mequbbeṣet ad</i> 144b in the name of Ravad and Rashba <i>Novellae ad</i> 144b: brothers. Since only rich and well-known people were ordered to pay for the city services, if the estate was renowned for its riches, the estate had to pay for the cost of services. If it was because of the personal renown of the person appointed, he had to bear the cost.</i>. If because of the head of household, he takes as head of household. As the following: Rav Naḥman bar Samuel bar Naḥman was appointed to the city council<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βουλή, responsible not only for city services but also for the collection of taxes.</i>. The case came before Rebbi Immi. He said, if Naḥman who was caught has enough property, it should be given from his property; otherwise it should be given to him from the common estate.
If she fell sick and was healed, the medical costs are on the estate<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this is a <i>baraita</i> which disagrees with the Mishnah.</i>. It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Ketubot (Lieberman) 4:5" href="/Tosefta_Ketubot_(Lieberman).4.5">Tosephta <i>Ketubot</i> 4:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 52b" href="/Ketubot.52b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 52b</a>. A shortened version of the paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.10.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:11, Notes 231–236</a>. It is commented on by Ran (<i>Ketubot</i> 4, <i>ad</i> Alfasi #249), and Rosh (<i>Ketubot</i> 4, #23).</i>“Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, for any hurt which has a fixed medical fee, she is healed from her <i>ketubah</i>; if it does not have a fixed medical fee, she is healed from the estate.” As the following: The eye of a female relative of Rebbi Simeon bar Abba hurt. She came to Rebbi Joḥanan. He asked her: Did your doctor mention a fixed fee? If [the fee] is fixed, from your <i>ketubah</i>, if it is not fixed, your husband will give it to you<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Ran proves that Maimonides read this to mean: “From your husband’s estate it will be given to you.” He holds that the placement of the extended story in <i>Bava batra</i> indicates that it refers to an estate, not a living husband who bears unlimited responsibility for his wife’s health.</i>. But did we not state: “Do not turn yourself into a pleader<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Abot</i> 1:8. It is unethical for a judge to dispense legal advice.</i>”, and did not Rebbi Ḥaggai say in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, it is forbidden to disclose a judgment to a party? They said, Rebbi Joḥanan knew her to be an honest woman; therefore, he disclosed it to her. If her husband wants a treatment at a fixed rate, but she wants one without fixed rate, does one not listen to her husband? Rebbi Mattaniah said, only if the case not be before him. But if the case be before him, he has to tell it as it is<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">If the case is before him, he can disclose the judgment. He is only prohibited to talk to one party separately before and during the trial.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sent bridal gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Gifts to his preliminarily married wife.</i> to his father-in-law’s house, if he sent there for 100 minas and ate there a marriage meal for at least a denar’s worth, they cannot be collected<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If for some reason there will be a divorce before the definitive marriage, he cannot sue in court to get the gifts back.</i>. If he did not eat there a marriage meal, they can be collected. If he sent large bridal gifts which she is expected to bring with her to her husband’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Clothing and jewellery.</i>, they can be collected<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he ate at his in-law’s house. The first part of the Mishnah deals with average gifts which cannot be characterized as either large or small.</i>. Small bridal gifts which she is supposed to use in her father’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Perfume and cosmetics.</i> cannot be collected.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody sent bridal gifts to his father-in-law’s house,” etc. A person sent to his preliminarily wedded wife large bridal gifts. His relatives told him not to taste anything there. He went, did not listen to them, and ate. The house collapsed; they kept everything.
A person sent to his preliminarily wedded wife 24 gourds filled with various kinds of agricultural produce<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries and editors read מיני חדת “new kinds”. It is difficult to emend, only to end up with an uncommon expression. It seems preferable to read the word as חרת̇ حَرْث “cultivated field”.</i> between Passover and Pentecost. It was only a problem for the rabbis: from where did he bring flax and olive seeds<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Which are not found early in spring.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This is a slight reformulation of a paragraph in <i>Peah</i> 7:4, Notes 78, 81. The paragraph also is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 6:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.6.4.2"><i>Ševi‘it</i>6:4, Note 133</a>.</i> Rebbi Perira brought before Rebbi Jehudah the Prince two radishes between New Year’s Day and the Fast. It was the year after a Sabbatical, and they were a camel’s load. He said to him, rabbi, are they not forbidden? Are they no aftergrowth? He said to him, they were sown after the end of the Sabbatical year. At that moment did Rebbi permit to buy vegetables immediately after the end of the Sabbatical year.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a bedridden person distributed all his properties to others but reserved any real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Since he reserves property for himself, it is a sign that he hopes to recover. The gift document written on his orders is not a death-bed will. But if in the document itself it is described as a last will and testament, it is revocable at all times.</i> his gift is permanent; if he did not reserve any real estate his gift is not permanent<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">A death-bed will (which may be written as a gift document and does not have to follow the formal rules of a will) is revocable if the patient recovers.</i>. If [in the document] it was not written “bedridden”; he says, I was bedridden, but they are saying, he was healthy, he must prove that he was bedridden, the words of Rebbi Meїr<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the actual situation before the court may serve as <i>prima facie</i> evidence that so was the prior state. If the bequeather is healthy now, proof is required that he was not healthy when the gift document was written.<br>An alternative interpretation would be that he holds that a duly executed and witnessed document is always valid unless proven invalid. The technical term for this position is: “the holder of a document is advantaged.”</i>. But the Sages say, the burden of proof is on the claimants<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the gift described in the document was not executed, the recipient is a claimant and the document is subject to the giver’s interpretation unless the claimant can prove otherwise. The technical term for this position is: “the holder of a document is disadvantaged.”</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a bedridden person distributed all his properties to others,” etc. Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: If he reserved any movables, he did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 150a" href="/Bava_Batra.150a">Babli disagrees, 150a</a>, if the amount reserved is more than minimal.</i>. But if he reserved money and bought real estate it is as if he reserved real estate. And what you say that if he did not reserve any real estate his gift is not a gift, if he did (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly, the cases where the patient recovers and has occasion to revoke his will, or when he dies, are switched in the text.</i> recuperate. But if he did [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly, the cases where the patient recovers and has occasion to revoke his will, or when he dies, are switched in the text.</i> recuperate, that is the case about which Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai, they treated words of a bedridden person like those of a healthy person who wrote and delivered<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 11:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.11.1.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 11:1 Note 23</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:5:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.5.8"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 1:6, Note 201</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 13a" href="/Gittin.13a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 13a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 15a" href="/Gittin.15a">15a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 151a" href="/Bava_Batra.151a"><i>Bava batra</i> 151a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 175a" href="/Bava_Batra.175a">175a</a>.</i>; but only if he died from that sickness. If a critically ill person distributed his property<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">By oral instruction.</i>, whether on a weekday or on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">When acquisition is forbidden.</i>, what he did is valid. But if he was healthy [it is valid] only if he wrote “by money, or contract, or possession.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a>.</i>”
Rebbi Joḥanan said: Somebody became possibly impure<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 6:6" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.6.6">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i> 6:6</a> declares that a doubt which arises about impurity in a private domain is treated as if the case were about certain impurity whereas in a public domain the same case is treated as one of purity. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:7</a> then notes that an agricultural area is treated like a private domain during the rainy season (the entire time between ploughing at the time of the first rains through harvest in early summer) but as a public domain after the harvest is completed and the fields are cleared.</i> in an agricultural area whether in summer or in winter; this refersn to the dispute between Rebbi Meїr and the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Meїr holds that individual cases have to be decided according to the situation actually before the court (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.6.4">Note 79</a>), for him the problem is not the state of the area when the question arose but when it is asked of the rabbinic authority.</i>. If he comes to ask in summer, one answers him according to the rules of summer; in the winter, one answers him according to the rules of winter. Rebbi Joḥanan said, only days close to the rainy season<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Even R. Meїr will not decide according to the situation when the question was asked if there was a long interval between the creation of the problem and its resolution.</i>.
Rebbi Yannai in the name of Rebbi: The holder of documents is disadvantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">80,</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the gift described in the document was not executed, the recipient is a claimant and the document is subject to the giver’s interpretation unless the claimant can prove otherwise. The technical term for this position is: “the holder of a document is disadvantaged.”</i><sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 154b" href="/Bava_Batra.154b">Babli, 154b</a>, the version is: The holder of a document of indebtedness or gift has to get judicial confirmation of its validity.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said to him, is that not the Mishnah: “If [in the document] it was not written ‘bedridden’ but he says, I was bedridden,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The position of the anonymous majority in the Mishnah.</i> Rebbi Yannai praised him “those who pour out gold from the wallet<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 46:6" href="/Isaiah.46.6"><i>Is.</i> 46:6</a>.</i>,” “my son, they should not be removed from your eyes,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:21" href="/Proverbs.3.21"><i>Prov.</i> 3:21</a>.</i>” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Similar texts are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.1.6"><i>Kilaim</i> 8:1 Note 17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.21"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1 Notes 96–103</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.5"><i>Soṭah</i> 2:6 Note 177</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.5.6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:5 Note 166</a>.</i> A young man was selling his properties<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Real estate. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:8:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.8.1-2">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:8</a> permits underage children to buy and sell movables as soon as they are able to handle money. The inference is that for real estate transactions one has to be an adult. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 156a" href="/Bava_Batra.156a">Babli, 156a</a>, restricts the right to sell inherited real estate to people who have reached the age of 20. It is clear from the case discussed here that this is a purely Babylonian restriction.</i>. The case<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The family objected to his selling inherited real estate. They disputed the validity of the deed on the grounds that the seller was underage.</i> came before Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph and Rebbi Joḥanan. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph said, the presumption is that they<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The witnesses.</i> signed for a mentally capable person<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees that the seller of real estate not only has to be an adult (13+ years for a male, 12+ years for a female) but also must understand the consequences of what he is doing.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, since he undertook to uproot the properties from the family, he has to bring proof<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">That he was an adult understanding what he was doing.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the holder of documents is advantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the actual situation before the court may serve as <i>prima facie</i> evidence that so was the prior state. If the bequeather is healthy now, proof is required that he was not healthy when the gift document was written.<br>An alternative interpretation would be that he holds that a duly executed and witnessed document is always valid unless proven invalid. The technical term for this position is: “the holder of a document is advantaged.”</i>. Rebbi Yasa asked before Rebbi Joḥanan, how about Rebbi, since Rebbi said, the holder of documents is disadvantaged? He told him, everybody agrees that the holder of documents is advantaged. But how could you say that a case came before Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph who said, the presumption is that they signed for a mentally capable person. And you said, since he undertook to uproot the properties from the family to another family, he has to bring proof! He told him, I never said this<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">He now holds that the person attacking the validity of a duly witnessed document, not the defendant, must prove his case. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 18b" href="/Ketubot.18b">Babli (<i>Ketubot</i> 18b</a>) this opinion is credited to R. Simeon ben Laqish, confirmed in his name by the Yerushalmi <i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:5 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 9:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.9.6.4">Note 96</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.2.6"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:2 Note 46</a>, in R. La’s name <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.3.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:3 Note 61</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said before Rebbi Yasa, even if Rebbi Joḥanan wants to deny it, did not Rebbi Yannai say in the name of Rebbi: The holder of documents is disadvantaged. Rebbi Joḥanan said to him, is that not the Mishnah? This is the opinion of the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 154b" href="/Bava_Batra.154b">Babli, 154b</a>, the version is: The holder of a document of indebtedness or gift has to get judicial confirmation of its validity.</i> who follow Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">But R. Joḥanan himself decides practice to follow R. Meїr.</i>.
Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Meir<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav and R. Joḥanan decide in the same sense, this is the established practice in the Yerushalmi. This opinion is not mentioned in the Babli.</i>. Samuel said, the Mishnah is inverted<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion ascribed to R. Meїr in this Mishnah is rejected by him in another.</i>. In what is the Mishnah inverted? The senior colleagues said, “witnesses.”<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 2:3</a>, an anonymous Mishnah which therefore is held to represent R. Meїr’s opinion, witnesses may agree that they signed a deed but that nevertheless the deed is invalid since they acted under duress. Therefore, R. Meїr in the Mishnah here should not object to an investigation of the witnesses on a document of questionable validity.</i> The junior colleagues said, no “witnesses”: did they only disagree about witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">There is no discrepancy between the two Mishnaiot since the question here is about the claim of the donor, not about the witnesses.</i>? Rebbi Aḥa said, since I told you to write “bedridden” and you did not write it, you are liers<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the disagreement is about witnesses, it has nothing to do with the Mishnah in <i>Ketubot.</i> Since people who write down oral dispositions of a sick person are required to state in the document that it is a death-bed will, the donor claims that the document is invalid because it did not follow his instructions and, therefore, the genuine signatures on the document convict the signatories of lying.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody distributes his property orally, Rebbi Eliezer says that whether he is healthy or critical, guaranteed properties can be acquired by money, or contract, or possession. Not guaranteed properties can only be acquired by drawing close<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that oral dispositions must in every case fully comply with the rules laid down in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a>.</i>.
